O, son, since I have completed for you this book, which is the culmination of the science of medicine, and through its completion, its end, and through its expositions and declarations, it seems to me that I should satisfy you with this treatise, which is a part of hand-operation, that is, surgery.
Because hand-operation is almost entirely lost in our region and in our time, so that its science is greatly diminished, and its practice is obstructed; and nothing remains of it but some small descriptions in the books of the Ancients, which are mostly altered and have fallen into errors and uncertainties, so that their intentions are obscure and their power and art are weakened.
And it seems to me that I should revive it with the ordering of this treatise, according to the way of exposition, declaration, and abbreviation; and that it should come with the forms of the cautery instruments and the other instruments of the work, as it should be, by addition of the description, and by its preparation, which is a necessary cause.
But the reason why a good artisan with his hands is not found in our time is that the art of medicine is long, and that its practitioner must first be exercised in the science of anatomy, which Galen mastered, so that he knows the joints of the limbs and their forms, and their connection and separation; and he should have knowledge of the bones, nerves, and muscles, and their number, and their origin; and of the pulsating and quiet veins, and the places of their origin.
And for this reason Hippocrates says that with one name much is said, but little in the work, and especially in the art of the hand.
But we have already spoken of this in the introduction to this book; for whoever is not learned in what we have said about anatomy, is not exempt from falling into error, by which men will be killed, as I have seen many of them, for some are trained in this science and practice it without knowledge and without experience.
For I saw a foolish and inexperienced physician incise a scrofulous abscess in the neck of a woman, and he incised some arteries of the neck, so that a great quantity of blood flowed until the woman died in his hands.
And I saw another physician who undertook to remove a stone from a man who was in his old age, and the stone was large, and he came to him and extracted that stone with a piece of the bladder, so that that man died on the third day.
And I was called to extract it, and I saw that because of the size of the stone and the condition of the patient, I knew that he would die.
And I saw another physician who received a stipend from a duke of our land for surgery, and it happened to a black eunuch who had a fractured thigh; and the physician, through his ignorance, extracted the fracture, over the wound, with cushions and splints with strong constriction, and did not allow the wound to breathe (i.e., did not allow for proper ventilation or drainage).
Then he unbound it according to his desires, and then he bound it again, and then he left it for several days and ordered that we not unbind the bandage; and it remained thus until the thigh and foot were abscessed, and it came to ruin (i.e., resulted in a bad outcome).
And I was called to him, and I prepared to unbind the bandage, and tranquility was achieved and thus a lessening of his pains.
But however, corruption was already confirmed in the limb, so that I could not restrain it, and the corruption did not cease to spread in the limb until the patient perished.
And I saw another physician who perforated a cancerous abscess, and it was ulcerated for several days, until the malignancy of it was magnified with its lord (i.e., the disease worsened significantly).
The reason is that because the burning cancer is pure melancholic humor, then it is not fitting that it be approached with iron at all, but rather it should be in a limb which suffers such that it should be completely avoided (or left alone).
And for this reason, sons, it is necessary for you that the operation with the hand be divided into two parts: that is, in an operation which is associated with health, and in a work with which there is danger in the greatest dispositions (i.e.,  in the most serious cases).
And I have already explained this in every part of this book, in which operations come which are full of error and fear; therefore it is necessary for you that you guard against this and leave that (i.e., avoid dangerous operations), so that fools do not find a way to speak and dishonor you.
Therefore, take up your instruments with diligence and with the protection of God, and treat your patients with happiness and firmness.
And use the best method, by the grace of God, leading to health and praiseworthy success, and leave the terrible illnesses of difficult healing, and avoid those things that make you fear, so that hesitation in your faith and in your name may not escape you: for it is more beneficial to your glory, and higher in the world and behind your sanctities (i.e.,  it enhances your reputation and adds to your holiness).
Galen already said in some of his admonitions: Do not treat a bad disease, lest you be called a bad physician.
And I have divided this book into three chapters.
Begins the division of the book, and first of the first part. The first chapter deals with the head down to the feet,  the forms of instruments, and the tools of cautery, and what is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, of incision and perforation, and of cupping glasses, and of wounds, and of the extraction of arrows and similar things, the whole chapter organized by the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, of reduction and dislocation, and of the treatment of sprains and similar things; a chapter ordered from head to foot, and of the forms of the instruments.
It is necessary that I say the quality of the benefits of that cautery and its drawbacks, and in what constitution cautery is prohibited.
Therefore I say that the discourse on the quality of the benefit of cautery and its drawbacks is a long and prolix discourse, and subtle in science, and a hidden secret; and many wise men have already spoken of it, and they have disagreed about it, and a little of their discourses suffices for me, fearing prolixity.
And therefore I say that cautery benefits all complexions universally, whether with matter or without matter, except two complexions, which are hot complexions without matter.
In the hot and dry complexion with matter there is disagreement; for some say that cautery is beneficial to it, and others say the contrary, that cautery is not suitable for a disease that is due to heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness, and it is inconvenient that a hot and dry disease should be healed with that which is hot and dry.
And I oppose those who say the contrary, that benefit is done by cautery in every hot and dry disease that comes to the bodies of men, for when you compare the body of man and its humors to the complexion of fire, you find the body of man cold.
And I say, concerning this discourse, that experience has revealed this to me several times.
However, it is not fitting that one should undertake this who is not already experienced and exercised in the chapter on cautery by previous practice, and has known the diversity of the complexions of men and the disposition of diseases in themselves, and the causes of them, and their accidents, and the duration of them.
But in other complexions, there is no fear for you, and especially in cold and moist diseases, for all physicians are agreed on these, and are not in disagreement about the benefit of cautery in them.
And know, sons, that the secret of healing by cautery with fire and its goodness, over cautery with burning medicine, is because fire is simple, its action does not reach but to the member which is cauterized, and does not harm another member contiguous to it, but with little harm.
But the action of burning medicine reaches to those members which are distant from it, and quickly brings to the afflicted member a disease of difficult healing and quickly kills.
But fire, by its sublimity and the goodness of its substance, does not do this except to purge.  And it is known to us by experiment and by long service of study in the art concerning the truth of the causes; and therefore I am excused from the length of discourse. And if it were not that it is not fitting to explain in my book this secret entrusted to you concerning fire, and the quality of its operation in bodies and its expulsion by which it expels diseases, with a philosophical demonstrative discourse, which would be tedious to your minds.
And know, sons, that they disagree about the time in which it is convenient to perform cautery, and they posit springtime as the best time.
And I say that cautery is convenient at all times, because the harm from accidental timing is submerged in the benefit of what is obtained with the cautery, and especially if the cautery is for pains, or necessary, strong and urgent pains, which do not allow delay, because it is feared that evils may arise which are greater than the slight harm caused by the timing.
And this does not occur to your minds, or, sons, this is only considered by the common people and foolish physicians, namely that for that illness which is healed by cautery, there is no remission at all times, and they posit this consequence.
And the cause is not at all as they think for this reason, because cautery is nothing other than a medicine which alters the complexion and dries the humors which are the cause of the pains.
However, cautery is superfluous over medicine because of the speed of its efficacy and virtue of its operation and the greatness of its power.
And it is possible that his illness returns at some time, according to the complexion of the patient and the location of his illness, and his constitution, and what is prepared in his body from the aggregation of superfluities in him, and what has come to him in his acquisition of foods and similar causes; unless the illness in which cautery is administered is a subtle illness in a member with few superfluities and humors, such as cautery of a tooth for pain and similar things, it is possible that that pain will not return to him, but this happens in small things.
And moreover, the common saying is that cautery is the last resort of medicine, and this is a true saying, not in the sense that they understand it, for they believe that it is not a cure that benefits with medicine or with anything else besides cautery.
And the reason is contrary to that; and the intention that cautery is the last resort of medicine is only when we administer methods of cure in certain illnesses, and those medicines do not work, and then at the end of the process, one administers cautery, and it is beneficial in that case. And therefore, from this it is clear that cautery is the last resort of medicine, not according to the understanding of the common people and the many foolish physicians.
And the ancients said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they did not say this except for the evenness of gold and the nobility of its substance.
And they said that the location of cautery does not matter, although that is not entirely true, for I have already tested this and have found that it only works this way in some bodies and not others, and cautery with gold is good and better than with iron as they said; however, when you perform cautery with fire with gold, it is not clear to you, because it is melted according to the quantity you desire due to the softness of gold, and because it prepares to receive cold; and if you apply too much ignition, the gold melts from the fire, and some plates fall into the fire so that the artisan is troubled by that.
And therefore, cautery with iron is done more easily and more appropriately to the correctness of the operation.
And I have already ordered this chapter on cautery according to chapters, and I have ordered those from the head to the feet, so that I might provide the enquirer with what he seeks from it.
The first chapter.
This cauterization counteracts the humidity and coldness on the brain, the accident of which has two causes: that is, a cause of cold and a multitude of rheumatic flux, called coryza, flowing from the head to the parts of the eyes and ears, and much sleep, and toothache and throat ache, and universally of every illness that comes from coldness, such as paralysis, sopor and apoplexy, and similar illnesses.
The form of this cauterization is this: first, order the patient to be evacuated with laxative medicine, cleansing the head, for three or four nights, according to the strength of the patient, his age, and his constitution.
And then order him to shave his head with a razor, and then make him sit between your hands on a low bench and make him place his hands on his feet.
And then place the root of your palm near the pulse on the root of the nose between his eyes. However, when your middle finger has reached that spot, mark that spot with ink, and then ignite the olive-shaped cautery, of which this is the form:
And then place it on the spot marked with ink, in such a way that it descends, with which your hand is gradually tightened, and you turn it.
And then lift your hand slightly and observe the area, and if you see some quantity of the head of the bone uncovered, like the amount of a grain of barley, then lift your hand, and if not, return your hand with that iron, with another of those, if it is cooled, until that which I have told you is seen of the bone.
And then take a little salt and dissolve it on the area and leave it for three days.
And then place on it cotton soaked in wine, and leave it there until the burn scar is destroyed. And then treat the area with tetrafarmicon ointment until it is healed.
And it has been said that when the wound remains open, it is better to leave the arm thus, because it is better and more beneficial.
And some of them say that the bone marrow should be cauterized to the bone, and the cautery should be held there until some part of the bone's substance is hardened.
And then scrape away what is burnt from the bone, and then let it be cured.
And others say that with the cautery, penetrate to the rear, until it firmly enters the bone, pressing until the bone breaks, and this is the form of a small alphecat: And they believe that vapors from the head expire from that place.
And the wound should be kept open for a long time, and then it should be cured until it is consolidated.
But I have not seen these two types of cautery at all, except in some men, as a way of frightening them; and leave that, it seems to me, better is health when it is; because the head is weakened by the solution of its continuity, as we have testified in the other members, and especially when the patient's head is of a cold nature.
And the first type of cautery is safer and better, in my opinion; and with that I use and with that I work towards health.
Chapter two.
Concerning the cautery of the head.
When chronic and prolonged pain occurs in the entire head, and this is prolonged in the patient, and he uses leeches and cupping and purgatives and plasters and potions and poultices and ointments and similar things, and especially if he has already been cauterized with a cautery which we mention and none of those things benefit him, then consider whether the patient's head is of strong constitution by nature; and if it is not cold and he finds much great cold, then cauterize him with another cautery a little below that one. And then cauterize that above the horn of his head with a cautery until the scab is separated. Incise the scab and let the bone be uncovered to the extent that we have recounted, and cauterize that with a cautery in the rear part of the head in the place which is called the vas — and this is the place where a cupping glass is placed. And lighten your hand in this, and do not uncover the bone, for the patient through this finds much great pain different from the pain of all the other cauterizations of the head.
And I would say this cautery in its place, for it is necessary that the cautery with which the horns of the head and the occiput are cauterized is a more subtle cautery than that with which the middle place of the head is cauterized; and this is its form.
Chapter three.
On the cauterization of recent migraine.
When a sudden pain occurs in the part of the head, or a widespread pain within the eye, let the head be evacuated with purifying medicines, and let the other treatment be administered, which I have described in the chapters on diseases; and if all this does not help, then cauterization in this case is done in two ways: either cauterization with a sharp burning medicine, or with iron.
But cauterization with a burning medicine is that you take a clove of garlic, and peel it and cut off the ends from both sides. Then incise the location of the pain in the temple with a lancet until a place is made under the skin, until it is hidden.
Then bind it tightly with a compress to cause constriction, and leave it for the space of three hours; and then unbind it and remove the garlic, and leave the wound for two or three days, and then apply a cotton ball soaked in vinegar until the area becomes sore.
And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, do this with some burning medicines which I have written in the twelfth treatise on burning medicines.
But cauterization with iron in this manner:  heat the cautery, of which this is the form:
and it is called a nail or peg, because its head is like the shape of a nail, in which there is some curvature, and in the middle of it is a small prominence.
And then place it on the location of the pain, and hold your hand — so that the cautery, which is below, is not burned — and turn the iron little by little with speed.
And if the amount that is burned by the heat of the cautery is about half of it, then lift your hand so that the artery below is not burned, so that no blood flow occurs.
Then soak cotton in salt water and place that on the area, and leave it for three days; then apply cotton soaked in wine, and then treat with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, cauterize with the tip of the knife as with a cautery, and be careful not to cut the artery in this migraine, specifically not the old kind.
Chapter four.
Concerning the cauterization of chronic migraine.
When you treat a migraine with what we have said in the preceding treatment, and what we have said in the divisions of diseases, and the treatment is not effective, and sometimes because of the illness, what we have said about the first cauterization with medicine or cauterization with fire is insufficient, then you should heat the knife cautery until it is white hot,  then mark the location of the pain with a line whose length is half a finger or thereabouts; and place your hand for a moment and hold it while you incise the area and penetrate to the bone.
However, it is necessary for you to be careful of the continuation of the jaw, which is moved during mastication, and that the muscle not be injured and the nerve that serves it, and that spasm does not occur.
And if it is about the protection and caution against blood flow from the artery that is incised, because in its incision there is danger, and especially for one who ignores what he does and is not studious and experienced; and leaves the operation at the beginning.
And I will come to the recollection of the regime of accidental blood flow from the incision, according to its manner, in its place in this book.
And if you see that this illness is not relieved by this cauterization and the patient's body is tolerable, cauterize it in the middle of the head as we have recounted, and treat the wound until it is healed.
And if you wish, administer the cautery which we have mentioned in the chapter on the constriction of an artery with a cautery having two blades, and this cauterization is better and more suitable.
Chapter 5.
Of the cauterization of earaches.
When pain in the ear comes from cold and is treated with laxative remedies and with the other treatment which I have described in the division, and the pain is not relieved, then apply the cautery, and this is what is called a puncture, of which this is the form:
And then puncture, then apply the cautery, and let it be moved around the entire ear as a wheel is turned, or in the circumference of each part if the pain is in a specific part. Extend the cautery from the root of the ear little by little, after you have marked the place with a probe.
And if the cautery requires ten punctures in the whole ear, or whichever one it is, then treat the places until they are healed.
Chapter 6.
On the cauterization of a cough.
A cough which is cured with cauterization is only of those types which come from phlegm, as we have said in the divisions of illnesses.
Avoid cauterization of the type that comes from dryness or from a spasm of the nerve.
When you treat that type of cough with leeches and purgatives and gargles, and your treatment is not effective, then you should cauterize the patient with three cauteries:  the first at the root of the ear, the second a little below the temple, and the third at the junction of the two lips.
And place your cautery on the opposite side from the affected part, because softening does not occur except in the part that appears healthy.
And the method of cauterization is that you cauterize it with the cautery at the very tip of the upper ear, a little above the temple, and at the temple another, the length of which should be according to the length of the little finger, and you hold your hand with the cautery until the amount burned is half the thickness of a fingernail.
And this is the form of the cautery, and it is a type of knife, the form of which is shown before. (This implies an accompanying illustration). However, it is more subtle to do it a little like this; and it is necessary to cut away some of the superfluous thickness.
And then treat the area with what has been previously described until it is healed.
Chapter 7.
Concerning the cauterization of chronic apoplexy.
When apoplexy is chronic and you treat it with what we have said, and your treatment is ineffective and the patient has no fever, then cauterize them with four cauteries:  on each of the corners of their head; and with a cautery in the middle of the head, as we have said; and with a cautery on the nape of the neck. And the manner of cauterization is done as described above.
And it is already cauterized above the mouth of the stomach; and it is further behind; and then let it be treated with what follows.
Chapter 8.
Concerning the cauterization of forgetfulness (amnesia) done by flame.
So that large potions and purifying pills for the brain are given to the patient at the beginning.
And then shave the entire head of the patient and place on his nape of the neck a plaster of the kind described in the treatise on plasters.
And apply that several times, for this is a manner of cauterization. And do this according to the same order which I have already said.
And therefore if he is healed by those means, it is good; and if not, then make for him three cauterizations in the back part of his head, descending from the highest part of the head to the lowest part of the neck. And place between each cauterization the width of a finger, and then treat the cauterization with what has been previously described.
But if you see increased swelling and the patient tolerates it, then make a cauterization in the middle of the area; and if you see even greater swelling, then cauterize it above the two horns (presumably referring to bony protrusions or similar).
Then treat that until it is healed; and let the cauterization be olive-shaped, according to the form previously given.
Chapter 9.
Concerning the cauterization of paralysis and the softening of the whole body.
So that you proceed in the purification of the head with leeches and with what we have said; and then shave the patient's head, and then cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head, and with a cautery above all the horns of the head, and with a cautery on the back part of it, and with three cauteries on the vertebrae of the neck.
And if it is necessary in the illness of softening of the body to find that and the patients are suffering and the illness is severe, then cauterize it with four cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and finally with a cautery until it is burned from the tailbone of it, and raise your hand. And then treat it until it is healed, and this should be an olive cautery.
Chapter 10.
Concerning the cauterization of epilepsy.
An epileptic is not cauterized unless that epilepsy is due to phlegm.
And therefore it is necessary that you purify his brain at the beginning with herbs and with the other treatment which we have described in the section when the patient is strong and is tolerant of the expulsion of medicines.
And if it is a child who does not tolerate medicines, then administer gargles and masticatories that purify the brain, before that, for many days, with improvement of the diet.
Then shave the head of the patient, and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head according to the previous method, and with another cautery on the occiput, and cauterize over each temple. And if the patient is strong and tolerant, then cauterize him with the cauteries which I have mentioned, having paralysis and softening of the body over the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back.
And let it be an olive-shaped cautery, as has been previously described.
But if the patient is a child, then apply a small, thin cautery according to this method.
Chapter 11.
On the cauterization of melancholy.
When the causes of melancholy are corrupt humors and thick phlegm, then cauterize that area with the cauteries which we described in the treatment of paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholy is a superfluity tending towards melancholy, and the patient's body is humid, then give him something to drink that cleanses his brain, according to what has been explained previously in the division [of treatments].
Then shave the patient's head, and then make a properly-made headband for him on the circumference [of the head], and then place that in the middle of the head.
And the patient should be seated upright, and held firmly on all sides.
Then apply a liniment; and it is best to use old sheep's fat. And heat that [liniment] over the fire with moderate warmth,  which the said [patient] can tolerate when it is applied to him. Then pour that [liniment] into the middle of his head within the headband, and leave it there until it cools.
And do this to the patient once a week, along with his other treatment, until he is healed; or, if you wish, cauterize that [area] making small punctures according to the need of the patient, but hold your hand with a cautery thus prepared: this is a type of cautery which moistens the brain evenly.
Then apply that cotton soaked in sheep's fat or goose grease.
Chapter 12.
Concerning the cauterization of descending water in the eye.
When the beginning of water in the eye is declared to you by the signs which I have mentioned in the division, then begin and give the patient to drink that which will purify his head, and guard him from all humidities.
And make him sweat in a bath for several days.
And then order him to shave his head, and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head; and then cauterize it with two cauteries on the two temples if the beginning of the descent of water is in both eyes, or on one side if it is the beginning of the water in one eye.
Incise with a cautery all the veins and arteries which are below the temples; and let the cauteries be far apart in the width of the temples.
And beware of the flow of blood when it is cauterized; and if you see a flow of blood from it, stop it with some medicine, which is possible for you.
And I come with wisdom which is in the extraction of arteries and with the excision of them and the caution of blood flow.
And sometimes it is cauterized in the neck below the two bones with two cauteries properly placed.
Chapter 13.
Concerning the cauterization of old tears (likely referring to chronic wounds or sores, possibly fistulas,  given the context).
When the tears from the eyes are old and persistent, and through the veins and arteries which are in the manifest place of the head outside, and it is verified that this is a superfluity of cold phlegmatic humors, then cauterize that with the cautery which we recounted in the beginning chapter of water falling into the eyes, and with a cautery in the middle of the head, and with two cauteries in the neck below the two temples, and with two cauteries in the neck below the two bones. And if it needs additional treatment, then cauterize that in all parts of the corner of the eye above the edge of the eyebrow with a small cautery.
Chapter 14.
Concerning the cauterization of nasal odor (or a foul smell from the nose).
When you treat this with what we have said in the division and your treatment is not effective, then you prepare and give the patient the decoctions for three nights; and then shave his head, and cauterize it with a medium-sized cautery, with an olive-shaped cautery. And then cauterize that with a clove-shaped cautery, with two cauteries above the two eyebrows, a little below the hair.
And beware of the artery, that you do not cut into it.
And this is the shape of the clove-shaped cautery.
Chapter 15.
Concerning the cauterization for softening of the eyelids.
When the eyelid is softened by illness or humidity, then cauterize that eyelid with a lunar cautery; and this is its form:
And if you cauterize, do so above the two eyebrows a little, with two cauteries on each side, and let them be elongated towards the temples. And let the length of each cautery be according to the size of the eyebrow.
And do not press too hard with your hand and the cautery, but only enough that a third part of the eyelid is cauterized.
Chapter 16.
Concerning the cauterization of the eyelid of the eye when the hairs are turned inward and prick the eye.
When the hairs of the eye are turned inward so that they prick the eye, cauterization in these cases is done in two ways, for cauterization is either done with fire or with a burning medicine.
If you cauterize with fire, instruct the patient beforehand to shave his hairs like those who cleanse them, until they are elongated and thinned; and if it pricks him near their roots, then you bind his eyes with a bandage so that they are not harmed until the hairs are grown.
Then, when they are grown and thinned, place the patient's head in your lap, and then mark on the eyelid of his eyes with a probe, with a mark which should be according to the shape of a myrtle leaf, and let the beginning of the mark be near the hairs.
And then apply lint soaked in egg white or in gum arabic to the eye.
And then heat the cautery, of which this is the form.
And then cauterize over the shape that you have marked, little by little, in many times, until the surface of the cornea which is like a myrtle leaf is burned, entirely and properly apparent.
And a sign of the success of your work is that you see the eyelid of the eye already contracted and its hairs raised above the puncture of the eye.
Then raise your hand and leave it like that for three days, and then apply lint with a bandage until the scar is healed. And then treat that with ointment until it is healed.
But if some matter returns through the hairs near their roots and the eyelid is softened, then apply the cautery again to that area in the same way as you did initially.
But if the hairs are below the eyelid, then cauterize that until it is raised to its natural place and is even, so that the hairs do not prick the eye.
But cauterize with a burning medicine, that is, instruct the patient to pull the hairs until they are extended and even, and then make a shape like a myrtle leaf from a piece of cloth.
Then take known soap and quicklime, about one ounce, or whatever that is, and mix those ingredients well, and do this quickly so that it does not get cold; then spread this on the cloth that is shaped like a myrtle leaf, and place that over one or both eyes; and place under the eye a cotton ball soaked in egg white, and the patient's head should be in your lap.
And place your finger on the medicine and press it gently, but do not remove it until the patient feels the bite of the medicine, for the bite is like fire.
And so, when you perceive that he feels the bite, then leave the medicine and remove it with your finger; when the biting sensation ceases, then remove the medicine and wash the eye with water; then observe if you see that the eyelid is now raised, as if it were burned by fire or by incision, for then it is well; and if not, return to applying the medicine to the place where the medicine has not yet taken effect and has not blackened, until your work is even and the eye is contracted.
Then place a cotton pad with ointment on it until the scab from the cauterization is removed, and then treat it with healing ointment or another until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you, during your operation, to watch with vigilant diligence that nothing of the medicine falls into the eye, and if the eyelid after a few days is softened and some hair grows into the eye, then repeat your medicine on that softened part of the eyelid, exactly as you did at the beginning, and then treat it again until it is healed.
And this is the method for myrtle leaf.
And know that the eyes of men are diversified according to their size and smallness, and therefore your contraction should be adjusted accordingly.
And the path of truth will not be hidden from him who studies this art.
Chapter 17.
Concerning the cauterization of a fistula in the tear duct of the eye.
When you treat a fistula with what we have said in the divisions of diseases and your treatment is not effective, then you must cauterize it in this manner: instruct the patient to place his head in your lap, and have a minister hold his head between your hands in such a way that his head is not moved or agitated.
Then place a cotton pledget soaked in egg white or gum arabic on his eye.
Then heat the cautery, of which this is the shape:
which should be concave, like the shape of a cane or a vulture's feather, at the end with which the cauterization is performed.
And if you wish, let it be penetrating to the other end. And if you wish, let it be shaped like a probe.
However, the concave one is better for your work.
Then press the fistula if it is open, and draw out the pus and dry it. And if it is not open, then open it and extract the rottenness from it.
Then place on it the cautery, which should be well ignited, and hold your hand a little below the cautery, from the eye to the side of the nose, so that your hand does not burn or the patient is disturbed, and then place the cautery on the eyelid and burn it.
And so if at the beginning your cautery penetrates to the bone, it is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery once or twice, if necessary; and leave it like that for three days, and then place on it a dressing with lint and around it with drying ointment until it is healed.
But if ten days have passed and it is not healed, then place on it a sharp corrosive medicine until the bone is uncovered and is scraped as is fitting in its chapter.
And there is another method of cauterizing a fistula which some ancients described:  Locate the fistula and open it, and then place in that fissure a thin tube, which is shaped like this:  And pour into it the quantity of one ounce of molten lead; and hold your hand with the tube with a good grip, and do not move the patient so that the molten lead does not reach the eye of the patient; and it is necessary that you place on the patient's eye a dressing soaked in egg white or water, because lead adheres to the fistula and heals it with marvelous healing.
And if the fistula is healed with what we have said of the cautery and the treatment, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary to administer a probe through the nose and reduce the fistula to the body of the nose as it will come to its proper place.
Chapter 18.
Of the cauterization of the fissure of the wells (or, possibly,  Of the cauterization of the cleft in the  [context-dependent;  'potz' needs further context to be certain. It could refer to a wound, a bone structure, or something else entirely]).
Many times fissures occur on the lips, and especially on the lips of children.
Therefore, if you treat those fissures with what we have said in the divisions and your treatment does not help, then ignite a small, knife-like cautery according to this form that is shown:
And let its concavity be according to the thinness of a knife.
Then carefully place that ignited cautery in those fissures until the cautery reaches the bottom of the fissures.
Then treat it with cerates until it is healed.
Chapter 19.
Concerning the cauterization of a fistula in the mouth.
When in the root of the uvula, or in the palate, or in the roots of the teeth, an abscess develops and then breaks, and then becomes chronic, and pus runs and a fistula is formed, and you treat it and the treatment is ineffective, then you should ignite a cautery according to the size of the fistula, and then place that ignited cautery in the opening of the fistula, and hold it with your hand until the heated iron reaches the bottom, and repeat this once or twice; and then treat it with what we have said about treatment until it is healed.
And so if the wound is closed and heals, it is good; but if not, then it is necessary to uncover the area and remove the corrupted bone, as will be described in its chapter.
Chapter 20.
Concerning the cauterization of teeth and the softening of gums.
When the gums are softened by humidity and the teeth are loose, and you treat them with medicines and it is ineffective, then place the patient's head in your lap, and then ignite the cautery whose shape will be described below, in such a way that you place the small cup over the tooth and quickly insert the ignited cautery into it, and hold your hand for a short time until the patient feels the heat of the fire has reached the root of the tooth.
And then remove your hand, and then repeat the cauterization a few times as needed.
And then the patient should fill their mouth with salt water and hold it for an hour, and then spit it out, because the loose tooth will be fixed, the softened gum will be tightened, and the corrupt humidity will be dried.
Chapter 21.
On the cauterization of toothache.
When toothache is due to coldness, or there is cold or worms in it, and you cannot cure it with medicine, then cauterize it in one of two ways: either cauterize with cow dung, or cauterize with fire.
Cauterization with dung is done by taking cow dung and boiling it in an iron spoon, then taking cotton and wrapping it around the end of a probe, then dipping it in the boiling dung, and quickly placing it on the aching tooth until it cools.
And then repeat this several times until the power of the heat reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, submerge wool or cotton in the aforementioned dung, and place that on the aching tooth, and place ignited iron on top of it until the iron reaches the bottom of the tooth's cavity.
However, cauterization with fire is done by using a cannula of brass or iron, and there should be some thickness in the core of it so that the sharpness of the fire does not reach the patient's mouth.
Then ignite the cautery, whatever its form may be, and place it on the tooth, holding your hand there until the cautery cools; and do this several times, for the pain will be relieved either that day or the next.
And it is necessary that, after the application of that cautery, the patient fill their mouth with good dung and hold it for an hour, and then spit it out; and this is the form of the cautery.
And cauterize with whichever extremity you wish, as is possible for you.
And this is the form of the cannula:
Chapter 22.
Concerning the cauterization of extrophuli (likely referring to a type of skin eruption or lesion).
When extrophuli are caused by phlegm and cold humors and are not responsive to digestion by medicines, and you wish to digest them lightly, then you ignite the cautery whose form is as follows: having two open ends so that smoke escapes from one end of the cautery, and place that ignited cautery on the abscess once, and again if it is needed, until it reaches the bottom of the abscess.
And if the abscess is small, then apply the cautery according to the size of the abscess, and then leave it for three days and apply cotton soaked in wine over it until what the fire has burned is softened.
And then treat it with ointment and bandages until it is healed.
Chapter 23.
On the cauterization of hoarseness and constriction of the throat.
When humidities come upon the windpipe, and especially when this is with a cold constitution, then the patient should be evacuated at the beginning with laxative medicines, and then cauterize that below the root of the throat in the affected place.
And be careful not to reach the throat with the cautery, nor burn his windpipe, only a little.
And cauterize that with another cautery near the juncture of the neck at his last vertebra. And his cautery should be a nail according to the preceding manner.
And then treat it with what was previously described until it is healed.
Chapter 24.
Concerning the cauterization of lung disease and cough.
When cough and lung disease are caused by cold humors, and the patient has no fever or consumption, and the illness is chronic, then cauterize with two cauteries on the clavicle in the previously mentioned hollow and prominent areas; and with another cauterization in the middle of the chest between the two nipples; and let the cautery be a nail according to the preceding form.
And if you wish, your cauterization may be done by puncturing with the cautery that is called a puncturing instrument, the form of which precedes in the puncturing for earache.
And let the points be close to those; and then treat it with the same things that were prescribed previously until it is healed.
And let the cautery have three prongs because cauterization is done with it: with one application you can make three cauterizations according to this form:
Chapter 25.
Of the cauterization of the testicle, which is the head of the helper (presumably referring to the penis).
When the head of the helper (penis) is dislocated due to lubricous humors and is not fixed at the time of its return from its dislocation until it is accustomed to returning, and then with a small movement it is dislocated again as we have testified, then it is necessary that you first reduce the dislocation, and then the patient should be turned on his back or on his healthy side; and then raise the testicle which is on the inner side to the upper parts with the fingers of your left hand, if the joint is dislocated on the inner side.
And then heat the cautery having two heads; and this is its form:
And then cauterize the testicle until it penetrates to the other side; and the figure of the cauterization will then be of four cauterized heads (presumably referring to a four-headed cautery mark).
And sometimes it is adjusted according to this count and therefore there are eight cauteries.
And then place over the cautery cooked barley with salt, and then keep the patient calm, and the limb should not be moved for some time until it is strengthened/healed.
And if the dislocation is in the upper parts and this is done late, then cauterize over the muscle with a nail-shaped cautery or with many pointed cauteries, because the joint is strengthened and the humidity is removed and the patient is healed.
Chapter 26.
Of the cauterization of the stomach.
When cold and many humors come into the stomach, exceeding its capacity and multiplying within it, and if you continue the methods of treatment and do not cure it, then it is necessary that the patient lies supine on their back, and extends their arms and legs. Then cauterize it with three cauteries: one cautery below the feet, to the width of one finger, with a nail-shaped cautery, and with two cauteries below on both sides of the first cautery, so that a triangular figure of cauteries is formed; and space them out so that they are not touching, which would cause decay/rot.
And if the depth of the cautery is two-thirds the thickness of the finger.
And let the figure of the cautery be according to this form without addition. And if you wish, cauterize with a large cautery in the middle of the stomach according to this form; and this should be a cautery according to the form of a circle according to this form: (The text implies diagrams were present in the original manuscript to illustrate the shapes of the cauteries).
And let the stomach be cauterized, paying attention to what is feared from this cautery: and it is that you mark on the stomach points according to the quantity you wish with a probe, and then cauterize those points with the cautery; and then treat it with the previously described treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 27.
Of the cauterization of the cold liver.
When pain arises in the liver due to coldness, or humidity, or excessive flatulence, so that it is driven from its normal fullness by superfluous egress, and the patient is not cured by what we have said and done, then the patient should be placed supine on his back, and three cauteries should be marked with a probe according to this form and this same quantity on the liver beneath the hypochondria, where the recumbent position of a man is apparent. (Again, this implies accompanying diagrams).
And let the space which is between each cautery be according to the thickness of a finger, and let the cautery be according to the length of a right-hand finger.
And do not press too much with the cautery.
And if the quantity of that which is burned is the amount of half the width of a finger, and no more, and if the patient is standing on his feet; and if he is not standing, then let him be lying down, having already extended his thighs, and let him raise his arms.
And it is possible for you to cauterize these areas with a knife-cautery, when you have subtlety and prudence in the art.
And be careful not to proceed too much in the cauterization, and let the width of a whole finger be burned so that the entire body is burned and it reaches the intestines, because the finger is thin here; and know this.
Chapter 28.
Of the perforation of an abscess of the liver with a cautery.
When you find a wound or abscess in the liver and you want to know if that abscess is in the flesh of the liver or in its tunic, then if it is in the flesh of the liver, the patient finds heaviness and pain without sharpness.
And if it is in the tunic of the liver, the pain is very sharp, and sometimes it even tires the doctors in its treatment.
Therefore, the patient should be turned onto their side; and then mark the location of the abscess with a probe; and then apply the cautery, which is shaped like a rake, the form of which was shown on the previous page. And cauterize with that cautery until the entire swelling is burned, until it reaches the pus, until blood comes out.
And then treat it with the treatment for other abscesses until it is healed.
And it is not fitting that this type of cauterization be used except by one whose study in the art of medicine is extensive, and in whose hands many illnesses have been known; however, someone with experience should sometimes dare this operation, but leave it to us, it is better.
Chapter 29.
On the cauterization of pleurisy.
The ancients said that a cautery made from the roots of birthwort for cold pleurisy, according to this manner, is effective.  And this is how you do it: take a long, dry root of birthwort, as long as a finger, and of the thickness of a finger.
Then submerge it in oil and hold it to the fire, and then cauterize with a cautery in the area that is between the continuation of the clavicle and the neck; and with two small cauteries below the chin, slightly inclined towards the area below the beard; and with two cauteries in the area that is inclined between the fifth and sixth ribs, inclining slightly towards the back; and with another cautery in the middle of the chest; and with another on the stomach; and with three cauteries on the back; and with one cautery in the area that is between the two shoulders; and with two cauteries on the two sides of the chest, below the cautery which is between the two shoulders.
And it is not necessary to cover your hand with the cautery; rather, let it be manifest in the work of cauterization.
And some ancients said that some men use a cautery of iron similar to a ray [of light], and they heat it, and apply it to the area between the ribs, until it reaches the abscess and healthy tissue is excised, as we have said in the case of an abscess of the liver.
But this perforation with a cautery is not without danger, for you may quickly kill the patient in the place, creating a fistula which is not easily healed.
Chapter 30.
Concerning the cauterization of the spleen.
When you treat the illness of the spleen with what we have said about treatment in the division and your treatment is not effective, then cauterization is appropriate in three ways, all of which are correct.  One is to make three or four cauterizations lengthwise according to the length of the spleen, following the shape of the liver cauterizations, the form of which has been previously described.
And let the width between each cauterization be the breadth of a finger or slightly more, and the manner of cauterization should be the same as that which we have described for the cauterization of the liver.
And do not press your hand with the cautery; and let the patient be lying on their side.
And another method of cauterization is to ignite the cautery having two points, which procedure we have described in the chapter on muscle dislocation, and raise the linen which is in the area of the spleen where the patient's left side lies.
And let your lifting of the linen be according to the length of the body, so that cauterizations are made according to the width of the body.
Then insert the two ignited points, very quickly, into that space so that with them you penetrate the linen on the other side.
And then withdraw the cautery; and thus four cauterizations are made.
And if you wish, cauterize with a cautery having three points.
And then you should care for the cauterization after it has stopped emitting pus for many days, for that is more important than the other preceding matters in the cure.
Chapter 31.
Concerning the cauterization of a dropsical person.
Cauterization is not suitable for all dropsies, but only for a specific kind.  (The "3" after "alcinte" is likely a scribal error or annotation.)
When you treat a dropsical patient with the methods of treatment that we have mentioned in the division and your treatment is ineffective, then it is necessary that you cauterize them with four cauteries in the circuit of the navel, and with one cauterization on the stomach, and with another cauterization on the liver, and with another cauterization on the spleen, and with two cauterizations between the vertebrae of the back, and one cauterization on the right foot, and with another cauterization on the stomach. And the depth of the cauterization should be proportionate to the thickness of the skin.
And then leave the cauterized wound open so that it discharges pus for a long time.
And do not deprive the patient of treatment after cauterization with what is necessary, so that two intentions are added to him, and he is hastened towards healing with help. (This suggests a holistic approach, combining cauterization with other therapies.)
The shape of the cautery with which the abdomen is cauterized should be nail-shaped, as its form is described above; and that with which the back is cauterized should be olive-shaped, as its form is described above.
Chapter 32.
On the cauterization of the feet and thighs.
When the feet are afflicted with dropsy and are filled with yellowish water, it is necessary to cauterize the feet on the dorsum (top) of the foot, not at the point between the little finger and ring finger, but elevate your hand with the cautery and do not twist it; then raise your hand, and do not completely retract it, for the yellow water will flow out.
The cautery should be of this shape: Then make two cauteries on both thighs, and on each thigh let the cautery be made with the knife-like end of the cautery. And let the cautery be according to the length of the thigh: one below the knee and the other below the middle of the thigh; and on each thigh, two cauteries.
And leave the wound open without treatment for a long time so that some water drains from it.
And then treat it with those things which we have mentioned.
Chapter 33.
On the cauterization of the abdomen.
When the abdomen is cold and damp to the point that it weakens the virtues, that is, the digestive and retentive virtues which are in the stomach and intestines, and this is treated with methods of cure and does not improve, and sometimes the patient suffers, exhibiting a deficiency of virtue, then cauterize that [abdomen] with a large cautery on the stomach as previously described in the cauterization of the stomach, with a circular cautery and with four subtle cauteries in the circuit of the umbilicus, with a thin awl-shaped cautery; and with a cautery on the hypochondrium above the spleen, or with two cauteries.
And if sometimes the humors are excessive and the patient tolerates it, then cauterize that [area] on the pubic bone and on the intestines, and quickly apply two cauteries close to the stomach and near the groins, for it is a perfect cure and helpful remedy, from which you will not err.
Chapter 34.
On the cauterization of old hemorrhoids.
When there are in the nature of old hemorrhoids, one of thick cold humors or of corrupted humors, and they are treated with what we have said in the division and the treatment does not improve, then cauterize with three cauteries on the lowest part of the sacrum, small and slightly triangular, and with a cautery above the umbilicus about two fingers' breadth.
And if you suspect that his stomach is cold and his food is not digested, and you see his face inflamed, then cauterize that [area] over the stomach with a large cautery as previously described; and with one cautery over the liver, and with another cautery over his spleen with a nail-shaped cautery, and leave the cautery open for some time, and then treat him until he is healed.
Chapter 35.
On the cauterization of swellings near their excision.
When you excise a swelling, then you should use a cautery that resembles a ray (or spoke, suggesting a shape). Then insert that ignited cautery into the excised swelling, and finally use your hand until the cautery reaches the opening of the vein from which the blood flows.
And do this once or twice, for if the swellings are numerous, then cauterize each of them as we have recounted, and afterwards treat them with ointments until they are healed.
And if you cauterize with a large cautery on someone, it is slower in healing.
Chapter 36.
Chapter 36. On the cauterization of fistulas which are formed in the lower parts of the body.
When nature does not obey the treatment which we have recounted in its place, and the patient is afflicted by this, let it be healed with cautery.
Therefore, when someone has a fistula in the lower body, and it is old, corrupted by pus and moisture, then what is most fitting is to probe it with a thin probe, and then ascertain the extent of its depth with the aforementioned probe.
Then heat the cautery which is shaped like a ray. And then insert that heated cautery into this fistula, according to the depth that the probe indicated.
And repeat the cautery application until those corrupted tissues are burned, once or twice or thrice, according to your need.
And beware of burning nerves and veins; or if the fistula goes towards the bladder or the intestine, then beware of all those areas; and do not do this unless the fistula is in fleshy tissue and you believe it is not penetrating deeply.
And then treat the area until it is healed.
And if the area is sealed, and the infected matter has been removed, and it remains so for a long time, then know that it is completely cured.
And if the infected matter has not been removed, then know that it is penetrating or the bone itself is corrupted, or this comes from those causes which will be mentioned in their place.
Chapter 37.
On the cauterization of sores.
When in sores pain arises from cold or great inflammation, and because of this the patient's body is weakened, then it is necessary to cauterize that area over the sores with a cautery; over each sore with a nail-shaped cautery, the preparation of which will be mentioned later.
And quickly cauterize again a little lower, so that three linear cauterizations result, and then they will be more helpful in healing.
Chapter 38.
On the cauterization of the bladder.
When weakness and softening occur in the bladder due to cold and humidity, such that the patient cannot retain urine, then cauterize that area in the lowest part above the bladder, where the hairs of the pubic region begin, and cauterize with one cautery on the right side and with another on the left.
And let the length of each cautery be according to the size of the hazelnut; and cauterize that with a cautery in the lowest part of the back, or with two cauteries if necessary.
And let the cautery be a nail-shaped cautery, as is appropriate.
Chapter 39.
On the cauterization of the breast.
When cold and humidity come to the breast, and because of this conception is prevented, and her menses are corrupted, and their provocation is delayed, and pain occurs before their arrival, then it is necessary that the place be cauterized with three cauteries in the circumference of the navel, as we have said in the cauterization of the bladder; and with a cautery on the place below the back, or with two cauteries, and let the cautery be nail-shaped.
Chapter 40.
On the cauterization of a dislocated hip.
When mucous humors are poured into the area of the hip, and are the cause of its displacement, and a sign of this is that one thigh is longer than the other when compared. And the location of the dislocation is found where it hurts.
Then it is necessary that you cauterize the patient on the affected part of their hip with a circular cautery, after you have marked with a probe the circumference of the affected area as it is swollen, so that the affected part falls in the middle of the circle.
And let the cautery be of the kind previously described in the cauterization of the stomach.
And if this cautery is not at hand, then cauterize it with three cauteries using a large olive-shaped cautery.
And let the cautery be made according to the quantity and thickness of the whole spine; and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 41.
On the cauterization of the sciatic vein.
When pain comes in the hip area, and it is caused by cold and dampness, the patient is treated with what we have said and do not disagree, and this is ancient practice; then you should purge the patient of thick humors with foul-smelling pills or artificial pills or similar; and then cauterize it.
And the manner of cauterization in that case is according to two methods: either cauterization with burning medicines, or cauterization with fire.
If according to two known methods, of which one is that you cauterize the area of his hip with three triangular cauteries according to this form; and make them of good depth. And let the space which is between each cautery be the amount of the thickness of a finger, whether the cautery is olive-shaped or large.
But sometimes let the cautery be in the middle of the area, on the head of the hip, so that there are four cauteries.
And if you wish, cauterize with the circle whose form precedes in the cauterization of hip dislocation, so that it is a continuous circle and burns the thickness of the entire area.
And this is the method of cauterization in sciatica when the pain is adherent to the hip and not extended to the thigh.
But if it is extended to the thigh, then cauterize that with two cauteries on the thigh, on the place where the patient indicates the pain is; and with one cautery on the groin, four finger-widths slightly below the left side.
And if the cautery is knife-shaped, let the depth of the cautery be according to the amount of the thickness of the area.
And if the patient indicates that the pain is extended to the area near the toes, then cauterize there where indicated with a cautery in three or four points, or more if that is needed.
And if the patient indicates the pain to be below the buttock towards the thigh, then cauterize there with a knife-shaped cautery.
And be careful in all cauterization that you do not go so deep with the cautery that you burn nerves or large arteries, and that because of this you do not cause the patient harm, such as loss of feeling or contraction (of muscles).
And I have seen one and another of those whom I cauterized on the ankle, and went too deep in the cauterization, and the leg became swollen until the swelling reached the foot, and the whole foot was perforated and corrupted (decayed/infected). And then a severe case of diarrhea followed, and then death.
And if the pain is in two places at once, cauterize each according to this manner (method).
And some wise men say, in the cauterization of the ankle, use a cautery of which this is the manner: make a circle of iron whose circumference is half a palm; and let its opening be according to the thickness of a date pit, or a little less.
And within that circle let there be another third; and let the space which is between them be the quantity of a hazelnut; and let those circles be open on two sides; and let the length of those irons be a hazelnut or two hazelnuts, and make in those irons a properly prepared margin within the circles; and this is its form:
Then let it be heated in the fire until it glows red and throws off sparks, and then let it be placed on the part of the ankle, the patient lying on their side. And then cauterize that with three round cauteries at once.
Then leave it for three days; then plaster it with a bandage and leave the wound open for many days; and then cure it with ointment until it is healed.
Albucasis says: O, son, we have used this type of cautery little because of the fear of looking at it, and because we have found few who dare to look at it.
But of the good cauteries to him who suffers them, and finds with it the place of it. (This sentence is grammatically awkward and its meaning somewhat ambiguous. A more natural English rendering might be: "But among the good cauteries is one which those who undergo it find effective.")
But cauterization with burning medicines is that you make two circles having a perforated handle in which you place the iron head; the length of the walls in height should be two nuts or something like that.
And if the space which is between each is the quantity of the thickness of a finger; and they are open below and above, and they contain themselves between them according to this form:
Then place the patient on their side over the affected part of the hip, and press your hand on the affected part of the hip; then take hot water and pour it between the two circles, and let the heating be gradual with a small fire, and keep it there for about an hour, and let the patient endure the burning and cauterization—for this feels like a burning—until the burning stops.
Then lift your hand with the instrument and wipe the hot water from the entire hip with fresh water.
Then leave it for three days and apply a bandage until the blackness of the burn is removed, and leave it for some days, and pus will drain from it; this is the last treatment.
Then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if the pain extends to the hip or leg, make for him a bath that contains water, following the same procedure as was done for the hip.
And this is the method of hot water, and I have already written about it in the treatise on the rectification of medicines. Take alkali and quicklime, an equal part of each, and crush them and put them in a new pot, the bottom of which is perforated by a small hole, according to the quantity that will enter the ray [presumably, a body part or instrument].
And place under the bottom of the pot another inverted pot, and pour over the alkali and quicklime fresh water, which will cover them by a finger's breadth, and when you have compressed them with your hand with good pressure, and allow the sharp water to descend from above into the inverted pot below.
And then collect all that water, and pour it over more fresh quicklime and alkali, for then it will be of very strong acidity, which is used in many medical operations and in cauterizing other members, because it works like fire.
And some of the medicines with which it is also cauterized are, thus,  verdigris and honey and quicklime with soap, so that they are mixed.
And Galien recounts the operation and cure of a man from antiquity, with which sciatic nerve and vein pain was cured, and greatly praised this method, saying that it needed no other treatment, and because it was healed at once as he recounted, because he very easily entered the bath and was free of it.
And it is that some fresh rue should be taken, and if fresh is not found, a fresh fig should be taken; and it should be well crushed with a little lard and placed on the hip where the pain is, or on the thigh, or on the leg, and it should be bandaged.
And it should be left on for three hours, or for however long the patient feels relief from the burning.
Then put him in a bath, and when his body is reddened, expose him to the sun, for the pain ceases and he is healed.
And know that one should not use cautery with these medicines except after the evacuation of the body.
But already Dioscorides said that when the varicose vein is cauterized with a goat's horn, it profits him very well; and the cautery should be done in this manner: wool should be taken and soaked in old oil, and placed between the arches of the foot and between the bones.
Then take a dry goat's horn and burn it in the fire until it becomes charcoal; and then place it on the wool and leave it until it is extinguished. Then repeat this, and do not cease doing this until the sensation of burning reaches the ankle and relieves the pain.
And this manner of cautery is called cautery of vapors.  (or possibly "cautery by fumes" or "cautery of fumes" depending on the precise intended meaning of "vaquas" in this context).
Chapter 42.
Concerning the cauterization of toothache.
Sometimes pain is in the teeth from various causes, such as from a fall or a blow, or from excessive evacuation, and from causes similar to these, by the infusion of cold and humid matter.
And cauterization is only suitable for that single type which is caused by the effusion of cold matter.
Therefore, after evacuation of the patient with purging pills and similar remedies,  cauterizations should be made on the tooth where the pain is, three lines according to its width, making marks with ink on each line for five cauterizations or more, depending on the patient's tolerance and constitution.
And the cauterization should be done with a pointed cautery.
And if you wish, cauterize with three or four cauteries using a nail-shaped cautery according to this form:
Chapter 43.
On cauterization at the beginning of jaundice.
This illness often happens to small children; and a sign of its beginning in children is that it affects their structure in the wing with elevation and movement.  (The "wing" likely refers to the area around the shoulder or ribcage).
And you find in the last vertebra of the back and the nape of their neck that it already begins to rise above the other vertebrae.
And so when you see this and want to prevent it, then cauterize that with a cautery that is circular according to this form: so that the cautery takes in all parts of the vertebra equally.
And if you wish, cauterize in the circumference of the vertebra with the pointed cautery two or three lines. And let the points be close to each other.
And then treat the area until it is healed with what we have said.
But beware that cautery is not administered in swelling that is caused by a spasm of the nerves.
Chapter 44.
Concerning the cauterization of gout and joint pain.
When joint pain is caused by cold humors which are infused into each member of the body, then when pain comes in the feet, it is customary for doctors to call this gout properly.
And therefore, if gout caused by cold is cured with a method of cure and the pains are not removed, then cautery removes them.
And it is so that you cauterize that near evacuation in the area of the joint of the foot with many cauteries.
And let the cautery be olive-shaped, of which this is the form:
And if it is necessary that a puncture be made on the surface of the foot, then do it with a pointed cautery. And if the pains rise into the knees or other joints, and this often happens, then cauterize that area on each knee with three or four cauteries on each side with this olive-shaped cautery.
And if many cauteries are necessary, then cauterize that area, and do not press too hard with your hand using the cautery beforehand; let it be only around the thickness of a finger.
And if the pains rise into both knees, then administer the cautery which we have mentioned in its chapter.
And if the pains are only in the hands, and it is called "suagra" (likely a form of hand ailment), then puncture in the area around the knuckles, as if revolving, puncturing in those two lines.
And if the pains remain in the fingers, then puncture in the area around the knuckles on each finger and on the palm of the hand.
And if the pains rise near to the elbows or muscles in a few days, then cauterize them on every side of the joints.
And do not neglect the patient's good regimen and reception of medicines, for if a good regimen is followed and the inflammation is evacuated with this cautery, he will be healed, God willing.
Chapter 45.
Concerning the cauterization of ruptures (or fractures).
When a rupture occurs in the groin, and the intestines protrude before the scrotum, and this begins to happen frequently, then it is necessary that you instruct the patient to cease eating on that day and that he use lenient remedies so that the rupture descends from his belly.
Then support the patient between your hands on his back, and instruct him to hold his breath until the hernia or the intestine is expelled or appears.
And then return that with your fingers and gently push the rupture back over the pubic bone, noting that the extremity should resemble a semicircle in the upper parts of the body.
And then heat the cautery, of which this is the form:
until it is very red and throws off sparks. And then return the intestine or hernia to its cavity, and then place your hand on the area so that the intestine does not protrude.
And you have already opened the space between the patient's thighs and placed a cushion underneath; and another servant should be at their legs, and another at their feet, who should hold their hands.
And then place the cautery on that mark with your hand, keeping the cautery straight, and hold it there until you reach the bone of the hip through the middle of the swelling; and do this again another time if you did not reach the bone on the first attempt.
And observe carefully the position of the intestine during your cauterization, so that you do not burn the intestine, lest death or other great harm come upon the patient.
And know that if you do not reach the bone with the cautery, your operation is not complete/successful.
And it is necessary that the cautery for children be small according to their size and measure.
And then care for the cauterized area for three days with a poultice until the burn scab is shed.
And afterwards treat it with other ointments until it is healed.
And the patient should lie on their back for forty days until the area and wound are sealed/healed.
And it is necessary that you give him nourishment according to the length of his treatment, which will cleanse his belly, and that the intestine does not protrude due to the effort of healing.
And then, when he wants to get up after about forty days, apply a properly made bandage, and keep it on for another forty days.
And let him be reduced in work and the fullness of food and drink, and vehement voices, for when he uses this regimen of healing entirely, he will be healed.
And I will describe the treatment of ruptures in other parts of the abdomen; and at the beginning, if you want them not to spread, cauterize the rupture with a round cautery according to its size, and let that which is exuded from the cautery be like a third part of it; and then treat it with what we have said, and it is well-adjusted.
And some earlier authorities said that a rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery according to this shape, since it is marked with a probe:
and a line is placed across the widest part of the rupture, and another line above it; and place your hand with a pointed cautery, but the first cautery is lighter and better.
Chapter 46.
Concerning the cauterization of torsion (or twisting).
When pain develops in some member because of a fall or a blow, and that pain persists for a long time and does not yield to treatment with medicines, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And let the cauterization of each limb be according to its size and smallness, and its strength and weakness, and the location of the torsion and its pain.
Therefore, if it is healed by the first cauterization, it is good; and if not, then repeat the cauterization; for it is customary for such pains to be punctuated from one limb to the one adjacent to it, so it is necessary to treat those with cautery until the patient is healed.
Chapter 47.
On the cauterization of leprosy.
Lepers are sometimes helped greatly by cauterization, especially if the type of leprosy is caused by putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy.
And so, if you wish to cauterize someone, consider whether the leprosy is spreading and whether it can be cured with what we have said in the division and if not, and you fear that the corruption will affect the whole body and its entire complexion, then cauterize the person on the head with five cauteries: one in the middle of the head, and the second just below it, towards the forehead at the hairline; and two more on the temples; and one at the back, on the nape of the neck.
And finally, with the cautery, press your hand slightly inwards so that a small impression is made on the bone, and the scabs are removed, so that the expulsion of thick vapors is relieved; and cauterize over the spleen as previously described.
And if the leprosy is already widespread on the patient and manifestly apparent, then you must cauterize with the cauteries we mentioned on the head; and with a cautery on the tip of the nose, and with two cauteries on the two cheekbones, and with two cauteries on two vertebrae of the neck, and with six cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and with one large one on the vertebrae near the coccyx, and one on the coccyx, and with two on the hips, and one on each hip, and with two on the buttocks and one on each buttock, and with two on the knees, and with two on the muscles, and with two on the ankles of his feet; and cauterize on all the joints of the fingers of the hands and feet, and on each heel of his feet, and on the wrists of his hands.
And beware of the nerves that are on the heel, that you do not burn them.
And sometimes it is cauterized with one cautery on the pubic bone and another on the stomach opening, and another on the liver.
And know that the more cauteries you apply, the more helpful and beneficial it is; and know that the patient does not feel pain from the cautery, as they do from cuts, because their body is already numb.
And the cauteries should be large or small according to the limbs and joints, as prescribed by the types of instruments.
Then treat the cautery with cooked barley flour mixed with honey and with other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
On cauterization for numbness.
When a limb is numb or asleep and has been treated with medicines, oils, and plasters, and is not healed, then you should cauterize that numb limb with cauteries according to the size or smallness of the limb.
And let your cautery penetrate the thickness of the skin a little. And afterwards, treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And sometimes a numbness that extends to the hands and feet, and to the vertebrae of the back, is cauterized near the emergence of the nerves that move that limb, and the numbness is relieved.
But do not attempt this except someone skilled in the anatomy of the limbs and the emergence of the nerves moving the whole body.
Chapter 49.
Concerning the cauterization of albaras, which is morphea.
When albaras is chronic and you have not administered any medicinal ointments, then cauterize it with a cautery iron a little according to the size of the affected area until the whiteness and its color are relieved and its color is changed.
Then treat it with flour of barley and lentils with rose oil and with leaves of arnaglossa, and with pigeon's blood or lizard's blood; take a portion of each and mix them all together, and then spread a cloth and place it over the area until it is healed.
Chapter 50.
Concerning the cauterization of cancer.
When cancer is beginning and you want to remove it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery iron around its circumference as it is swollen.
And the learned say that it should be cauterized with a pointed cautery in the middle; but to me this is not advisable, for I fear that it will become ulcerated, and I have already seen this happen several times.
And it is more proper that it be cauterized around its circumference with a circle, as we have said, or with many cauteries.
Chapter 50. (Assuming a Roman numeral system)
Concerning the cauterization of a bubo/swelling.
When a bubo is slow to mature, and this is due to the superfluity causing it, or due to the patient's age—because they are old and decrepit, with little blood—or due to the season, and you wish to hasten the maturation of the bubo; then cauterize around its circumference with many small, pointed cauteries; and then leave it, for it will hasten maturation.
But if you wish to perforate it with a cautery, then you heat the cautery, of which this is the form: and place it in the middle of the bubo until it penetrates the skin.
And if the cautery is in that lower place, so that the body of the arm may be relieved; and then care for it until it is healed.
Chapter 52.
Concerning the cauterization of a corrosive (ulcer/sore).
A corrosive is a corruption moving in the limb, which corrodes it as fire consumes dry wood.
