Therefore, if you see this corrosive in a place which can tolerate cautery with fire, then you should ignite very small, hot cautery nails,  large ones as is appropriate for the place where the corrosive is.
And then cauterize it completely until you eradicate all the corrosion and no cause of it remains.
And then leave it for three days, and place crushed salt mixed with oil on the cauterized areas until the scabs are completely gone and all the corruption is eliminated.
And then treat it with a flesh-generating ointment.
But if after three days you see that healthy flesh has grown where there is no corruption, it is good; but if not, then repeat the cautery on what remains in the corrupted areas.
And a corrosive is sometimes medicated with a sharp medicine, for that is a substitute for cautery. But however, cautery with fire is of more readily available help.
And I have already mentioned the treatment of it with sharp medicine in the division, and therefore take that when you have need of it.
Chapter 53.
Concerning the cauterization of wounds and other similar things.
Many times there happens to the parts under the foot a disease that is called a wound, and it is a large, deep wound, which harms the foot; and cautery in those cases is according to two methods, that is, cautery with fire and cautery with hot water.
But cautery with fire is that you heat the concave cautery similar to a vulture's beak, which should be made of iron according to the size which contains the whole wound, and should be of fine edges.
And then place that heated iron upon the wound, and then move your hand with the cautery in a circle around the wound until the cautery reaches the depth of the wound. And leave it there for three days until it begins to shed eschar. And then plaster it with crushed wild mallows mixed with salt, and leave the plaster on it for one night, for this will eradicate it from its roots.
And then treat the area with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
And if it is not converted, and this often happens on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet, then you should administer cautery with water to those areas.  Take a cannula of bronze or iron or a vulture's quill, and place it over the wound; and then pour a small amount of hot water into the cannula, and hold your hand, and you should move it with the pressure of your hand altogether, and let the edge of the cannula be pressed into the root of the wound, and the hot water will find its way to the root of the wound; and let the patient endure the burning of the hot water for about an hour, and then remove it, for the wound with its roots will be eradicated.
And do this with those and then the others until you have reached all those which are in the body.
And then treat those sores, once the roots of the wounds are eradicated, with those ointments which generate flesh.
Chapter 54.
On the cauterization of tremor.
When someone experiences tremor due to cold in the nerves, or due to a cold fever, or a similar cause, then you should cauterize them with four or five cauteries on the spinal vertebrae, one cautery between each vertebra; and with a cautery on their feet, and on their stomach, using an olive-shaped cautery.
And their tremor will subside, and the healing of the cold illness will be hastened.
Chapter 55.
On the cauterization of accidental swellings in the body.
When swellings appear on the body, and there are pustules and fissures caused by thick, corrupted matter; then, at the beginning, when they appear, it is necessary that they be cauterized on the top of the fistula with a thin cautery, with myrtle wood whose end is ignited with fire; or with long roots of eryngo; or with a lenticular cautery.
And sometimes a cautery is also applied at the beginning of their expulsion in this manner; and they are not believed, and the superfluity is broken up by making those, and it heals from them. The translation attempts to convey the general sense.)
However, it is necessary that this be done near evacuation with phlebotomy.
Chapter 56.
Chapter 56: On the cauterization of bleeding caused by an arrow wound.
Many times, bleeding occurs from an arrow wound which is incised through the wound which comes from outside, or through the incision of abscesses or cautery of the limb, and similarly, and it is difficult to stop that.
Therefore, when this happens to someone, then you press with your hand on the opening of the arrow wound, and place your finger on it tightly many times until the bleeding stops below your finger, and it does not come out.
Then put small and large olive cauteries in the fire, many in number, and blow on them until they are very hot.
Then take one of those, small or large depending on the location and wound where the artery is broken,  apply it to that vein quickly after you have removed your finger, and hold the cautery until the bleeding is stopped and restrained.
And if the blood is expelled from the opening of the artery when you remove your finger and remove the cautery, then take another cautery carefully from among the cauteries which are prepared in the fire, and then do not cease doing this one after another until the bleeding is stopped.
And be careful not to burn the nerve that is there, and that another harm comes to the patient.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, it is not possible to stop it in those cases, and especially when the artery is large, except in one of four ways: either with a cautery as we have said, or with extraction when it is not extracted, for when it is extracted, its extremities are best burned and the blood is stopped, or that it be tied with thread or with a ligature by strong constriction, or that medicines whose virtue is to stop blood be placed upon it; and they should be held tight with a compress with appropriate constriction.
But he who administers proper constriction with a ligature and with tight bandages or by the application of cauterizing agents and similar things, does not confer it except late.
But if this happens to someone and the physician or medicine is not present, then gently press the index finger over the opening of that vein as we have said, and apply strong pressure to it until the blood is stopped from the main part of the wound, and the finger should not be moved from the artery; and over the artery, let cold water of great coldness be sprinkled until the blood is congealed and thickened; and then consider whether cautery or medicine is needed.
Of incisions and perforations and cupping and of phlebotomy and of wounds and their similars.
We have already discussed in the first chapter every illness in which cautery with fire and with burning medicine is suitable, and the occasions and causes thereof, and the necessary instruments with the forms of the cauteries; and I have arranged the chapters from the top of the head down to the feet.
And I will proceed in this chapter in the same manner, so that the one who seeks this may be relieved, then concerning that which he will have requested.
And before I begin this, it is necessary, son, that you learn that in this chapter is that which is in the first chapter on cauterization, but in a less frightening way.  (Or: ...but this chapter is less frightening than the first chapter on cauterization.)
And because great caution is necessary, for in the operations which are in this chapter, many times there occurs evacuation which is with blood, with which life is maintained, through the opening of a vein, or incision upon an abscess, or opening of a fistula, or purgation of a wound, or extraction of an arrow, or incision of a stone, and the like. And all of these are accompanied by fear and trembling, and in most of them there is the danger of death.
And I warn you that this should not happen to you, that is, hesitation, for sometimes you will deal with men with many kinds of illnesses. And some of them are angry with their illness and death is light to them, and this because of the vehemence of what they experience from their illness, and because of the length of their illness, and with the illness is a fear which signifies death.
And among them is one who gives you his possessions and says, you are hoping for his health, and his illness is pernicious.
Therefore, it is not fitting that you consent to that which comes to you, of which this is the manner; and your caution should be greater than covetousness and your greed; and do not presume any cause regarding this except with certain knowledge which verifies this for you, and to which a praiseworthy outcome follows.
And use in the cure of all your patients prognostication and indication of him to whom health is restored, for in that is to you help in the acquisition of fame and memory, of praise and of glory.
O, sons, may God inspire in you His understanding and not withhold from you guidance and protection, for this is in His hand.
And I have ordered this chapter, distinguishing it by chapters as preceded in the chapter on cauteries, from the care of the head to the feet, so that that which you ask for and of which you have need may be relieved for you.
Chapter One.
Of the treatment of water which is contained in the heads of children.
Know that this illness often happens to children at birth and when the nurse squeezes the child's head without gentleness.
And sometimes it happens for a hidden cause which is not known.
But I have only seen this illness in children; and in all those whom I saw, death followed, and thus it is in my experience a fatal condition for them.
And I have seen that a child whose head was filled with water, and his head was always thus enlarged until he could not support himself because of the size of his head, and moreover, the humidity increased until he died.
And this humidity is either accumulated between the skull and the bone, or it is accumulated under the bone on the membrane.
Because if the humidity is between the skull and the bone and the abscess is small, then you should make a fissure in the middle of the head with a cut in the width, and the length of the fissure should be about two finger-widths so that the humidity can drain, and this is the shape of the spatula:
And therefore, if the humidity is more increased and the abscess is larger, then there are two fissures in this manner:
And if the humidity is under the bone, the sign is that you see the sutures of the head open on all sides and the water is squeezed out when you compress it with your hand from the inside, and this is not hidden from you. You should make a cut in the middle of the head with three fissures in this manner:
And then extract all the humidity through the incision; and then bind the fissures with cloths and soft feathers; and then wash it with wine and oil until the fifth day, and then untie the bandage and treat the wound with tents and ointments, and bind the head evenly; and let the patient eat dry food with little humidity until the member is strengthened and healed.
And there is another method of incision, that you consider where the magnitude of the abscess and the collection of water appear, for sometimes it is more in the occiput, or in the front part, or on the right, or on the left; and therefore aim with the incision where the abscess and the collection of water appear, and do it as best you can.
And observe that you do not cut the artery and cause a flow of blood, and the patient dies from that blood flow with evacuation of humidity.
Chapter second (or Chapter two).
When a catarrh or rheum occurs in the eyes or feet, and this is chronic, and medicinal cures do not work, then the last resort is incision of those two arteries.
And therefore, when you wish to incise them, the patient should shave their head, and rub the area with a rough cloth so that the arteries appear, and the patient's neck should be constricted with a cloth; and observe where the vein pulsates; and the location of each is near the ears, compressed, and thus hidden except in some men.
Then mark the artery with a probe, and incise each one with a sharp lancet from each side with an incision reaching to the bone, and let this be across the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the lancet under the artery and sever it at the incisions above.
And let the length of the incision be about two fingers' breadth, because when a vein is incised, the blood comes out with a pulsating flow, which leaps out frequently.
But if the artery does not appear to the touch, then you must measure from the ear the distance of three fingers, and mark it with a probe, and cut to the bone.
And the amount of blood that should be let is about six ounces, and quickly let out more or less, and all this is according to the strength of the patient and the fullness of their artery.
And then examine the wound, and if any cause of infection remains on the bone, then incise it so that a hot abscess does not develop.
And then bind the wound with lint and linen bandages, and treat it with ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Concerning the extraction of arteries which are in the temples.
When someone suffers from chronic migraine or sharp headaches, due to acrimonious humors and a quality in the tissues of the temples, or a similar, longstanding and vehement affliction, and it is treated with medical remedies and this does not help, then we should attempt in these illnesses the extraction of the arteries in the temples, or their cauterization as we have shown.
And the manner of the operation and extraction of these is that the patient shaves the hairs which are on the temples; and attend to the artery apparent in the temple, for it is shown to you by its pulse, and is hidden only in few men by the vehemence of cold.
And if it is hidden from you, then let the neck of the patient be constricted with some cloth; and then rub the place with a rough cloth or evaporate the place with warm water until the artery appears to you manifestly. And then you take the spatula of which this is the form: And then you scrape the skin with that [spatula] easily until you reach the artery; and then put in [the artery] an instrument and extract it to the upper parts until you extract it from the skin and uncover it from the tunics which are beneath it, from every part.
But if the artery is subtle, then wrap it with the end of the oncii [a surgical instrument], and then incise a part of it according to the quantity by which its ends are lengthened and contracted, and no flow of blood will happen, for if they are not incised the blood will not flow at all.
And then evacuate from one to three ounces of blood.
And if the artery is large, then it is necessary that you tie it in two places with strong double silk thread or with cords at length, and do not allow it to putrefy before the consolidation of the wound and the occurrence of blood flow.
And then incise the superfluity which is between the two ligatures; and do this at that time or at another time.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery up to the bone with a knife-shaped cautery until its ends are incised, and it will be in place of that same operation.
And it is a better procedure unless the patient has a fever or a hot complexion; for cautery is one of those things that helps to dry up humors, and therefore is better in that case.
And it is necessary after the extraction of the arteries that you clean the wound with old linen, and that properly made powders are placed upon it, and then it should be treated with drying medicines to produce granulation and with dressings until it is healed.
And if while you operate another time, a flow of blood from the artery occurs, then you should take care to restrain that blood, either with a cautery, or by filling the area with linen, and squeezing with your hand until the bleeding is stopped. And if none of those things is at hand, then place your finger on the area until the blood is congealed, and apply water of extreme coldness to the area until the sharpness subsides, and it is constricted as necessary.
And that which is easiest in the extraction of an artery is that you cauterize the vein with this cautery having two blades, after you have marked the two places with a probe, and apply each, very hot, until they reach the bone and sever the vein in two places, so that that which is between its extremities is elongated, for they are not completely consolidated by this cautery.
And this is the form of the cautery:
And let the sharpness of the two blades be equal. However, if a lesser sharpness than two knives is better, for if they are sharp like two knives close to each other, they will not sever the flesh with speed.
Therefore, since in those there is some thickness, the heat of the fire is retained in them and the flesh is severed easily.
And this method is better in every respect and gentler.
And let the space which is between the two knives be the amount of the thickness of a finger.
Chapter Four.
Chapter on the treatment of sharp and acidic tears from the eyes.
When there are acidic tears, and the source of those is in the veins above the forehead, and you find no cause for treatment in medicine, and sometimes the patient's face reddens and they feel a crawling sensation on their forehead as if it were the crawling of ants, and their eyes are strained, wet, so that the skin around them is swollen, and the eyelids are inflamed by the sharpness of the tears, the ancients used various methods in the treatment of this condition, and I will describe that treatment with a sponge.
And it is that you instruct the patient to shave the hairs that are on their forehead, and then incise three fissures on the forehead, equally distant according to the length of the forehead. And if the length of the fissure is two fingers: one fissure should be equally distant from the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second according to the length, a little from the movement of the muscles which are in the lacrimal area, and the third on the other side.
And be careful of the incisions of the arteries which are on both sides, and extend your hand from the continuation of both jaws. And if the space of each incision is the amount of three fingers, adjusted.
And if you have a piece of prepared sponge or very dry cloths with which to dry the blood.
Then insert a spatula with two sharp ends, of which this is the form of its incision: which is from the part of the temples to the second incision. Excoriate with that [tool/instrument] the entire area between the two incisions with the cypha [surgical instrument] which is on the bone, and then do this from the middle incision to the second incision. And then remove that spatula and insert into the first incision again another instrument which is called a cuticular knife, sharp on one side and not sharp on the other.
And place the sharp edge of that [instrument] against the flesh attached to the bone, and the blunt edge towards the bone, and push it in until it reaches the middle fissure, and sever with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes.
But let the incision be continued to that part of the bone, and then do this in the middle fissure to the other fissure.
And after a sufficient quantity of blood has flowed, press the places of the coagulated blood clots.
And then place in each fissure a piece of old linen, and put on that powders soaked in wine and oil and vinegar, and see that a hot abscess does not develop.
And on the third day untie the bandage and administer a large infusion of warm or tepid water, and then treat it with basilicon ointment with rose oil and others until the wounds are healed.
Chapter 5.
Concerning the treatment of tears caused by cataracts and descending to the eyes from the inner parts of the head.
When someone has acute and persistent cataracts, and the patient's eyes appear small and sunken, and their vision is weakened, and the eyelids are ulcerated and their skin shed, and there is sharp, persistent pain in the forehead and consequent sneezing, then know from these symptoms that the matter and cataracts come from many veins originating in the inner parts.
And the best cure in such cases is this: order the patient to shave their forehead, and then incise a fissure in the middle of the forehead, a small one in width (and begin the incision from the left temple to the right temple; and let the incision be between the bones; and extend your hand to the temples' muscles which are swollen from chewing), until the bone is exposed.
Dry all the blood with a sponge or a dry cloth and then fill the space between the two lips of the fissure with old cotton or linen cloths. And then tighten it above with powders boiled in wine and oil, so that a hot abscess does not develop.
And when you unbind it and see that the abscess has diminished, then it is necessary to scrape the bone until new flesh begins to grow, and then treat it with a drying regimen that promotes the growth of flesh, such as if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony and mix them, and from them an ointment is made and applied to these wounds to promote the growth of flesh.
Chapter 6.
Concerning the treatment of what falls into the ears.
Everything that falls into the ears is one of four types: either a mineral stone or something similar to stone, such as iron or glass; or it is from a plant, such as chaff or a bean; or it is a fluid substance, such as water, sour wine, and similar things; or some animal.
And therefore, when a stone or other similar substance falls into the ear, and it does not dissolve or grow in the ear, place the ear in the sun; and if you see the stone, then instill some violet oil or sesame oil into it, and then proceed with its extraction, and induce sneezing with a suitable remedy, and block the nostrils before the onset of sneezing. Then place some pieces of cloth or wool around the ear, and pull the ear upwards, for often it is expelled by these means.
And if it is not expelled, then proceed with its extraction using the instrument called a delicate epicyclic scoop, of which this is the form:
And if it is expelled with the scoop, it is good; and if not, then proceed with its extraction using a thin probe with little doubling.
And therefore, if it is not expelled by this, then make a small brass tube and insert the end of the tube into the opening of the ear, and seal around the tube with wax and softened oil so that nothing is open except the tube. Then extract it with your breath with strong suction, for many times it is done with what we have recounted.
But if it is not expelled in this manner, then take a small amount of ignited or prepared birdlime, which is used to catch birds, and place that on the end of a thin rod, and then carefully wrap it around that properly placed cotton. And then gently insert that into the opening of the ear, and then dry the ear of its moisture.
And therefore, if it is not expelled by all the means which we have recounted, then you should aim to extract it before it develops into a hot abscess or spasm.
And the manner of extracting it is that you first bleed the patient from the cephalic vein and extract blood according to the strength of the patient. Then, hold the ear between your hands and turn it upwards, and make a small incision at the root of the ear, below the cartilage, in a well-prepared spot. And let the incision be crescent-shaped until it reaches the stone; and, the stone found, remove it if you can with appropriate instruments; and then quickly sew up the incision, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the cause affecting the ear is a growth that is increasing and swollen, then administer its cure and extraction; but if it does not obey your treatment, then take fine pounded lead according to this method: and apply this to the excision of the growth which has fallen into the ear.
And do not do this when you think that it is already moistened by the vapor of the ear, and place it on cotton pads, and then extract it with tweezers or with a fine instrument, or with an incision as we have said, for its extraction is thus eased.
But if water has entered the ear, the patient should use sneezing as a first remedy, and fill his ear with some old cotton, and lie on the ear into which the water has entered; and if it is expelled by this, it is good; and if not, then take some heated smooth stones and pebbles, according to the length of the finger, and warm them slightly in the fire, and the patient should insert one of these into the opening of his ear and jump on one foot on that side and strike with another stone on the stone which is inside the ear. Therefore, do not cease this with the stone until all the water is expelled.
Therefore, do not cease this with the stone until all the water is expelled. And if the water is not expelled in this way, take paper or tow and place one end in the ear, and hold the other end to the fire until the majority of it is burnt.
And then put in more tow and do this several times until all the water is expelled, and extract it with a cannula as described above for the stone extraction.
But in the extraction of a creature entering that ear, if it is small, like a fly or similar, cure it as we have said in the division; but if it is large, which appears to the eye, then administer its extraction with forceps and hooks, and its cause is the easiest of all the other causes that fall into the ear.
But in the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you treat it with what we have said in the division and in the treatise on distillations and your treatment does not work, then you must expose the ear to the sun.
And so if any cause of the worms appears there, then extract it with forceps or with fine hooks; but if no cause appears, then take a cannula that is narrow below and wide above, of which this is the form: And introduce the narrow end into the ear according to the amount the patient can tolerate; and then suck with it with a strong suction, and do this many times, until all the worms are expelled.
But if it does not obey you in this way, then you fill and close the opening of the cannula with wax as we have said; then administer distillations which the ancients have already proven in the treatment of worms, and you will find this in the treatise on distillations.
And let your effusion, with which you pour the medicinal oils into the ear, be with this instrument, of which this is the form: And make it of silver or bronze, narrow below, in which there is a small hole, and wide above.
And so let it be such that it pushes that which is in the concavity of the cannula towards the outside where it will be necessary. And if you wish, take a rod and wrap its end decently with cotton, and then pour oil into the concavity of the ear, or juice of some herb, or whatever you wish of these medicines into the cannula—and this is in the ear—and then insert the rod with cotton on it and press with your hand, with a moderate compression, until the oil is expelled into the concavity of the ear and the patient feels the oil inside.
And that which is poured into the ear should be warmed a little with fire. And be careful that what you distill into the ear is not too cold, for ear medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter 7.
On the treatment of ear obstructions.
Sometimes children are born without perforated ears, and this also happens in some adults, an obstruction in the ear canal due to injury or excess flesh in it; and this obstruction sometimes occurs in the beginning of the ear canal which does not allow the passage of air, and sometimes it is in the outer parts.
And that which occurs at the beginning of the canal, which does not allow passage of air, is in the greatest disposition of difficult healing.
And in that which allows the passage of air, you should expose the patient's ear to the sun and observe it. And then if you see an apparent obstruction, open it with a thin spatula, of which this is the form:
If its extremity is small in width, one side being sharp and the other not; and the spatula should be two fingers wide so that it does not injure the ear.
And then if the obstruction is due to flesh grown in the ear or in its canal, then take those with thin hooks and incise it vigorously until all the flesh is removed.
And if the obstruction is in the opening of the ear, then take a thin, fine rod and heat it slightly with fire, and then place it in the ear canal.
And then if you have felt an obstruction with what the patient easily finds, due to the difficulty of hearing, ease its pressure gently; and be careful not to injure the nerves of the ear.
Then place in the ear a linen cloth according to the size of the opening, which you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment; and do this for several days until you are sure of the healing of the wound; or take some wool and dip it in warm water and sprinkle it with finely ground [substance, likely a powder], and use it; and if you see the ear already suppurating, then you must remove the wool and replace it with other wool which you will wrap in a plaster made with rose-colored oil, until the abscess is reduced; and then treat it until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs, then submerge a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear, and use the other treatment which restricts the flow of blood, which is described in the treatise on distillation.
Chapter 8.
Concerning the treatment of a stye which appears on the eyelids.
Styes which appear on the eyelids of the eyes are sometimes moist, sometimes dry.
Therefore it is necessary that you hold them with forceps or with a hook, and incise them with a spatula from their roots, and then place a dressing on the incised area.
But if pain with bleeding occurs, then cauterize them with a fine lenticular cautery.
And the best cauterization is near the incisions, and often they return when some cause remains from their roots; and when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Chapter 9.
Treatment of swelling that comes to the eyelids of the eyes.
Often there appears on the eyelids of the eyes some growth which resembles a swelling in its firmness and hardness, and because of this swelling it is called [a swelling/tumor].
And it is an accumulation of thick humidity in the eyelid, below and above.
Therefore, the operation on them is that you consider then if the swelling originates in that which appears from the eyelid and is spread everywhere; and its cause is slight.
Then make an incision on it lengthwise, and then peel it off completely easily until it comes out, and then suspend it with a hook and cut it off.
And if the wound is large, then you close it with sutures; and treat it until it is healed.
And if it is small, it will not harm you because its ointment restores it and causes it to heal.
And if the growth is leaning towards the inner parts of the eyelid beyond the inner surface, then raise the eyelid and suspend the growth with a hook, even if you need to incise, and press it on all sides, and if the eyelid is lengthened by the incision, this harms nothing.
Then, after the incision, wash the eye with salt water; and treat the area with a healing agent until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Concerning the treatment of a stye on the eyelid above the eye.
A stye is a swelling which is generated in the membranes of the upper eyelid, and mostly affects children, it aggravates their eyes and causes them pain in the morning; and they cannot look at the brightness of the sun because of this, for tears flow from them.
And therefore you see them lying on their faces all the time, and sleeping on their faces; and the lower eyelids and eyebrows are moist, which already show swelling, and the imminence of a stye is apparent in the eye; and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel the stye within.
And a certain method of operation is this: the patient places their head in your lap, and then you place a linen cloth on them and make a compress, and make a circle according to the size that contains the stye on all sides, and then place that on top and press with your fingers all around so that the stye is aggregated in the middle of the circle, and then make an incision in the middle of that swelling with a lancet, making a slit; and the slit should not be larger than the incision that is made in phlebotomy.
But in the incision, it is necessary that you cut completely until you reach the stye, because in most cases the stye proceeds by incision according to the form of a white swelling.
Then extract it with cloths which you have wrapped around your fingers at the outer parts and roll it between your right and left hand until it is removed.
And be careful not to push too hard in the incision, so that with the lancet you do not reach the eye and damage it.
But if the stye does not appear at the beginning of the incision, then push gently into the incision until the stye comes forth; and then extract it as we have said; and then soak cloths in wine and vinegar and place them on the area and compress them with powders.
And some people crush salt and place it in the cavity of the incision so that it draws out the remaining moisture.
And then treat it until it is healed.
But if a hot abscess occurs at the site, then treat it with soothing plasters until it is healed.
Chapter 11.
Of the ways to treat the eye.
When in the eyelids of the eyes, pustules are born under the natural skin and are chronic, then these harm the eye and cause various illnesses, such as constant tears and softening of the eyelids and membranes, until this causes destruction of the eye, which is called a disease of the eye (tactinir), and it is cured in four ways: either by cautery with fire; or with sharp medicine as previously mentioned in the chapter on cautery; or it is treated by incision and suture; or with cannulas as I will describe.
Therefore, you should place the patient's head in your lap; then, turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand, and if it is turned, it is good; and if not, insert a needle into the part where the pus is under the eyelid, and make the needle penetrate through the pus to above, and this should be – near the edge of the eyelid where the hairs grow – near the hairs, and pull the pus to the upper parts with the eyelid, and turn it with a ray [of light, presumably to better see].
Then make an incision in the inner part of the eyelid under the pustules with a sharp instrument from the greater lacrimal [duct] to the lesser lacrimal [duct]; and then extract the pus and place under the eyelid small pieces of cotton or cloth, and then sew up the eyelid with thread, and it should be like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, the shape should be according to the amount of swelling of the eyelid, for this varies in people, for some it is necessary to incise a large amount of the eyelid depending on how much the eyelid is softened.
Then make an incision with the instrument along the two lines that you have sewn, beginning from the greater lacrimal [duct] to the lesser lacrimal [duct]. And if your incision is near the natural hairs, let it be the thickness of a king's [hair/thread].  (The text implies a very thin incision).
Then place the hook in one of the two corners of the wound, and then scrape it all, and then join the two lips of the wound with glue or fine wool thread, and wipe away the blood, and stick any excess thread onto the eyebrows with some sticky substance, if you wish to do so, and if not, don't worry.
Then leave the stitches in for three or four days, and then take care of it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without sutures, and treat it with something that dries and constricts, because the eyelid is raised due to the sealing of the wound and its aggregation; but suture is better. And thus the ancients said of this (treatment).
However, let there be diligence on the part of the surgeon, and it is a good operation, and let there be no fear in him.
And there is another way to treat (this), which is to make an incision on the eyelid in the shape of a myrtle leaf, and then lift the eyelid with three separate and joined hooks according to this form:
But if this is not possible, to hold the hooks, and this is not appropriate, then take a needle which has thread and place it in the middle of the figure, and insert the other thread near the larger lacrimal duct, and the second thread near the smaller lacrimal duct, and adjust the threads between your fingers with equality, and then with those, with equal elevation, lift the whole fold upon which the incision has been made, as we have described.
And then join the two lips of the wound with a suture, and treat it until it is healed.
But if an abscess forms near your incision or fissure, treat that abscess with a plaster and similar things, and with ointments.
And sometimes it happens to the eyelid that its hairs are inverted; then it is necessary that you use what we have said about incision; but suture in each case is more skillful/artificial.
To staunch bleeding with canes is according to this method: and it is that you turn back the eyelid and incise the growth that is inside as I have recounted; and then skillfully make two small canes or splinters, whose length should be according to the length of the eyelid and their width less than the width of the space/gap. And you have already incised at the ends of each part, where you have the threads.
Then carefully add each [cane] with subtlety over what is superfluous from the eyelid of the eye, and bind the canes to each part with a strong binding, and leave it for some days, for the growth, because of the binding, will die and dry out and will rot until it falls off by itself.
But if it is delayed, then break it with forceps, and then cure the wound until it is healed.
Then, once it is solidified, the eyelid is raised and the hairs do not prick.
And this is the form of the two canes which you will make according to this form: and have the quantity of length and width neither more nor less.
Chapter 12.
Chapter 12. Of the elevation of prickly hairs in the eye with a needle when the hair is hard.
When unnatural, hard, rough, and prickly hairs are in the eyes, then the operation is that you take a fine needle and place on it a fine silk thread or linen thread, and then adjust its ends very subtly, so as not to harm [the eye] in any way. And let the length of the threads be the quantity of a palm's length.
And then you prepare another short, fine thread underneath, and tie its ends if you wish.
And then have the patient place their head in your lap, and be near the sun so that your work is illuminated, for it is a subtle and delicate operation.
Then insert the needle into the root of the offending hair,  carefully guiding it, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly until you extract it above the natural hair.
Then pull the hair out and place it in a container; let there be one, two, or three hairs, but no more.
Then pull your hand with it until the hair is completely removed with the tweezers from the eyelid along with the natural hair.
But if it is pulled with tweezers and the hair is not removed, you will pull the tweezers downwards with force, with the mixture that you have prepared with it, until the tweezers are removed from the hole above.
And observe it with the vision of the eye, for then you will remove the tweezers and the mixture, and you will gently clean and close the eye, and leave it closed for two or three days, until the hair adheres to its place and good flesh grows over it.
And if the hair is very short, then you attach a long hair from the natural hairs, and lift it with that, or leave it until it is lengthened a little, and then you lift it.
Chapter 13.
Concerning the treatment of a sty which appears on the upper eyelid.
When a sty appears in the eye, and it is a contraction of the eyelid when it is contracted upwards, or to the right or left side, due to poor healing of a wound or incision or cautery, and it is called lagophthalmos (leporina), and this condition, whether natural or accidental, is caused by the healing of a wound, incision, or cautery, or by similar poorly treated ailments.
The method of operation is that you should incise that consolidation and separate its lips, and place between them a small piece of linen cloth, and bind them until they are healed.
And it is not advisable that you use treatments that dry or constrict; for if you do this, the sty will return worse than it was before.
Use treatments that soften, such as fenugreek, and apply a poultice made from the water in which mallow and linseed have been boiled, or  dyachylon ointment liquefied with some oil, and dip the linen cloth into this and apply it as a dressing.
And the key to your treatment is that you should prevent it from healing in a way that is different from its original state.
And this operation, which we have described, is merely a correction of the sty, so that it does not return to its previous form.
Chapter 14.
On the treatment of a stye which forms on the lower eyelid.
This stye, which forms on the lower eyelid—and is truly called a stye—is of two kinds: natural and accidental.
But the accidental kind is caused by a wound, or a cut, or cautery, or other similar things.
And the method of its operation is that you take a needle with double thread, and insert it into the flesh, passing it from the left lacrimal duct to the right, until the thread reaches both ends of the flesh.
Then stretch the flesh upwards with the needle and sew it with the needle, and incise with a wide spatula. And if the eyelid then returns to its proper shape, it is good; but if not, take a reed and place it in the split, and with it reshape the eyelid, and sew the two fissures in the lower eyelid.
And let the ends of the two fissures be two angles of the incision, which you have made so that they meet, and let an angle be formed of them so that when they come together a figure is formed from them.
And this figure is the Greek letter: lambda (Λ).
Then remove the flesh from that side to the extent that the lower edge of the wound is sharp enough to form the eyelid.
Then adjust the separated parts and sew them together with woolen thread; and then treat them with the mollifying medicines and poultices that we have mentioned until it is healed.
And if it is raised by a fissure, suture, or cautery, then you must make a flat incision on the skin of the eyelid as previously described. Then separate [the wound] with a poultice between two lips as we have said.
In summary, for the treatment of a [prolapsed] eyelid, whether above or below, perform the operation as prepared according to the form of the eyelid, and that is in a variety of forms.
And the skilled craftsman always prepares the tools in every possible way so that he can return the form to its natural shape, or close to it, and the patient according to what suits him in terms of the operation and instruments, in the best possible conditions.
Chapter 15.
Of the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or to the cornea.
When this consolidation happens to many people such that the upper eyelid is solidified with the white of the eye and its natural movement and function are impeded, then you must insert the tip of a probe under the eyelid and lift it up, or spread it with an unguent.
And then you incise the adhesion with a fine spatula, which should not be sharply pointed, but rather blunt, so that the patient is not disturbed during the operation, and keep the spatula from harming the eye with its sharpness.
And let your incision carefully separate that adhesion easily until the eyelid is returned to its natural form and the adhesion is completely healed.
And then you apply salt water to the eye with which you wash it; or distil into it some alum dissolved in water.
And then separate the eyelid from the eye with a small spatula, and place wool infused with egg white on the eye, and then on the third day use consolidative remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
Concerning the incision of a nail and the fleshy eminence of the tear ducts.
Know that a nail is of two kinds, for it is either nervous, and resembles a hard, thin shell; or it is non-nervous, resembling a congealed white humidity, which when an iron touches it, or is forced to take hold with tweezers, is cut and the tweezers are not fixed in it.
And both types do not begin except from the larger tear duct, until it gradually covers the vision and prevents light and eye movement.
But the manner of operating in its incision is that the patient should place his head on your lap, and then open his eyes, and lift the eyelid with your hand, and then seize the nail with tweezers of little breadth and extend it to the upper parts. Then take a needle and insert into it a horsehair, or ox hair, or cow hair, or similar, or strong thread, or double the point of the needle little by little and insert it into the middle of the nail, and penetrate it with the needle, and tie the nail with thread; and extend it to the upper parts, and scrape with the hair the edge of the nail which covers the pupil. And thus you follow it with the hair into the back of it.
Then you incise the remainder at the root of the tear duct with a fine spatula or with small scissors, and leave the natural flesh of the tear duct so that continuous flow of tears does not result from the incision of it.
And sometimes the nail, with only tweezers extended, or with thread, as we have said, and then it should be scraped with a lion spatula, of which this is the form:
And be careful that with the spatula you do not touch the cornea and that a rupture occurs in it, and that the pupil is perforated.
Then, into the wound, put a little crushed salt or a powder made from cinquefoil, and leave it there until the next day; and then treat it with what is appropriate until it is healed.
And if the nail is not sensitive and you cannot insert the needle, nor is the tweezers inserted into it, then often this type of nail is cured with medicines, and is healed as I have recounted in the division (section).
And if you wish to incise it, then you open the wound of the patient and take a fine lion spatula of sharpened steel according to this form: with subtlety/precision. And if you see that it is rarefied and has receded and roots have grown in it, then distill from your still into the wound a powder made from cinquefoil or with rue or with crushed salt, and occlude the wound until the next day.
And then repeat the operation on it until all that is in it is removed, unless conversely a hot abscess forms in the wound, for then you leave it and cure the abscess first until it is healed.
And then repeat your operation on it with reason/judgement until it is healed.
And the prominence of the lachrymal flesh, if it is noticeable in the wound as a great hindrance, should be suspended with hooks; and incise it on one side, and do not extend the incision lest you cut the flow of tears.
And then instill into the lachrymal duct rue and cinquefoil powder until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Concerning the incision of a carbuncle and that which is born of flesh fastened in the eye.
Sometimes in the eyes of some, red flesh is born, and it grows until it covers the pupil, or approaches or presses upon the eyelids, and strongly binds the eyelids to the outer parts and is like the rose of a balustrade.
But the operation is that the patient rests his head on your lap; and then open the patient's eyes and collect all that flesh with suitable forceps for this, and hold it with forceps or tweezers; and then incise the flesh at the beginning until all is finished with the lancet, and be careful of the eye that you do not injure it during your operation.
And let your incision, with which you incise these, be with a spatula, which we have recommended in the incision of the nail, or with small scissors with which stubble is collected, according to how its form will come to this chapter.
Then, when your incision is completed and all that flesh is removed, then fill the eye with crushed salt, or instill into it a red powder similar to that of corrosive medicines, and place a bandage over the outside of the eye with egg white until it is secured from a hot abscess.
And if some cause of granulation remains and hot blood is secreted, and you fear a hot abscess, then leave the eye and treat that abscess with what cures that abscess, and then repeat the operation on it until it is healed, and similarly do with the adjacent flesh which grows in the eye of this kind.
However, you must know that you should avoid all treatment of the weak eye by natural or accidental means, operating with iron, and do not put your hand to any of these operations until you see that that member is able to withstand that operation.
Chapter 18.
Concerning the collection of pus in the eye.
Pus is caused by hardened veins that are located on the eye and prevent the vision from its function and weaken the eye over many days.
Therefore, it is necessary at the beginning that you consider if the eye in which there is pus is strong and has no other disease than the pus; then you collect that pus.
And it is that you instruct the patient to place his head in your lap, and then suspend those veins with an ounce or two, according to your judgment; and let that ounce be of subtle thickness according to this form:
Or two ounces gradually in one body according to this form:
And then let those veins be collected with fine forceps, with subtlety, and wipe away the blood little by little, until you see those veins being removed from the eye and being pulled out.
And be careful that you do not injure the eye with the ends of the spatula; and if your operation is at midday before the sun, and you are very firm in your operation that you do not incise except those veins.
And when you have completed [the procedure], then you distill into the eye verdigris or dew so that with its sharpness it corrodes what has been removed from the eyelid.
But if this is not possible for you, collect everything at that time, and then you plaster everything with something that soothes his pain, and leave it for a few days until his pain is calmed and the danger of a hot abscess is avoided.
And then repeat on him according to the same manner until he is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps:
Chapter 19.
On the reduction of nasal dislocations.
When you treat a nasal dislocation with cautery or with a sharp, burning medicine, as is described previously, and it is not healed, then there is no pus in it, but rather fissures over the abscess before its maturation, and you must extract all the moisture that is in it and the pus until the bone is uncovered.
And then, when the bone is uncovered and you see corruption or blackness in it, then you scrape it with an instrument of which this is the form:
And it is named a rasp, and it should be made of Indian iron, and its head should be round like a chisel, which should be pointed by filing or sharpened by subtle pointing (as is shown in the following form).
And place that [rasp] on the corrupted part of the bone, and then rotate it between your fingers, and press your hand little by little until you know that the corruption is scraped away.
And this should be done several times, and then restore the area with drying and astringent substances.
Then, if the area is consolidated and good flesh has grown there, and the body of the pus is separated and remains for forty days, and is not renewed, and is not abscessed, and no accident befalls it, then you know that it is healed.
And if not, then there is no other way than to reduce the fissure to the shape of the nose, and according to this method:  that is, you uncover the second bone with an iron or with a sharp medicine, and when the bone is uncovered, then you take a trephine according to this form:
Let the extremity of the iron [trephine] be triangular, and its shaft be thin and tapered to the extremity, like this you see here [the text implies an accompanying illustration].
And then place that [trephine] on the bone, and let it be near the lacrimal bone; and extend your hand a little from the eye, and then rotate your hand with the trephine until it penetrates the whole bone, and the patient will feel this because they will feel air escaping when you have your hand there.
Then restore the area with styptic and drying medicines as has been shown to you.
Then, when the flesh grows and the area is hardened, the matter which flowed to the outside parts returns to the nose, thus this is easier on the patient.
Chapter 20.
Concerning the reduction of the prominence of the eye.
When the eye protrudes entirely and is not injured by a wound, diminution, or any damage, then it is necessary to bleed the patient, and then to follow this by placing cupping glasses on the neck without scarification, drawing blood by good suction; and then place on the eye a liniment which should be made of acanthus, aloe, incense, and sarcocolla, and then bind the plaster firmly to the eye with many powders.
And the manner of the powders is that you take many linen cloths, and each cloth should be folded in four layers; and make of these many layers.  (In other words: use many folded linen cloths as a compress.)
And then place these one upon another according to the quantity that the eye needs. And then bind firmly over these with a strong cloth with a tight binding; and repeat the cupping with suction without scarification; and then remove the cupping glasses and leave the eye bound tightly day and night.
But if meanwhile the ligament is softened, then it is necessary that it be very tightly bound.
And then remove the bandage during the day and night as we have said.
And if at times the eye is already reduced, it is good; and if not, then repeat the plaster with the powders and the binding and the cupping until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
Concerning the incision of the prominence of the uvea. (or Concerning the incision of the eminence of the uveal tunic.)
When there is a rupture in the uvea, and it protrudes and extends beyond the eyelids like a grape, and the human form is disfigured by this, and you wish to incise it, then the operation is as we have recounted, that you insert the needle into the root of the uvea from below upwards, and then place another needle with double thread from the lachrymal side, and make that penetrate and release the first needle according to its position; and then incise the place of the double thread and lift with that the part of the uvea around the needle, and tighten it strongly.
And then remove the needle and place on the eye wool infused in egg white and leave it until the thread with the uvea falls off.
And then treat the eye with something that strengthens it until the wound is healed.
And sometimes gather from all sides with a circle of cloth around the eye a large prominent area, and then it should be perforated with a fine spatula that reaches the pupil of the eye, and runs the albuginous humor, and the eye descends and enters immediately.
And then tighten it until it is healed.
Chapter 22.
On the treatment of alconati (likely a type of ailment or injury;  a precise translation requires further context from the overall treatise).
This illness, which is called alconati, is nothing but a swelling which is located in the eye, similar to descending water, but it is not that.
But the method of working on it is that you make the patient sit on a prepared and straight chair, and then take his head in your hands on both sides and gently pull it until you see the swelling descend to the lower parts, and then it should be dried and not descend, and let the light be obstructed, and the patient see things as he used to see.
And if it does not descend downwards, know that it is water (referring to the fluid-like nature of the swelling).
But if the descent of the swelling is not prepared with what we have said, then make the patient sit between your hands and then take a thin spatula and use it on the pannicle [eyelid],  scraping a little below the junction of the conjunctiva with the cornea in the limbus (towards the empty space), until the swelling is expelled.
Then, when it is expelled, you instill warm water into the eye in which you have mixed honey, or water in which you have previously boiled fenugreek and honey.
And then it should be treated with other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 22.
On the treatment of the watery discharge from the eye.
We have already mentioned the types of watery discharge in the division and which ones it is appropriate to puncture with a lancet, then that according to the state of health.
Then you should make the patient sit between your hands on a chair and tie their healthy eye, very tightly.
And then place your left hand near the eyelid of their eye, if the eye with the watery discharge is the left eye; or with your right hand, if the eye is the right one.
And then with your right hand take an almond, if the eye is the left one; or with your left hand, if the eye is the right one.
And then place the end of the almond near the cornea, along the thickness of the iris in the white of the eye on the side of the smaller lachrymal gland, and then gently press the almond and rotate your hand with it.
And then penetrate the white of the eye and feel that the almond has reached an empty space.
And it should be that the distance of the almond to the pupil is the measure of the space which is from the pupil to the end of the blackness, and that is the cornea of the eye; for you will see with that pupil through the vision of the eye by the clarity of the corneal tunic.
And then place the almond into the place where the water is, and then you draw it down one time and then another, and then if the water descends immediately, then the patient sees that upon which his sight falls immediately, and the almond in his eye.
And then rest a little; then if the water has descended, then you remove it another time before the almond is cast out.
And then when it is dried and does not descend, then you extract the almond with ease, and rotate it with your hand little by little, and then dissolve in the water a little clear rock salt and with that wash the eye inside; and then place on the outside of the eye cotton or tow or wool infused in rose oil and egg white and wipe the eye with this.
But we and those who are of our time place over that a finely crushed mixture with egg white.
And if the almond does not obey you upon entering the eye because of its hardness — for there are some men whose eye is very hard — then it is necessary that you take the spatula which is called an alberid, of which this is the form:
And then perforate that joint with it, and do not force your hand into the hole, and it is only that you make a subtle path to the location of the almond.
And then introduce the almond as we have said.
And for the completion of your work, let a sleeping chamber be prepared for the patient, decently made, in which he sleeps on his back in a dark house, and let all movement be forbidden.
And position him in such a way that his body is aligned, and he does not turn his head to the right or left at all.
And let the healing be, according to his constitution, within three days.
And then release him from that dark house and expose him to a gentle view, and let him see some things, and then restore the bandages within seven days.
And it is not fitting that you do this at the hour of the treatments or immediately after the infusion. Having said this, you should avoid this because the water might easily leak due to great and vehement observation/examination.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then you should open the wound before the seventh day and treat it with something that reduces the abscess.
Then you cover his eyes and place a mask over his face so that his sight is shielded from him for some days, him being in the dark room.
And then he should be moved gradually from that dark room and gradually return to his activities.
And know that in surgery, the student is not excused from seeing certain things, and then you should be bold in the operation.
And it has come to us from some of those who are in Artach, and some say that fire is made in Ayrac, a perforated pot, with which water is drawn.
However, I have not seen anyone in our land who has perfected this, nor have I read this in any of the books of the ancients; and it is possible that this is new.
And these are the forms of the kinds of cupping glasses, so that he who is ignorant of them may know them:
And they should be made of bronze or iron properly, and their extremities should be of this triangulated subtlety, the sharp end thus, as that is the form.
Chapter 24.
Of the treatment of rotting flesh in the nose.
When in the nose diverse fleshy growths arise, of which some cause is similar to a very small scorpion, or to a polyp fish; and this illness is called polypus because it resembles that fish in appearance. And from it is cancerous flesh, foul, of dark color; and from it is flesh that is not of dark color, but leonine (lion-like).
Therefore, in that which is in these fleshy growths that are not cancerous or malignant, you should make the patient sit between your hands and place them in the sun, and open his nostrils, and lightly prick those fleshy growths with your nail. Then extract it from the outside parts, and then incise what you have grasped of that with a sharp, thin spatula from one side until you know that all the flesh has been removed; for if any part remains from which excision is possible, then you scrape it with one of the fine instruments easily until nothing remains of it. But if it bleeds profusely or a hot abscess forms, then you apply what is necessary; or if it is one of the malignant abscesses, then you incise and cauterize it until the bleeding is stopped and all cavities are removed.
Then let vinegar and water or wine be poured into the nose near the incision. But if the nose is open and no moisture proceeds from it to the palate, then you know that it is healed.
But if the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then you know that inside there is rotten flesh in the upper bones of the nose to which excision has not reached.
Then it is necessary that you introduce a linen thread into which some thickness has been added, and tie many knots on it, and place between each knot the quantity of a finger or less, and instruct the patient and introduce one end of the thread into the nose with a rod or with whatever else is possible for you.
And after that is done, let it draw its scent until it reaches the nasal passages and comes out through the throat, for often children at school do something similar, and it is an easy thing for someone who wants to do it.
Then place the two ends of the thread, one end coming out of the nose and the other remaining in the nose, and then apply sawdust of meat with the knots that are on the thread, and do this until you know that the flesh is already with the knots of the thread.
Then remove the thread, and apply to the nose, near the extraction of the blood, a poultice, and do this for three days or more until the ointment corrodes all that is rotten of the flesh.
Then, at the end of the treatment, place a lead cannula in the nose for a few days until it is healed.
But if you need a drier treatment, use that one.
And this is the form of the instrument, a head-purger, with which oils and medicines are distilled into the nostrils:
And make it of silver or bronze, which should resemble a small open lamp, like a small bowl, and the spout similarly.
And if you wish, make a closed cannula like a tube, and the cannula of the head-purger instrument should be flat and open; it should have a handle at the back, with which you hold it when you heat the oil or water, or whatever you want of the juices and extractable substances.
Chapter 25.
Of boils that arise in the extremities of the nose.
Many times a boil arises in the extremities of the nose, and it is magnified and increased in a few days, so much so that it is very noticeable.
And for that reason it is necessary that you incise it at the beginning of its appearance, and eradicate the whole thing. And then place on the area either a cautery or a burning medicine which will stay in its place.
But if its incision is delayed until it is magnified, then consider if it is hardened,  hard, of a dark color and with little feeling; then do not touch it with iron, for it is a cancerous abscess; for many times he who incises this abscess suffers great harm from it, whoever it was on.
And if the abscess is of a light touch and is not of a dark color, and sometimes incision is possible throughout it, then evacuate the patient and incise it without fear, and cure the place with that which dries and constricts until it is healed.
Chapter 26.
Chapter 26. Of the suturing of the nose and the lips and the ear when their continuity is broken by a wound or similar.
Know that when a solution of continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, you should have little confidence in that operation except in certain men.
Therefore, when such a thing happens to someone, you should consider if it is a fresh wound with its blood, and then you should join the two edges of the wound with sutures, and then treat it until it heals.
And if the edges of the solution of continuity are already separated, and each is healed, then you must scrape each part of its apparent surface until blood comes out. Then join the two edges with sutures and tighten them, and over them sprinkle dragon's blood and crushed incense, and place over the powder a piece of diachylon plaster, or of some other of the consolidating plasters, and leave it tied for two or three days, then untie it and change the plaster and leave it until the stitches are themselves cut, and then treat with ointment until it is healed.
But the manner of suturing is that you close the solution of continuity either with needles, as we have recounted in the suturing of the abdomen, or close the solution of continuity with thread, as has been shown to you.
Chapter 27.
Of the extraction of those things which come between the two edges in the inner parts.
Often there appear in many men in the inner parts of their lips certain hard abscesses resembling goose grease, and some of them are smaller and some are larger.
Therefore, you must expose the lips and slit open each of those hardnesses on all sides, and then fill the place of excision with crushed sage until the bleeding is stopped, and then it should be washed with sharp vinegar and the place treated with something styptic, until it is healed.
Chapter 28.
On the incision of the flesh attached to the gums.
Very often a growth appears on the gum, flesh attached, which the ancients called an albulus; then you must lift that with a hook or hold it with forceps and incise it down to its root, and let the pus or blood flow.
And then place crushed sage or other styptic or drying powders on the area.
And if that flesh returns, then incise it a second time, and cauterize it, for after cauterization it will not return.
Chapter 29.
Concerning the treatment of teeth with iron.
When on the surface of the teeth, both the inner and outer parts, and between the gums, rough, sticky, and sometimes blackened crusts are attached, and they become citrine or green, so that corruption reaches the gum and the teeth become loose.
And then you should make the patient sit between your hands and rest their head on your lap, and scrape the teeth on which those crusts and similar sandy substances appear until nothing remains of them.  Do this to remove the blackness, the greenness, and the others until they are cleaned.
Then, if what was initially present is removed, it is good; but if not, then you repeat the treatment on those teeth the following day, and the second, and the third, until you achieve the desired result.
And know that teeth require many scrapers of different shapes and forms, according to what is prepared for your operation. And because the scraper with which the tooth is scraped on the inner part is different from the scraper with which it is scraped between the teeth according to other forms, these forms of scrapers should be prepared by you, which you will see immediately:
Chapter 30.
On the extraction of teeth.
That you treat the tooth for its pain with all diligence and delay its extraction, for there is no cause that supplants its usefulness when it is extracted, for it is a sublime substance, for which reason its extraction is excusable.
But if the patient is compelled to have that tooth extracted, you must be firmly certain which tooth is affected, for many times the pain deceives the patient and they think it is in a healthy tooth, and you extract that one and then the pain does not cease until the diseased and aching tooth is extracted, for we have already seen this through the operations of barbers.
Therefore, when you are certain which tooth is aching, and you know it well, then you must scarify around the tooth with a spatula in which there is some virtue, until it is separated from the tooth and the gum in every part, and then loosen it with your fingers, or with fine forceps, little by little at first, until you extract it completely.
And then apply strong, large forceps for good traction or firm grip, and place the patient's head between your knees so that you hold them well and they do not move, and then pull the tooth straight so that you do not break it, nor cause the patient greater pain.
And then, if it is not expelled, then take one of those instruments, and place it under the tooth on all sides easily and forcefully remove it as was done at the beginning.
But if the tooth is cracked or eroded, then you must fill that crack with cloth and bind it tightly with a fine thread so that it is not broken at the time of your tightening with forceps.
And you must open carefully with scarification around the entire gum and take great care not to break it and leave a part of it behind, and return to the patient for the harm which would be greater than their initial pain.
And beware that you do not do what some ignorant barbers do in their haste and audacity regarding the extraction of teeth, without administering what we have recommended; for many times they cause great illnesses to men which are more difficult to cure, and their roots remain, all or some of them, or that you extract it with some bones from the jaw as we have testified many times.
And then, after its extraction, the mouth should be washed with warm wine or sour wine with salt.
And if bleeding occurs from this, as often happens, then crush some quantity of sage and sprinkle it on the wound, and if the sage does not help, cauterize.
The shape of the forceps and thin tongs with which the tooth is grasped at the beginning should be long in the extremity, short and thick in the handle, so that they do not bend when you have to work with them.
And let them be like tongs with a thick handle so that when you squeeze with them they are not bent, but with short ends.
And let them be made of Indian iron, specifically made with straight ends, and at the ends of those let there be teeth, of which some interlock with others and tighten by strong constriction; and sometimes the ends of these should be in the shape of the beak of a bird called a *ciconia* (that is, a stork), and they are of strong constriction.
Chapter 31.
Of the eradication of teeth and the extraction of bones from fractured jaws.
When, in the eradication of a tooth that is fractured, some cause remains, then you should place a poultice of cotton on the area for a day or two until the area is softened; and then insert an elevator or forceps, the ends of which resemble the beak of a bird called a *ciconia* or stork, the shape of forceps:
Let their ends be made inwardly, like a file or rasp.
And if it does not yield to you thus with these forceps or elevators, then you should dig down to the root and scrape away all the flesh and insert the instrument which is called an *atali*, that is, a small lever, the form of which is this:
And let it be of short, somewhat thick extremity, and not be bent so that it is not broken.
Therefore, if by this the root is extracted, it is good; and if not, help yourself with these other instruments, of which these are the forms: the form of the first instrument is of a triangular extremity in which there is some thickness:
The form of the second instrument is that it be triangular and of a more subtle extremity.
And now be helped with this instrument having two prongs, of which this is the following form: and with other instruments which precede in tooth extraction; for help is done with this instrument which is similar to a large hook, and this is the form:
of the triangular, curved or short extremity, which should have some thickness, and not be bent so that it is not broken.
And you should know that the instruments for loose teeth are similar to the other instruments.
But the wise and subtle artisan in the art finds for himself, according to what the operation and the diverse illnesses signify to him; for there are illnesses to which the ancients did not assign nor create a proper instrument, because of the forms of diverse species.
But if the bone of the jaw or of the other bones of the mouth is broken, or if it is possible, then let it be cured in that place with what is convenient for it of the instruments and forceps which we have mentioned in the extraction of the roots of the teeth, and help yourself with a splint of which this is the form:
And let there be some thickness little by little, and let it be tightened or held with the mouth so that it does not escape or flee until the bone is completely set; and then restore the place with medicines suitable for this.
But if there is a small rottenness in the bone, then scrape away that rottenness and its blackness, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 32.
Of the treatment of teeth that protrude above the others.
When teeth protrude, not following their natural course, their shape is irregular, and this happens more often in women, and especially in elderly women; therefore, you must consider: if a tooth is next to another tooth and  extraction or filing is not possible, then remove it; but if it is not attached to another tooth, then you should incise it with an instrument which has this shape; and it is similar to a boar's tusk, and should be made of Indian iron with a very sharp point.
And let your incision, with which you incise it, be done in several days because of the hardness of the tooth, and so that no other teeth are damaged.
And if the protruding tooth can be filed down, then file it with an Indian iron file of this shape:
And let it be entirely of Indian iron and very smooth; and let it have very small points, like a file with which needles are made, with which the tooth should be filed little by little over many days easily, so that no other tooth is damaged or broken. Then smooth it at the end, and scrape it with some razors.
And if it is a tooth of which a part is broken and hurts the tongue, then it is necessary that you file it until the roughness of that fracture is removed and smoothed, and is filled so that the tongue is not injured and speech is not impaired.
Chapter 33.
Concerning the restoration of damaged teeth with gold or silver wire.
When the front teeth suffer concussion and loosening due to a blow or fall, and the patient cannot bite on what he eats, and you have treated it with styptic medicines, and your treatment has not worked, then to prevent them from falling out,  they should be bound together with gold wire, and not silver, because silver turns green and almost crumbles after a few days, but gold is never changed.
And let the wire be of such fineness and thickness according to the amount that can fit between the teeth; and the method of tying is that you take the wire and pass the double of it between two healthy teeth, and then you make two turns of the wire between the damaged teeth, whether one or more, until the wire reaches a healthy tooth on the other side. And then repeat the weaving back to the point where you began, and tighten it gently and skillfully until it is firm.
And let your tightening, with which you bind the wire around the roots of the teeth, be such that it does not loosen.
And then cut off the superfluous ends of the wire with forceps or scissors, and smooth those ends at each location with a file, and bend them and hide them between the healthy tooth and the damaged tooth, so that they do not injure the tongue. And then leave it thus tightly bound.
But if it is loosened or broken, tighten it or them with another wire in the same manner at all times.
And this is the form of the teeth and the figure of the straightening of two healthy teeth and two damaged teeth, thus as you see them bound:
And when it is done that one or two teeth are returned to the place of each in their locations, and are grown back as we have recounted, and that they are remade/repaired.
And this is only done by good and skilled artisans.
And sometimes some material from cow bones is suitable, and it is made from them in the form of a tooth, and it is placed in the place where the tooth is missing, and it is fastened as we have said, and it remains and serves with that for a long time.
Chapter 34.
Concerning the incision of the ligament which is in the tongue and prohibits speech.
This ligament, which is under the tongue, is sometimes natural, with which one is born, or accidental and occurs due to a wound which has already healed.
And the operation on it is that you open the patient's mouth, their head being in your lap; and raise their tongue, and then incise that nervous ligament according to its width until the tongue is freed from its constraint.
But if in that area there is some hardness or knotting, and this is due to the healing of a wound, then you should make an incision and divide it across its width until you have cut the ligament and the knotting is dissolved.
And be careful that no fissure is made in the depth of the flesh and that you do not cut too deeply and cause bleeding.
Then wash the site of the incision with warm water or with sour wine or with cold water; and then place under the tongue lint; that is, a piece of cloth which the patient should keep under their tongue each night so that it does not heal together again.
But if bleeding occurs, place crushed vitriol on the area; but if the bleeding persists, then cauterize the area with a lancet-shaped cautery which is suitable for this. And then treat it with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
On the extraction of a ranula from under the tongue.
Sometimes an abscess resembling a small tumor occurs under the tongue, preventing the tongue from its natural function; and it is quickly enlarged until it fills the mouth.
And the operation is that you open the patient's mouth, sitting in front of the sun, and examine the abscess.
And if you see it is dark or black, hard, and the patient feels no sensation in it, then do not touch it, for it is cancerous.
And if it is tending towards white, and contains moisture, then you place the hook there, and incise it with a fine scalpel, and incise it on all sides; and if blood flows during your operation, then place crushed verdigris (or vitriol) upon it until the bleeding is stopped.
Then return to your work until you have completely removed it, until it is washed with sharp vinegar and salt; and treat it with the other appropriate treatment until it is healed.
Chapter XXXVI.
