Chapter XXXVI.  Concerning the extraction of an abscess from the two ears, and of that which is located in the throat, and of other abscesses.
When a goiter develops in the throat, these are glands that are gathered together like cotton, which protrude outwards and are called "two ears"; if you treat them with what we have described in the division and it is not healed, then you should consider whether the abscess is hard, dark in color, and insensitive. If so, do not touch it with an instrument.
And if it is red and the root is thick, then do not touch it with an instrument because of the flow of blood; rather, let it mature, and then you will either remove it, or it will break open by itself.
And if it is white, soft, and its root is thin, then it is something that should be incised.
And the operation is that you should consider, before your operation, whether the abscess has already matured through complete rest, or if it has diminished in any way; and then have the patient sit before the sun, their head in your lap, and open their mouth. Then the assistant, holding the patient's tongue down with their hands, using a very fine instrument of which this is the form:
And let it be made of silver or brass, and let it be as thin as a knife.
Then, as you press the tongue down with it and the abscess is shown to you, and you cast your gaze upon it, then take an ounce and place it in the almond, and pull it outwards as far as you can. Furthermore, if you pull out some material from the tunics, then immediately incise it with an instrument of which this is the form:
Furthermore, if you pull out some material from the tunics, then immediately incise it with an instrument of which this is the form: and it is similar to forceps, however its ends are curved, so that the opening of each is opposite the other, very sharp, which should be made of tempered iron.
But if this instrument is not at hand, then incise it with a spatula of which this is its form: And let it be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other side.
And after you have incised one side, incise the other in the same manner, and immediately after the incision, let the patient rinse with cold water.
But if there is a flow of blood, rinse with a decoction of pomegranate rind or myrtle leaves and similar styptic substances until the flow of blood is restrained, and then treat it until it is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses appear in the throat besides those two behind the ears, and incise these in the same way as I have described in the incision of the two sides equally.
And I myself once treated a woman with an abscess which had formed in her throat.  This abscess was dark in color, almost insensitive, and had already closed off her throat, so that the woman breathed with difficulty. I had already forbidden her to eat or drink water, and she would have died within a day or two if I had not intervened.
And the abscess had grown so much that it had formed two points, ready to burst through two openings.
Therefore I treated it with a fistula and inserted an instrument into one of the openings, and pulled it out.  A large portion was drawn out, and then I incised the part of the abscess that communicated with the other opening. Then I opened the mouth of the abscess, compressed her tongue, and finally inserted the instrument into that abscess.
Then I excised a large part of it, and only a little blood flowed from it. Therefore, the woman's throat was completely cleared, and she immediately began to drink water and then eat food.  However, I continued to incise the abscess many times over a long period, and another abscess formed in its place, which I incised until it was completely eliminated.
Then I carefully cauterized her throat; this stopped the addition (of further problems). And afterwards I wandered and did not know what God would do about it with me.
Chapter 37.
Concerning the incision of a uvula when it is abscessed.
When catarrh descends to the uvula, and it is abscessed and elongated, then it is called a perpendicular (abscess). And if it is thick and round in the lower part, then it is called a bubo.
But if it is cured with what we have said in the division, and your cure is not effective, and you see that the already existing cauterized abscess is small and subtle, then you should incise that which is round and aggregated; and if it is of that length, and is bloody, of dark color or black, and there is no feeling in it, then you should attempt its incision, for in that case there is danger to the patient.
And when you see it according to the manner I have described, its whiteness and length, make the patient sit and press down their tongue with an instrument which follows its form, and then place the lancet in the uvula and draw it downwards, and incise it with one of the two instruments which are described in the incision of the tonsils.
And it is necessary that you not incise it except that which is exactly on the natural limit without addition, for if you incise more, you will impair his speech.
And after the incision, use what we have recommended in the incision of the tonsils, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the patient is frightened by the incision, it is necessary to use skill in cauterizing him without causing fear, and the method of cauterization is only with sharp water (likely a strong acidic solution), and it is that you place the patient's head on your lap, and then press down their tongue with the instrument we have mentioned, and then take the water which we have described in the chapter on cauterization, and apply it with a live coal, and do not apply it thickly or thinly, and with it fill the concavity of the instrument of which this is the form:
Let the extremity of it be a concavity in which the medicine is placed, like the concavity of a spoon, and place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula, and let the patient lie on their side so that saliva or mucus from the mouth runs out because of the caustic medicine, so that nothing of it descends upon their throat and harms them.
And then hold your hand with the medicine, and press it on the uvula for half an hour until you see it become blackened and dried by the action of the medicine.
And if you wish, take cotton and wrap it around the end of the spoon, and soak the cotton in the medicine, and insert the spoon with the cotton into a cannula, so that the cotton adheres to the cannula, and do this as many times as necessary until you achieve what you want from the cauterization of the uvula, and then leave it, for it will dry and fall off in about three or four days.
And if you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it, and after the cauterization wipe around the uvula with cotton soaked in wine, and cleanse with it what remains around it from the medicine, and wash it with cold water, and treat it externally with an embrocation, and internally with gargles until it is healed.
And this method of treatment is safer than incision and less frightening.
Sometimes the uvula is cured with a method gentler than cautery or incision, for it is cured by fumigation in this manner: take calamint, hyssop, oregano, rue, dried Arabic gum, chamomile, and similar herbs, and mix all these ingredients, or some of them, in a pot, and submerge them in sharp wine, and boil; and the pot should be well sealed with appropriate lute, and in the middle of the pot there should be a hole, over which a concave brass instrument is placed in this manner; it may be made of silver or brass.
And insert the end into the patient's mouth so that the vapor may reach the uvula through the cannula, until the uvula is sufficiently fumigated.
And then repeat this many times until it is dry, and be careful not to perform this treatment at the beginning of the appearance of an abscess, for it would aggravate the abscess too much, and you should not do this, but rather cauterize the declaration of the abscess.
But if you do not have this instrument at hand, then take a reed and fashion a hen's egg shell at its end, so that the patient's mouth is protected, for the eggshell prevents the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this treatment is salutary.
Chapter 38.
Of the extraction of spines and that which adheres in the throat and of other causes.
Many times something adheres in the throat, a fish bone, or some other thing.
Then it is necessary that you extract that which is apparent, upon which the eye falls, after you press the tongue with an instrument in front of the sun, so that it may be declared to you what is in the throat.
And that which does not appear and is hidden from you in the throat, you must make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, for very quickly the thing attached to the throat will be expelled by vomit; or let the patient swallow a piece of turnip or lettuce root or a piece of dry bread; or take a piece of dry sea sponge, bind it tightly with thread, and then let the patient swallow it, for when it arrives at the place where the spines are, it will extract them and they will be expelled. And further, take an eel, and bind it with thread, and let it be put inside and quickly extracted; or take a leek and quickly insert it and quickly remove it.
But if it is not expelled by those things which we have said, then use a lead instrument according to this form:
And let it be thicker than a finger, and at its end let there be a concavity which you insert into their throat with ease.
And let him have his head raised, and be careful not to touch his throat bone, so that it does not harm him, and with that, push the bone or spine, or let the physician do it with his hand.
But however, it is better that the patient himself insert it, because of his knowledge of the location of the affected area, and push downwards, and pull his hand up with the instrument, all this according to the amount that is prepared for him until it is expelled.
Chapter 39.
On the extraction of a leech attached to the throat.
When a leech is medicated with what we have mentioned in the section on treatment with medicines and it does not work, then consider applying it to the patient's throat in the sun, after you have compressed the patient's tongue with the instrument which we have recommended to you.
Therefore, if you see the leech, extract it with a small hook or with delicate, appropriate movements.
And if it is not possible for you to extract it with that, then take a concave cannula and insert it into the patient's throat, and then place a heated iron in the concavity of the cannula, and do this as many times (until the leech is dead and detaches from the throat), and abstain the patient from water all day.
Then take a vial full of cold water, and open the patient's mouth into it, and wash the mouth with it; and do not swallow anything from it and constantly stir the water with your hand, because the leech, as soon as it feels the water, will fall off.
But if it is not expelled by what we have recommended, then fumigate the patient with cymene or with asafoetida, using the instrument we recommended for fumigating the uvula.
Do this, so that it will fall out.
And another method of operation in fumigating it, is that you take a pot in which there are ignited coals, and the pot is covered with a lid in the middle of which there is a hole, and the end of the instrument is placed in that hole; and then let the fumigation proceed; and let the patient put his mouth at the end of the cannula, and close his mouth so that the fumigation is not expelled until you know that the fumigation has reached the leech, because the leech will immediately fall.
And if it does not fall, then you repeat the fumigation many times, and abstain the patient from drinking, and eating salty things and garlic, and do not drink water, and thus it is necessary that with this regimen it will be expelled.
And this is the form of the instrument with which a leech is extracted from the throat when it is seen; and it is similar to forceps like this, except that there is some curvature in it: which is placed in the throat, and its end is similar to a stork's beak, which is hollow, in which there is a roughness of filing, so that, when it compresses something, it does not let go.
Chapter 40.
In which is the summary of the discourse on the appearance of abscesses and on their incision.
Abscesses are of many species, of many manners, according to what their remembrance shows, of each one in this book.
And they are diverse in perforation and incision of them by two manners, of which one is by the species of abscesses in themselves; and by what we understand of the humidities.
And the second manner is concerning the locations in which they happen in the body, because an abscess occurring in the head is different from an abscess occurring in the buttocks; and an abscess in a fleshy place is different from an abscess occurring in a joint; and to each of them is its own judgment for operation.
And moreover, of abscesses, there are some which it is necessary not to perforate except near the maturation of the pus in them or their completion.
There are other abscesses which it is necessary to pierce when they are raw, and are not matured according to their completion, just as abscesses that are near the joints, because when an abscess is near a joint, its being is prolonged until that which is in its circuit can be purified, and very quickly the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted, wherefore there is caused a contraction of that member; or it is an abscess near a principal member, and if you delay its perforation until it is matured, it will harm the principal member; or it is near the anus, therefore perforate it unmatured; and it is not necessary to perforate it raw, of uncompleted digestion, except that which is near the anus, so that its matter may not fester and be elongated to the inner parts of the anus, and a fistula be formed, or that it be done in such a way that it may not be healed. But it is necessary that you know the time of the perforation of the abscess, and this is when it is matured.
And know that it is matured when there is the abatement of the pain and the cessation of the fever, and near the diminution of the hardness and of the pulsation, and the acuteness of the head of the abscess, and the other signs.
And because it is necessary that you know how to make the perforation fall in the place lowest in the abscess and from where the healing matter comes from the prominence.
And the perforation should always be along the length of the flesh or body, if the abscesses are in the parts of the hands or feet, and in the place of the tendons, of the cords of the nerves, of the arteries; and finally, in all even places in which there is no reflection; because in those that are reflected, use perforation according to that member.
But when the abscess is in a fleshy place, then it is better that you leave the perforation of it until its digestion is confirmed according to the completion, as we have said; for if you perforate it before that, the course of its pus and much filth is prolonged, and quickly its lips are hardened and its depth increased.
But some abscesses are perforated according to the width of the flesh or body because of necessity, or according to what the member requires.
And it is necessary that you administer a small perforation in small abscesses, and in large abscesses a large and wide perforation, or many incisions, according to the diversity of the size of the abscesses.
And others are abscesses in which it is necessary that the head be hollowed and incised, when it is made like bread and is ripe, as that which happens in cases of swellings and buboes.
And those are incised with a three-angled incision; and those which are incised like the shape of a myrtle leaf, like the abscesses of the armpits.
And there are those in which a round incision and a crescent-shaped incision are administered, and similar to those.
But that which is not like the aforementioned superficial abscesses, you must perforate it with only a superficial or flat perforation.
And when the abscess is large and has collected much pus, you should perforate it and do not try to extract all the pus at once; having extracted a part of it, then pack the abscess until the next day, then extract a larger portion, and then do this many times gradually until it is all discharged.
And especially if the patient is of weak constitution, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an elderly person in decrepitude, because the animal spirit is often dissolved with the exit of the pus, and the patient dies very quickly, and you will have opened them [the abscesses].
Therefore, observe this chapter with great caution, and concerning your perforation in which you perforate these abscesses, you must clean the wounds and consider whether the wound of the abscess is small, or is a flat incision; then you should apply lint or old linen; and if the abscess is large, and the fissures of the perforation are many, then you should place lint in each opening until they come together.
And if a part of the skin is cut off in the abscess, or you have hollowed it out in a circle, then you should fill it with old linen or thread made from linen cloth, as when you have extracted splinters or roots, without moisture; and bind it tightly until the third day; and then proceed with this and treat it with the appropriate ointments until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs during your operation, then apply cold water and sour wine in which you have soaked a linen cloth, and apply this to the site of the bleeding many times.
But if the bleeding persists, then you must apply the regimen and treatment and powders which I have recounted in many places in this our book and division [or section].
But if it is wintertime, and the location of the abscess is a place of many nerves, then you should mix powders in warm wine and oil, and apply these to the area.
And if it is summer, and the areas are fleshy, then you should apply powders soaked in water and oil or in wine and oil, and all this should remain cold until the third day, as we have said, and then you should open the abscess and clean it and administer to its treatment what is appropriate for it until it is healed.
And this is what is necessary for the knowledge of the treatment of abscesses universally; but according to the way of distinction, I have already spoken of each abscess and how to treat it in separate chapters.
Chapter 41.
Concerning the incision of abscesses which occur in the back of the head.
In the back of the head, small abscesses are found, a type of node which contains membranes on their surfaces; and their appearance is varied.
And they are purulent, and those contain a humidity similar to slime. And some of them contain a humidity similar to pus, and resemble it; and some are stony, hard.
And in the incision of all, and in the extraction of them, there is no fear, since the extraction of them does not cause contrary effects.
And the operation in the incision of these is that you probe them with an instrument whose shape is similar to this, which is called a probe, until you know what it contains.
For if what it contains is humidity, then incise it lengthwise with a superficial incision according to this form:
And begin the incision from line B to line G.  Then, when the humidity is evacuated, you should scrape the area that contained that humidity, and do not leave any of it at all, for it often returns if any of it remains.
Then immerse a swab in Egyptian ointment, if it is available, and if not, in salt water; and fill the wound with it and leave it there until the next day, for this will corrode everything that is rotten from that membrane.
And then repeat applying the swab with Egyptian ointment a second and third time if needed, until you are confident that there is no rotten part of that membrane remaining; then treat the wound with ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess contains fatty nodules, then you should incise it in this manner: with an incision in the form of a cross, and insert forceps between the two lips of the incision and scrape it from all parts, and exert your strength in extracting the membrane which contains it.
But if this procedure proves contrary to your expectations, then do what I recounted to you at first.
And similarly proceed with a stony abscess in its incision and treatment as we have told you; and the incision on a stony abscess is easier, because it has little blood and little moisture.
And sometimes some of these abscesses, which are in the head in some men, have absolutely no moisture; the reason is that I incised an abscess on the head of an old woman and found the abscess like a dull, rough, white stone, which could not be crushed, and if someone were struck with it, it would hurt them.
But in other accidental abscesses in the head other than this, this is not the chapter, such as the abscesses that occur on the heads of children and behind the roots of the ears; however, the incision of all of these should be a superficial incision.
And make the perforation of those in the lowest part of them so that the body is relieved of the pus in the lowest parts.
And then treat those with what is appropriate for that treatment.
Chapter 42.
Chapter 42.  On the incision of scrophulas which occur in the neck.
Often these abscesses occur in the neck and armpits and in the groin; and sometimes they occur in other parts of the body.
And those which occur in the neck, many are generated amongst themselves, and each scrofula of these is within its own proper sheath, and just as in the nodes and abscesses of the head, as we have recounted.
And the types of these scrofulas are many, for some of them are stony, and some of them contain humidity, and some of them are malignant which do not obey treatment.
Therefore, in that which you see to be of good disposition on touching, of which the color that is manifest is close to the color of the flesh, and is soft in every part, and is not connected to the nerves of the neck, nor to the veins, nor to the artery, and is not deep, it is necessary that you incise it with a superficial incision from the upper part to the lower part of the body, from the line G to the line B, and scrape it from every part, and extend the lips of the flesh with hooks, or with many hooks if you have need of them, as we have said in the abscesses of the head, and extract it little by little.
And it is most important that the vein or nerve not be incised.
And let the instrument not be too sharp, so that it does not hurt your hand with the incision, or that it disturbs the patient, and that you incise only what is necessary to incise; for if you incise a vein or artery and are forbidden from your operation, then place on the wound crushed vitriol or other powders which stop the flow of blood, and bind the wound, and leave it until the acuteness of the abscess subsides and the wound is softened and begins to be suppurated. When the blood is cut off.
Then return to your operation until you have completed it.
And then inquire with your finger if there is any cause of other small scrofulas, then incise those and clean them.
And if there is a large vein at the root of the scrofula, then it is necessary that you not incise that scrofula at its root. Beforehand, it is necessary that you tie it with strong doubled thread and that you leave it until it falls off by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old linen which you have already submerged in Egyptian ointment, and then cure that.
And if you incise the entire scrofula, then it is necessary that you join the two lips of the wound and stitch it as soon as there is no remaining superfluity.
But if you see that there remains superfluity because of the size of the scrofula, it is necessary that you understand how to incise the root of it, and extract it and administer suture and what has been said.
And that of the scrofulas which contains humidity, perforate it with a superficial perforation where the place of its maturation appears to you, and place the perforation so that it is the lowest part of the body, as we have said.
Then administer a perforation, perhaps with ointment and similar things, so that it corrodes what remains of the corruption. And then, when you see the wound already cleansed, then you cure it with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
Chapter 43.
Concerning the incision of the boil or abscess occurring within the throat.
The ancients described this incision in the boil, and no one in our land has been seen to have performed it.
And this is the description/writing of the speech/discourse of those (ancients).
But in men having a hernia, the extraction of the boil should be avoided, lest harm be done, because all the veins and major blood vessels of the patient are there.
But in those who have abscesses located in the mouth or throat or ears, when the disease is not in the [throat] pipe/windpipe, it is necessary to use the incision of the boil to avoid the damage which is caused by perforation.
Therefore, when we want to do this, we should incise the boil below three circles of the circles of the boil, or four, with a small incision in width in the area between two circles; to a degree that the incision is in the fatty tissue, not in the cartilage.  And this location is suitable for incision, because it is free of flesh and its blood vessels are far away.
And if the physician is timid/fearful, then it is necessary that he extend the [muscular] sheath of the throat with an instrument (oncium), and then cut the sheath until he reaches the pipe/canal, but he should avoid the blood vessels if he sees any.
And then incise the fatty tissue as we have recommended; and the incision of the pipe/canal is indicated by the inflammation which has emerged from it along with what has burst from it and by the loss of voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.
Therefore, when the time has arrived in which perforation is feared, bring together the two lips of the wound's sheath, and sew that alone without the cartilage.
And then administer medicines that promote the growth of flesh until it is healed.
And Albucasis says that the summary of the discourse which we have recounted, is an explanation only that you may see that some of these abscesses have already obstructed the gullet of the patient, and the patient has come close to death, and their throat is obstructed, and some go to incise the epiglottis so that the patient may breathe through the place of the wound for some respiration, and be saved from death.
And for this reason I order the wound to be left open until the malice of the illness is consumed; and its malice lasts for three days; and then I order the wound to be sewn, and cure it until it is healed.
And this which I have seen with my own eyes, is that a servant took a knife and put it in her throat and incised with it a part of the windpipe, and I was called to her cure, and I found the throat as it is swollen by a bite, and I uncovered the wound and I found blood which had issued from the small wound.
Then I was confessed that she had not incised a vein or artery, and wind was issuing from the wound, and I cured it until it was healed.
And nothing happened to the servant except hoarseness in the voice, and after a few days she returned to better disposition.
And now I say that in the section of the epilepsy there is no fear.
Chapter 44.
Chapter 44. Of an incision on an abscess which occurs in the throat externally, and is called a throat hernia.
This abscess called throat hernia is a large abscess according to the body's complexion, and it is common in women.
And it is of two kinds, for it is either natural or accidental.
And the natural one cannot be cured.
And the accidental one is of two kinds, of which one resembles fatty nodes, and the other kind resembles an abscess which is formed by the knotting of an artery, and in this there is danger. And one should not be presumptuous with iron except in the case of a small one. (referring to the abscess) And you probe it and investigate it with your finger, and if you find it similar to a fatty node, and it is not connected to any of the veins, then you incise it as with nodes, and extract it with whatever is contained within it from the skin and the blister; and if not, then its whole being should be examined, and treat the place with whatever is necessary for healing until it is healed.
Chapter 45.
On the incision of the types of nodes.
There are many types of nodes, and I have already mentioned all their types in the division (of chapters).
And it is necessary that I recount thus the difference between a node and an abscess, lest there be doubt.
Therefore I say that with an abscess there is heat and fever and vehement pain until the boiling of the superfluity is settled; and putrefaction is complete, then the fever, pain, and heat cease.
But with a node there is neither heat nor fever nor pain, and it contains that lymphatic humor which is in its own sac.
And it is according to the color of the body, and its beginning is like a swelling and is formed like a vesicle, either larger or smaller, and they are of two types, for they are either fatty and contain humidity, and the colors of the humidity are many, as I said in the division (of chapters).
Therefore, when you arrive at the treatment of a node, you must remove that and investigate that at the beginning with an instrument which is called a probe, according to how its form will appear in the chapter which is near this one.
And the manner of investigating abscesses and all nodes is that you take this instrument and insert it into the most humid place which you find in the abscess, and you will revolve your fingers with it little by little until you know that the instrument has already penetrated the pus.
Then place your hand around the abscess according to its size, and then remove the probe and consider what has come out in its wake.
And if the flowing humidity, whatever its color, is expelled, then you should incise it with a superficial incision as I have said in the case of other abscesses.
And if you find no humidity in the wake of the probe, then know that it is a tumor (or swelling).
And incise it with a cross-shaped incision in this manner: as I have shown you, and suspend it with hooks, and easily scrape the skin from all sides, and keep the skin or sac, if you can, over it and extract the pus with a knot.
But if it breaks during the operation, it is not possible to extract all the pus, and this often happens; and then you should extract it in pieces until nothing remains.
Because if anything remains, little or much, the swelling will return all the more.
And if you see that a small part remains, then fill the wound with some sharp corrosive powders and bind the wound tightly, and apply a hot poultice over it, and treat it with another cure until it is healed.
And if it is a large swelling, then you should sew the two lips of it together and treat it so that it grows flesh.
But if a pulsating vein is opposed to it, or a non-pulsating one, then you should consider and fill the place with crushed vitriol and leave it tightly bandaged for two or three days until the wound dries and the boiling of the blood subsides; and then return to incise what remains of the swelling.

Chapter 47.
Concerning the care of men's breasts which resemble those of women.
Sometimes the breasts of some men become inflamed due to the habit of sleeping, and then the breasts become inflamed, and resemble the breasts of women, and remain inflamed and ulcerated.
And for one who has this, it is necessary that you incise it with a lunar incision according to this form, from line B to line G, and then scrape away the entire thickness, and then fill the wound with incarnative medicine, and then approximate the two lips of the wound with suture, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the breast droops downwards and is softened by its size, as happens in women, then let it be incised in its two upper parts with two incisions similar to a lunar figure, each of which is contained within the other towards the end of each, so that the larger line contains the smaller according to this form, from line B and line G. And then scrape the skin which is between the two lips of the wound, and remove the fat, and administer what we have said about the suture and the powders, that which is necessary in the treatment until it is healed.
And if you do not complete the incision of what needs to be incised because of the patient's unease, and because of the flow of blood, then you must fill the wound with cotton soaked in corrosive ointment and leave it until it corrodes what remains of the fat, and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
Of the perforation of abscesses that appear under the armpit.
These abscesses that appear under the armpit are a kind of scrofula, hard, and containing a sac-like skin.
And some of these contain humidity; and those that contain humidity should be incised with a lunate incision according to this figure: from line G to line B. And when all the pus in the abscess has been evacuated, then you fill it with old linen and leave it until the next day.
Then the linen should be removed and it should be treated with a suitable ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess is of the kind of nodes and scrofula, then you incise it as we have said in the section on scrofula.
But if the wound is so extensive that it begins to fester, then apply cautery as we have previously described.
Chapter 49.
Concerning the incision of abscesses that occur from an arrow wound or a vein.
When an arrow is broken and the shaft that remains is consolidated, then often an abscess occurs. And the signs by which it is known that inflammation and abscess are from an artery or vein are these:  because when the abscess is from an arrow wound it is long and aggregated in the front of the body; and when you press the abscess with your fingers, you feel a creaking sound within it.
And that which is from a vein is round on the surface of the body.
And incision of this abscess is harmful, especially that which is under the armpits, and in the groin, and in the neck, and in many places of the body; and it is very large, therefore its treatment with iron should be avoided.  And that which is in the extremities, or in the head, should be avoided.
But concerning that which is from them [abscesses] by inflammation of the opening of the arrow wound, make a lengthwise incision in the shaft; and then open the incision with hooks and remove the arrow, and free it from the tissues until it is uncovered.
And then place a needle on it and make it penetrate to the other side, and tie the arrow with double thread in two places— which method I have shown you in the extraction of two arrows which were in the temples.
And then puncture the place which is between two ligaments with a spatula until all the blood which is there comes out, and the abscess is dissolved.
And then administer treatment in which pus is generated until the ligaments fall away; and then treat it with ointments suitable for this until it is healed.
And if it is an abscess from a severed vein, then it is necessary that you hold with your hand that which is possible of the abscess with the shaft, and then insert the needle below the place which you have held under your hand, and make it penetrate; and let there be double thread on it so that you extract it from the other side, and then tie the abscess with good ligature according to what I have told you in the ligature of a grape [presumably referring to a previous example using a grape as a visual aid] in two parts with two ends of the thread.
But if you fear that you will pull out the thread, then insert the thread with that needle below all the abscess through the incision of the first needle, and tighten the threads in four places.
Then dry the abscess in the middle of it until, when that which is in it has come out, then you incise the superfluity of the shaft, and leave that which is tied from it.
And then place on it a powder which you have submerged in wine and oil, and then administer the treatment which is done with poultices and ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 1 of the abscess which happens through the twisting of nerves.
Just as abscesses occur in arteries and veins, similarly abscesses occur in the nerves when they are struck or when excessive labor occurs, and similarly.
And it is made worse in the places of the joints or of the heel, or in any place where the joints are injured.
And the abscess is serious, and it resembles the color of the other body, and is in the worst of its dispositions, and when it is squeezed with force, the patient feels a kind of numbness.
And the abscess is not located in the front of the body, and therefore it is below the ribs, and it is injured in every part and does not spread to the front or back parts.
Therefore, in those [abscesses] that are in the joints, it is not appropriate to use iron [instruments], because contraction will quickly occur.
But that which is in the head or on the forehead,  bleed it with a spatula. But if it is a small abscess, then take it with forceps and excise it from the roots.
And if it is large, then you suspend it with a hook, and flay it, and then remove it; and close the wound with sutures, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 51.
Concerning the incision of an abscess which occurs in the area that happens to be in the abdomen.
It happens to some men many times in their bellies and in other parts of their bodies, those things which are called fungi, by similarity to fungi, of which the root is subtle and the head is large; and the lips of these are convex, and some of these are small, and some of these are very large.
And I have already seen one, and in his belly were two things which were very similar to fungi, and there was no difference between them, white, with thin roots.
And the lips of these were turned inward and split, and humidity flowed from them constantly.
Therefore I incised them and found in one eighteen ounces of pus, and in the other six ounces.
And the operation in the incision of them is that you consider then if the patient is humid, and the color of the swelling is white, humid, with a subtle root; then you incise it with a broad spatula, and have cauteries in the fire in your presence, because many times from the section of it much blood is expelled; and if the blood overcomes you, cauterize it.
And if you see the patient fearful and timid, beware of incision with iron; but then take freshly made lead plaster, and bind the swelling with it, in the manner which we have described; and leave it for two days.
And then adjust the tightness of the lead thread; therefore, do not cease to tighten the lead strongly over the swelling, until it is cut off and falls by itself without effort.
But if the swelling has a thick root, then in its incision there is some cause for fear and dread, and especially that which is in the belly.
But it is possible that you incise half of it, and then cauterize it so that it does not return.
And then treat it until the wound is healed.
And beware that you do not presume to incise a swelling which is dark in color, insensitive, and of little growth, for that is probably cancerous.
And I bring to mind a crab in connection with this.
Chapter 52.
On the treatment of a protrusion from the umbilicus.
A protrusion from the umbilicus is caused by many things; either by fissures of the peritoneum which is over the belly, and from it the intestine or bowel protrudes as happens in other ruptures; or by blood which flows from a vein or artery as previously stated, or by constricted wind within it.
But if it is due to a fissure of the peritoneum and a protrusion of the intestine, then the color of the swelling is similar to the color of the body; and it is soft without pain, and appears in various locations. And if it is due to the egress of the bowel, then its location is as we have recounted, with more vehement variety, and when you press it with your fingers it is hidden, and it returns; and it is easily replaced, and is greatly increased by the introduction of a bath and strenuous work.
But if it is due to humidity, then it is soft and is not hidden when you press it with your hand, nor is it constricted, nor is it swollen.
And if it is due to blood, then the swelling appears with these signs, inclining towards blackness.
But if it is due to rotten flesh, then the swelling is heavy, large and hard, and is located according to a certain quantity/amount.
But if it is due to flatulence, then the touch of it is soft.
And the operation in it is that you consider then if the prominence of the umbilicus is due to arterial blood, or venous blood, or due to flatulence; then it is necessary that you are prohibited from the treatment of it, because in that there is fear and danger as I have taught you in the chapter in which I discussed swellings from arterial and venous blood.
And if the prominence of the umbilicus is due to the intestine or the membrane, then it is necessary that you command the patient to hold his breath, and to lie stretched out; and then mark with a probe the circumference of the whole umbilicus, and then command him to lie between your hands on his back, and then encircle with a broad spatula the circumference of the umbilicus over the place which you have marked with the probe; and then extend half of the swelling to the upper parts with an inch or so; and then bind it in the circumference with strong thread or with a bandage, and the manner of binding properly.
And then open the middle of the extended swelling above the ligature and insert your finger into that opening and seek the intestine; and if you have found it and secured it by the ligature, then you extract gently and push the intestine into the inner parts of the belly.
And if you have found the membrane, then take that with forceps; and incise its superfluities.
But if it is contrary to your [expectation of finding] artery or vein, then encircle that spot, and then return to your operation; and take two needles and put in those two strong threads and insert the two needles into the circumference which you have made around the swelling in the manner of a cross, which you should make penetrate; and then tighten the swelling in four parts above the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and leave the place until the extended flesh may dry and fall off by itself; or incise that when it is thinned and dried, and then cure that with what is necessary of ointments until it is healed.
And if the prominence of the umbilicus is of raw flesh there or from humidity, then it is necessary that you concavate the swelling as we have said and extract the blood or the humidity which you find there, and then cure that with what is necessary and that consolidates the place.
Chapter 53.
On the treatment of cancer.
We have already spoken, in the division, of the type of cancer and in what manner it is possible to cure it with medicines, and that it is avoided in it, the cure with iron, so that it may not be ulcerated; and we have said that cancer is generated in the mother and the caution of its cure.
And the ancients said that sometimes cancer is in a place where its eradication is possible, as in the case of cancer which is in the breasts or in the groin and similar members, to extract all that, and especially when it is small and beginning.
But when it is old and large, one should not meddle with it, for I have not been able to heal anyone of it, nor have I seen another who has achieved this.
And the operation on it, when it is located as we have said, is that you proceed and purge the patient of black bile sometimes, and then bleed him, if manifest repletion is found.
And then prepare the patient with a preparation in which he is positioned for the operation, and then apply to the wound ointments which are suitable for it; and then thoroughly scrape from all sides with a sharp curette until no trace of its roots remains. And let the blood flow, and do not stop it lightly, and press the area, and wipe away the thick blood completely with your hand or with any instrument that is available to you.
But if you encounter excessive bleeding from a vein incision or an artery, then cauterize it until the flow of blood ceases, and then treat it with the other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 44.  (Assuming a chapter numbering system)
On the treatment of dropsy.
We have already recounted, in the division, the types of dropsy from water, and the signs of each type, and their treatment with medicines.
And that which is cured with iron is only the species of ascites, and iron is not applied to the species of tympanites or the species of anasarca, for that is mortal.
And when you have treated this type of tympanitic dropsy with medicine and your treatment is not effective, then consider, for if the patient has already acquired debility, or has another illness along with this dropsy, such as a cough or abdominal pain and similar ailments, then be careful not to treat them with iron, for there is danger in that.
But if you see the patient with exuberant strength, and with no other illness but dropsy alone, and neither a child nor an old person, then the manner of operating on them is that you lift the patient between your hands, and the attendant gently presses their belly with their hand, driving the water towards the area below the pubic bone, and then take a sharp pointed instrument in this manner:
Let it be two-edged, long, with a sharp point like a lancet; however, let there be some shortness in it, little by little, so that in the operation with it you do not penetrate the intestine and injure it.
And then consider if the origin of the dropsy is from the intestines, for then it is necessary that you make the incision at a distance of three fingers from the navel towards the lower parts, directly above the pubic bone.
But if the origin of the dropsy is due to liver disease, then your incision should be three fingers to the left of the navel.
And if its origin is from the spleen, then your incision should be on the right.
And the incision should not be on the side on which the patient wishes to lie, lest the superfluities flow to that weak place.
And then perforate the entire sheath with the instrument, and then insert it into that incision, and raise your hand with the spatula between the sheath and the peritoneum, and you should scrape that; and if [it's] the amount of a fingernail or thereabouts.
And then perforate the peritoneum until the spatula reaches the empty space, which is the location of the water. And then extract that and insert an instrument of which this is the form: and it is similar to a fine silver cannula; it is made of silver or bronze or tin, in the lowest part of which is a small hole, and on its sides are three holes: two on one side and one on the other; and its incised extremity is shaped like a feather, thus:
And at its uppermost extremity is a ring, because when the instrument reaches the water, then that immediately settles on the instrument, therefore evacuating the water in a moderate amount, for if you evacuate more than is necessary at that time, the patient quickly dies from the dissolution of his animal spirit, or a syncope occurs which is close to death.
But evacuate according to the strength of the patient and according to what the patient's condition, pulse strength, and overall well-being indicate to you.
And then extract the instrument, and retain the water, the reason being that it is immediately retained by the sheath which covers the hole that is above the cup, which I have told you to perforate in that manner.
And then repeat the procedure with the instrument on the following day if you see the patient tolerating it, and extract a small quantity of water, and do this for a few days cautiously, and beware of error so that little water remains.
But if you fear for the patient, and leave a large quantity of water, then treat him with burial in warm sand, and induce sweating in a bath, and expose him to the sun for a long time; and treat him with desiccative medicines until he is healed.
And cauterize the stomach, the spleen, and the liver after the extraction of the water, as I have recounted in what precedes.
Chapter 55.
Of the treatment of children who are born with imperforate urinary tracts.
Sometimes a child is born from its mother's womb and the head of its penis is not perforated (or the opening is narrow or not in its proper place).
Therefore, it is necessary to treat it with perforation as soon as it is born, using a very fine instrument, according to this method:
Then place a thin lead pin in the opening and leave it there for three or four days.
And when he wants to urinate, it should be moved and he will urinate; then return it to its place.
And if you do not place the lead [pin], it is possible that the urine, which goes to that place, will not allow it to close.
But that [opening] whose hole is narrow should be treated with a lead pin, as we have said, for several days until it is widened.
But in those in whom the opening is not in its place, it is said that the cause is that for those who are born, the opening is below the end of the penis, so that they cannot urinate unless they lift the penis with their hand upwards. And it is not generative for them because the sperm cannot reach the mother in a straight line, and it is a very prolonged illness.
And the manner of the operation on him is that the patient should be placed on his back, and then extend the head of his penis with your left hand with vehement traction, and incise the head of the penis at the location of the foreskin with a sharp scalpel, like a feather incision, or that you shave some part, so that the middle of it becomes similar to the head of the penis and that the opening falls in the middle place as it should be.
And beware of the flow of blood in your operation, for this often happens; and stop the bleeding, and care for the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 56.
Concerning the albater which occurs in the prepuce at the head of the penis, and of corruption, blackness, and adhesion of the prepuce to the head of the penis.
Often this albater occurs on the penis and is a swelling of fleshy matter.
And of these, one is malignant and the other is not malignant.
For the non-malignant, you should suspend it with a fine thread and cut it off until you cleanse it entirely; then apply to that wound something dipped in Egyptian ointment; and after that, treat it with diafinicon ointment until it is healed.
And if the albater is malignant, of a dark color, then you should apply cautery near its incision and its root.
But if the albater is in the prepuce of the head of the uncircumcised penis, and one part of the albater is inside the prepuce and the other part is outside, then you should remove the albater which is inside first until it is consolidated; and then you treat the outside part, because if you treat them together, the prepuce may be damaged.
And if you see in the genitals and prepuce blackness and corruption, then you should remove all that which is already blackened and which is starting to corrupt or which is already corrupt; and then apply to that place honey, which is mixed with finely ground pomegranate rind flour and crushed and sifted herbs.
And then treat it with the other cure until it is healed.
But if there is a flow of blood, then apply lunar cautery according to this method:
Because cautery is helpful for two conditions at once, that is, for the flow of blood and for the wound when it is corrupt.
And if the head of the penis is corroded and completely destroyed, then you must insert a lead cannula into the meatus of the prepuce so that the patient can urinate through it.
In the adhesion of the prepuce to the head of the penis (and this adhesion does not occur except in one whose prepuce is healthy, and circumcision is not required; and when adhesion occurs due to a wound or abscess), it is necessary that you scrape with a blunt spatula until the bond is dissolved and the head of the penis is freed from all parts.
And if it is difficult to separate these exquisitely, then it is necessary that you scrape with a spatula some part of the head of the penis and of the prepuce, and this is because the prepuce, when it is thin, is very easily perforated by its slight thinness.
Then separate the prepuce from the head of the penis with a thin linen cloth, which has already been moistened in cold water so that they do not adhere again.
And then let it be treated with styptic wine until it is consolidated (healed).
Chapter 57.
Of the cleansing of children and the treatment of what happens to those of circumcision.
Circumcision is not a solution of continuity like other wounds; however, since it is of our operation and our will, and since it is properly done on infants, it is necessary that we assign to it the best operation and the easiest method leading to health.
Therefore I say that the ancients did not discuss circumcision in any of their books, because they did not use it in their laws, and it is only because we have acquired it through experience.  The reason is that I have found most artisans and barbers use circumcision and cleansing with a lancet and with forceps; and they use ligation with thread and incision with the nail.
And I have tried all these methods and I find no better cleansing with forceps, with ligation with thread, than cleansing with the nail, because many times the upper skin escapes, because the foreskin has two layers, and the lower layer remains, and it is easily forced to another incision and certain pain. And with cleansing which is done with a scalpel, and is digital, which shoemakers use, the incision of the tip of the penis is not ensured, because it easily enters the hole of it.
But in the operation with a nail, it easily becomes rusty and corrupts your operation. And the foreskin of a child is short by nature, and many times it is born in such a way that it has no need of cleansing, and I have seen this.
But of the cleansing that is done with forceps and ligatures with thread, the goodness, experience has shown me, because the forceps are of proportional incision, for that reason because the sharpness which is above is like the sharpness which is below; and therefore when you press your hand, two sharpnesses are proportioned, and you incise according to a comparison at one time; and a compression of the thread is made with a shape containing the foreskin of the penis from all sides, with which no error occurs at all.
And the method of the first operation is that you should make the child, especially if it is one of those who understand a little, stay still, so that you only tie the thread on its penis, and leave it there until the next day.
And then do that in every way possible, according to what it can understand. And then hold the child steadily between your hands, and let it not be moving around; and hide the forceps in your hand or under your foot, so that the child does not see them or any other instruments.
And then place your hand on his penis and blow on the foreskin, and pull it back until the head of the penis is exposed, and then cleanse it of any dirt that is attached to it.
The shape of the forceps for circumcision, this is the shape:
Let some of the extremities be non-cutting and the others be cutting, so that the claw's branch does not protrude, and let the length of the two points be equal to the length of two handles equally.
But the error in circumcision is that you very easily remove the entire inner foreskin, or part of it, below the incision.
Therefore it is necessary that you extend that of your hour with your fingernail before the suppuration of the place and that you incise it according to equality.
And if you cannot hold it with your fingernail, then extract it with tweezers and incise it.
But if three days have passed, and what is swollen remains under the suppurating penis, then you leave it until the hot abscess subsides, and gently scrape it, and incise it as you see fit.
And guard the head of the penis, for that will not harm him.
And then cure it with that which consolidates the wound, from the powders which we have recommended in the treatise on powders.
And if it is incised beyond measure from the scrotum, and it obeys the upper parts, that does not harm him.
And moreover, cure it with what we have said until it is healed.
Chapter 58.
Concerning the cure of urine retained in the bladder.
Retention of urine in the bladder is caused by obstruction from a stone, or by congealed blood, or by pus, or by flesh, and similar to those [causes]; concerning the methods of cure, see the division [of this text, presumably a previous chapter].
And if it is not dissolved by what we have said and the cause is vehement in the patient, then it is necessary to administer extraction with an instrument which is called a syringe, of which this is the form: Let it be of silver and let it be subtly long and hollow, like a bird's quill, with a thin long shaft about a palm and a half long, which has a thin tube in its head.
And the manner of drawing the urine with this is that you take a folded cloth and tie wool or cotton to its end with a good binding; and insert the ends of the cloth into the part below the syringe, and cut with scissors if there is any superfluous wool, but insert it into the tube forcefully. Then lubricate the syringe with butter and egg white.
And make the patient sit on a chair, and rub his bladder and penis with moist oils, olive oil and rose water.
Then gently insert the syringe until it reaches the root of the penis; then bend the penis upwards towards the navel; then push the syringe inside that; and then push it until it reaches the bladder, and let the patient know that it has reached the empty cavity, and that this is not done according to this order, but the meatus, in which it goes tortuously.
Then slowly extend the cloth with the wool, because the urine will soak the wool.
Then remove it and perform a urine cure, and use a syringe, do not cease doing this and use a syringe until the bladder is evacuated and the patient finds relief.
Chapter 59.
Concerning the quality of bladder irrigation with a catheter and with similar instruments which are suitable for this.
When an ulceration or abscess occurs in the bladder, or congealed blood within it, or putrefaction is constricted there, and you wish to instill waters and medicines into it, then it should be done with an instrument which is called a catheter, and this is its form:
but at its extremity it is blunt, that is, closed. And in it are three holes: two on one side, thus far.
In the beginning is a concave place, in which is that which is pushed, and let it be according to the quantity that fills that, without addition, until when any cause is drawn from the humidities by it, is drawn, and when it is expelled, by this it should be drawn, and when it is expelled long, as the catheter does with which naphtha is poured in sea battles.
Therefore, when you wish to draw out the humidities in the bladder, insert the extremity of the catheter into the humidity, and draw with it that which is pushed to the upper parts, because the humidity is drawn into the concavity of the catheter.
And then insert the tip of the catheter into the urethra as we have recounted in the syringe; and then expel the humidity with that which you expel, for that humidity immediately reaches the bladder, and the patient feels that.
Form of the fine catheter with which the bladder is catheterized, and this is the form:
And let it be made of silver or tin, of which the highest end should resemble a small feather, and below it a smooth canal.
And then take a goat's bladder, and place in it the humidity that you wish to inject into the bladder, and then tie it between two [points] with a strong ligature with a double thread, and heat that humidity over the fire.
And then insert the tip of the catheter into the penis (or urethra).
And then squeeze your hand on the bladder with the liquid with gentle pressure until the patient says that the liquid has reached the bladder.
But if you do not have a bladder, then take a piece of parchment and make a circle from it; and perforate it throughout its circumference; and then insert strong thread into those holes, in such a way that it is interwoven; and then attach the circle such that it is firmly attached, so that you place in it what you want of the humidities, and oils and waters.
And then you tie that to the instrument and do with that as you have done with the bladder, and express with your hand until the humidity reaches the bladder.
Chapter 60.
Of the extraction of a stone.
We have already discussed in the division the types of stone and their treatment with medicines, and the difference between a stone generated in the kidneys and a stone generated in the bladder, and the signs of it.
And we have mentioned the stone in which [one performs] an operation with the hand, and incision.
And the stone generated in the bladder properly is that which is lodged in the bladder.
And I am narrating that with clear exposition and brevity.
Therefore I say that the stone generated in the bladder mostly affects children; and among its signs is that the urine that is expelled from the bladder is similar to water in its thinness, and sand appears in it; and the patient rubs his genitals, and often cries, and is restless; and with this, pus comes out of the anus in many of them.
And the healing of children from this is easier until they have reached fourteen years; and it is difficult to cure in the elderly. But in young people it is moderate because it is between those [extremes -  referring to easier healing in children and difficult healing in the elderly].
And of that [patient] in whom the stone is larger, the cure is easier; and if it is small, it is contrary to that [i.e., the cure is harder].
Therefore, to arrive at the cure of this, it is necessary at the beginning that we purify the patient with a clyster which removes all the waste that is in his bowels; for this waste sometimes prevents the discovery of the stone through its examination.
And then let the patient be taken with his feet up and be massaged and manipulated downwards so that the stone descends to the bottom of the bladder, or that it sometimes jumps out of its place.
And then make that [patient] sit between your hands, prepared, and his hands should be under his thighs, so that the whole bladder is made to incline downwards.
And then search for it [the stone] and all of it externally.
Therefore, if you feel the stone in the space, then you should immediately attend to it with an incision.
And if it does not entirely fall down under your touch, then it is necessary that you anoint with oil your index finger of your left hand, if the patient is a child, or your middle finger if he is a fully grown youth, and insert it into his anus. And search for the stone until it is under your finger. And bring it back little by little to the neck of the bladder.
Then press on it with your finger, and expel it outwards to the place where you made the incision.
And command the assistant to press the bladder with her hand; and command another assistant that with her right hand she extend the genitals upwards, and with her other hand she hold back the perineum which is below the genitals, near the place where the incision is made.
Then introduce a well-sharpened spatula, of which this is the form: and incise that which is between the anus and the genitals, not in the middle, but towards the left thigh; and let the incision be above the stone, and your finger be in the anus, and let a transverse incision be made, so that the external incision is wide and the internal narrow according to the size that the extraction of the stone allows, and no larger, for you will easily compress with your finger which is in the anus the stone through the incision, and it will be expelled without difficulty.
And know that some stones are angular and jagged, which makes their extraction difficult.
And those which resemble acorns, and are round, are easily extracted.
Therefore, if it is one which is angular and jagged, adjust it in the fissure (or incision).
And if it is not thus expelled, then you must press on it, or grasp it with a suitable instrument, the end of which is like a file with which you grasp the stone, and do not let go of it; or introduce a slender instrument with a curved end below it.
But if you cannot grasp it, then widen the opening a little, and if you see a flow of blood, staunch it with crushed vitriol.
But if there is more than one stone, then initially place your hand on the opening of the bladder, and then incise above it; and then press gently; and similarly proceed if there are several more than two.
And if the stone is very large, it is unwise to incise it with a large cut, for this will cause one of two things to the patient: either death, or continuous urinary incontinence, because the area is not completely consolidated (healed).
But administer its expulsion until it is removed, or work on breaking it up with forceps until you extract it in pieces.
Then, when you have completed your operation, fill the wound with aloe and dragon's blood, and bind it tightly. And place over it a cloth soaked in oil and wine, or in rose oil and cold water, so that a hot abscess may be calmed.
Then lay the patient supine on his back, and do not unbind the bandage until the third day.
And when you have unbound it, wash the area with water and plenty of oil, and then treat it with diafinicon ointment and diabazicon ointment until it is healed.
But if in the wound there appears a hot, burning, corrosive abscess, similar to blood frozen in the bladder, and because of it urine is prevented (and this is known by the passage of blood with urine), then insert your finger into the wound and extract that blood, for if it remains it will cause bladder corruption and putrefaction.
Then wash the wound with sour wine, water, and salt, and apply, according to its nature, whatever is suitable for its healing until it is healed.
And it is necessary during the entire treatment that you bind both thighs and adjust the medicines which are placed on the area.
And if the stone is small and is in the meatus of the penis, and is lodged there, and the flow of urine is obstructed, then treat it with what I recommended before resorting to incision, because many times for this treatment one is accused of incision; and I have proven this, and it is that you take a heated iron perforator, of which this is the form:
of triangular extremity, sharp, set in wood.
And then take bile, and bind with it the penis below the stone so that it does not return to the bladder.
And then insert the iron into the penis, perforating easily until the perforator reaches that stone. And rotate the perforator with your hand on that stone little by little, and you will enlarge the perforation until you make it penetrate from the other side; for the urine is immediately released.
And then press your hand on what remains of the stone on the outer part of the penis, because that is fragmented, and it is expelled with the urine, and the patient is healed.
But if this treatment is not suitable for you for any reason that prohibits it, then tie one bandage below the stone, and another bandage above the stone. And then make an incision on the stone in that penis between the two bandages. And then extract it. And then untie the bandages and clean the congealed blood which is formed in the wound.
And it is not necessary to have a bandage below the stone, except so that it does not return to the bladder, and the other bandage above is also there because, when the bandage is untied near the extraction of the stone, the stone returns to its place and closes the wound.
And for this reason it is necessary for you, when you tie the bandage above, that you raise the stone to the upper parts, or that it return after your completion, and the wound closes as we have said.
Chapter 61.
On the extraction of a stone from women.
Stones in women are rarely generated.
But if a stone is found in any of them, then its cure is difficult, and is forbidden in many ways.
One reason is that a woman is very easily made a virgin again.
And the second is that you will not find a woman, whether chaste or married, who willingly reveals herself to the physician.
The third is that you will not find a woman who knows this art well, especially the operation by hand.
And the fourth is that the location of the incision for a stone in women is far from the location of the stone itself, therefore requiring a deep incision, and in that there is danger.
But if necessity compels you to this, then it is necessary that you take a female physician who is well-skilled, and such are rarely found.
But if you are deprived of that, then seek a clever and subtle physician, or present a matron well-versed in women's matters, or a woman who knows some part of this art.
Therefore, have that woman present, and command her to do everything you order, from the initial examination for the stone; the reason being that if she observes whether the woman is a virgin, then she must put her finger in her rectum and search for the stone.
Then, if she finds it, carefully place her finger there, and then command her to incise it.
But if she is not a virgin, then command the matron to insert her finger into the patient's vagina and search for the stone, while placing her left hand on the bladder, and compress it with good compression.
Then, if she finds it, she must gently wash it from the opening of the bladder to the lower parts with a decoction of its virtue until it reaches the root of the thing.
And then incise it near the midpoint of the vagina, below the root of the thing, wherever it is stopped, and cut the stone in that area, and her finger should not be removed from the stone while cutting below it.
And the incision should be small at first; and then insert a probe into that incision, and if the stone is felt, then adjust the incision according to the size that you know the stone will be expelled from it.
And know that the types of stones are many: for some are small and large, and smooth and rough, and long and round, and having branches.
Therefore you should know how many types there are of them so that you can signify what you want.
And if you see a flow of blood, apply crushed vitriol to the place and leave it there for an hour until the blood is stopped, and then return to your operation until the stone is expelled.
And make sure you prepare the instruments which are mentioned in the extraction of stones in men, so that you may be aided by them in your operation.
And if you see a flow of blood, and you know that the expulsion of blood is from the artery that has been incised, then place powder on the place and bind it tightly with powders and a descending bandage, and do not repeat this; and leave the stone and do not extract it, for the patient will quickly die. And then take care of the wound.
Then, when the sharpness of the bleeding has subsided after a few days, and the place is healed, then you return to your operation until the stone is expelled.
Chapter 62.
Concerning the incision of a strangulated hernia.
A watery hernia is nothing but an accumulation of moisture in the white sheath which is below the tunic of the genitals, containing the testicle, and is called sheath.
And sometimes it is in the proper tunic which nature has prepared for it in the region of the testicle, so that it is believed that it is another testicle; and it is the tunic of the genital between the white sheath which we have mentioned, and this is nothing but tartar.
And the generation of this hernia is due to weakness which affects the genitals, so that this matter is poured into them, and sometimes it occurs due to a blow inflicted on the genitals.
And this moisture has many colors, for its color is either tending towards yellow or bloody, or reddish, or black and feculent, or white and watery, and this is most common.
And the signs by which it is known where this accumulation of water is, are these: for if it is in the white sheath which we have mentioned, then the abscess is round, tending slightly towards length, like the shape of an egg, and surrounds the testicle, for the moisture contains it on all sides.
And if the moisture is in the proper tunic, then the abscess is round near the testicle and therefore one believes it is another testicle.
And if the moisture is between the sheath of the genitals and the white sheath, then it falls below the feeling/sensation.
And as you want to know the color of the moisture, then you examine the color of the moisture in the abscess with a lancet, the form of which precedes; and according to what has emerged in the trace of the lancet, judge what is inside it.
Therefore, when we come to its treatment with iron, then it is necessary that we order the patient to be phlebotomized if that is possible for you, and you see his body full.
And then let him be supine on his back on something a little raised, and place many cloths under him.
And then sit you on his left side, and order the assistant to follow on his right side, who shall extend his finger to one of two sides, to the sheath of the genitals and to the part of the navel of the belly.
And then take a broad spatula and split the sheath of the genitals in the middle lengthwise almost to the pubic bone.
And let the incision be made straight and equally distant from the line that divides the skin of the genitals, until it reaches the included white tunic.
And scrape that, and observe, and beware that you do not incise that; and your scraping with which you scrape it, let it be suspended, however, with the utmost care and subtlety, scraping according to the quantity possible for you.
And then perforate the water-filled tunic with a wide perforation, and extract all the water.
And then separate the two lips of the incision with hooks, and extend the tunic to the upper parts; and do not touch the sheath of the genitals that it encloses, and incise the tunic in whatever way is possible for you. And incise it in its entirety or in pieces, and especially its thin layer, for if you do not meticulously examine its incision, there is no certainty that the water will not return.
But if the egg protrudes from its sheath during your operation, then when you have completed the incision of the tunic, return it inside, and then join the two lips of the sheath of the genitals with a suture, and then treat it like other wounds until it is healed.
And if you find the egg corrupted by another illness, then you should tie the vessels which are on the suspensory ligament, and extract the egg, and then treat it as we have said.
But if the water is collected in two parts together, then know that there are two hernias; therefore incise that part as you have done in the other equally; and if it seems to you that it is one operation, then you do it.
And then you wipe away the blood, and introduce into the incisions wool submerged in oil or rose oil; and place outside other wool which you have submerged in wine and oil, and spread this over the genitals and the pubic area; and place over it folded cloths and powders, and bind these on top with a bandage which is according to this form:
And then let two cloths be taken and filled with carded wool in this manner; and let the ends be made with which it is bound of cotton or wet wool, and let them be attached with a ligature in the waistband of the trousers, and one of the ends should go from the buttocks inwards to the front, and the second should go to the groin area, and two ends over the two thighs and two other ends over the two knees, and the whole ligature should be fastened in the waistband of the trousers.
And sometimes this hernia is treated artificially with cautery instead of incision with an iron; and it is that you take a thin, knife-like cautery and that you incise with it the skin of the genitals, and it is heated as we have recounted until the white cyst containing the water is discovered, and then you take a cautery of this form: and it is similar to the Greek letter.
And then pierce the cyst with it, and heat it until all the moisture is expelled.
And then stretch the cyst with tweezers, and scrape with tweezers, and scrape with a thin, heated cautery; and incise it as much as you can until you eradicate all of it; and be careful that the fire does not touch the egg.
And know that this operation is safer and less prone to blood loss, and better than operation with incision.
But if an abscess develops contrary to expectation during the incision or cauterization, or there is bleeding, then you must cure whatever is opposing you because of this, with the treatment which is appropriate for it, as stated.
But if the patient is fearful and does not expect a second operation because of its unpleasantness, then administer a perforation as I recommend here; and this is when you are certain, from what we have said about the signs, that the hernia is watery.
Then you should make the patient sit on some elevated seat, then pierce the abscess in its lowest part with a wide spatula, and make the perforation according to the length of the body.
And let the opening be wide enough so that all the water flows out.
And then place a cotton cloth over the area and bind it, and leave it so that the remaining water drains. And then treat it until the wound is healed.
But if there is resistance to the outflow of water at that time, you should perforate it: and this is done only because the white panniculus in the orifice of the wound creates resistance and prevents the exit of the water, then you should put into the wound a tool with which water is extracted from dropsies, the form of which has been previously described; or apply a small incision to the wound.
And know that the water returns, and it is collected again after about six months or thereabouts; and when it is collected, then it should be perforated in the same manner, for the patient will thus pass their days.
Chapter 63.
Concerning the incision of a fleshy hernia and its cure.
Know that in the incision of this hernia there is a danger leading to death in the most favorable conditions; therefore, in my view, its removal and being saved by that [method] is better.
And I am remembering the operation in that [hernia] and the types of abscesses.
Therefore, I say that abscesses originate in the genitals which are comprised of tissues and this is caused by many things: either by an acute, acrid superfluity which is poured into the genitals, or by a blow.
And the color of the abscess follows the color of the body, and no pain is found in it, and it is a serious abscess.
And very quickly it is a calcified abscess, of which the color is dark, and there is no feeling in it.
And sometimes it is caused by a knotting of the arteries, and by an inflammation of them as it is perceived in the memory of it, or by an inflammation of the veins.
But that which is from inflammation of the arteries is known by the dispersion of the abscess when we compress it with our fingers; and it should not be touched.
But that which is caused by inflammation of a vein is not dispersed by any means through compression with the fingers.
