And it is possible that an incision is made on this area and on the other flesh; and it is that you incise the skin of the genitals, and then extend the testicle upwards, and extract it from the white tunic, and free the suspensory ligament, and ligate it, and incise the suspensory ligament so that you free it from all parts of the testicle.
Therefore, if the testicle is already consolidated with those inflamed tissues, then you must extract the testicle and incise it.
And if there is adhesion between any part of the tunic, or between that which is between the vessels, then you must free all that adhesion and incise it with a circular incision.
But if there is a growth of flesh at the site of the adhesion which is behind, then you must incise all that which is there, and extract the testicle as we have said.
Then, when your operation is complete, fill the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and wine; and then treat it with other treatments until it is healed.
Chapter 64.
Concerning the treatment of a hernia that is of the intestine.
A vitelline abscess is an inflammation enveloped by some membrane similar to a boil, with softening of the testicles, and causes difficulty with exercise and walking for the patient.
And in the treatment of this illness, it is necessary to fear that which precedes it. (or:  And in the treatment of this illness, fear is due to the condition that precedes it.)
However, it is necessary that we describe the operation in that case, as the first physicians did.
And I say that you must have the patient sit on a raised seat; and then gently pull the suspensory ligament of the testicles downwards; and then hold the scrotum with your fingers and with the vessels that are proved to be in the vein; and have another assistant hold those, and extend them with strong extension.
And then incise with a sharp, broad scalpel with a transverse incision directly over the vessels until the vessels are exposed.
And then scrape away all the area as we have told you in the extraction of arrowheads which are in the temples.
And then insert a needle with double thread into those, and tie it in the first of the places where you incised, and tie it again in the back of them. And then incise those in the middle place with a straight incision according to the length of the body, and extract what is gathered from the fetid and corrupt humors.
And then treat the wound with the treatment of other wounds in which you wish healing to be engendered, so that the vessels which nourish one of the two genitals may fall, for that is not harmful.
But if you incise all the vessels, then it is necessary that the testicles be removed or that the testicles be tied off with the vessels, so that they are deprived of all nourishment by the incision of the vessels, and that they atrophy, or that they be tied off so that there is no nourishment to them.
Chapter 65.
Concerning the treatment of intestinal hernia.
The occurrence of this hernia is due to a rupture that happens in the peritoneum extended over the abdomen, from the inguinal region of the abdominal wall, causing the intestines to protrude from that rupture.
And this rupture is either of the peritoneum or an extension of it, and these two types occur for many reasons:  impact, jumping, shouting, lifting something heavy, or similar.
And the sign of this, when it is due to an extension of the peritoneum, is that it happens gradually over a long time, and not suddenly, and it is an inguinal abscess near the pubis because the peritoneum compresses the intestine.
And the sign of it, when it is due to a rupture of the peritoneum, is that at the beginning it causes sudden, severe pain; and it is a different kind of abscess appearing below the pubic bone, and this is because of the protrusion of the intestine, and its body outside the parts of the peritoneum.
And sometimes the omentum is expelled along with the intestine, and this hernia is called an intestinal zirbal hernia.  (Note: "zirbal" appears to be a variant or perhaps a misspelling of a term related to the omentum's involvement.)
And sometimes it is caused by flatulence, and sometimes a tear occurs in the intestine, and it is retained there, and this is a great danger for the patient, and sometimes it causes severe pain, especially when pressed.
And in the treatment of this type of illness with iron (cautery) there is danger, therefore the cauterization should be avoided in this case.
And the manner of operating on it is that you instruct the patient to return the intestine with his hand to its cavity if it obeys its return, and then it should be pressed on its neck between your hands, and raise his legs.
And then hold the cord which is the spermatic cord above the scrotum, and incise the spermatic cord lengthwise; and then place hooks in the two lips of the incision, as you need to keep the incision open with them.
And if the incision, as much as you can, allows the stone to be expelled.
And then scrape away the skin which is under the spermatic cord, until the hard white tunic is exposed on each side.
Then you insert your finger into what is the testicle, into that which is between the white tunic, which is under the spermatic cord, and between the second tunic, and break up with it the adhesion that is on the back part of the testicle.
And then gently hold the spermatic cord from the outside with your hand, and with this extend the white tunic upwards with your left hand, and elevate the testicle with the tunic to the edge of the incision; and tell the assistant to hold the testicle upwards while you dissolve the adhesion behind with complete resolution.
And search with your fingers that there is no bowel enveloped in the hard white tunic. But if you find any such thing, then you push it back down into the abdomen.
And then make an incision in the area below the spermatic cord, through which section blood and pus will flow, as we have recounted in what we have said.
And then apply wool soaked in oil and place it on the wound; and apply the ligature which we have described (and sometimes the white tunic is cauterized, which we mentioned to prevent bleeding at the incision, because this often happens) and leave the ligature until it falls off by itself.
But if its falling off is delayed, then let it be bathed with warm water, and keep that on it until it falls off as we have said.
And then treat the wound with the other treatment for wounds until it is healed.
Chapter 66.
Concerning a ventosity hernia (or, more accurately, a cystic swelling/hernia).
In this hernia, I have not seen anyone who has dared to treat it with iron.
And the first ones said that it is done in the same way as we have recounted in the hernia that is with pus; which is that you tie the vessels near the incision on that with gentleness; and then dry the area around the abscess with something that opens it, until the vessels collapse.
And cure the wound as we have said until it is healed.
Chapter 67.
Of the disruption that is made in the inguinal region.
Sometimes a rupture occurs in the groin as we have said, and it swells the area.
And do not allow the genitals to cause any displacement of the intestines to the genitals; and if they do, it is little; and it returns in all hours.  (A more modern interpretation: And don't let the genitals be a cause of the intestines being displaced into the genital area; if this happens, it's a minor issue, and it will return to its place repeatedly.)
But if the time with this is prolonged, a fissure is made in the inguinal canal until the intestine descends into the inguinal canal; and this happens because of the extension of the inguinal canal which is in the groin as we have said, the cause of which is that the inguinal canal is stretched, and then it is softened and the place is abscessed and swollen.
And its cure is with cautery, as the narration of it has preceded. (Meaning: And the treatment is cauterization, as described previously.)
And sometimes it is cured with iron according to this manner: and it is that the patient lies on his back between your hands, and then dry the place of the rupture of the swollen abscesses with a cut across the width according to the amount of three fingers. (Meaning:  Sometimes it is cured with a surgical tool (iron), in the following way: The patient lies supine between your hands, and then the swollen, ruptured area is dried with a three-finger-wide incision.)
And then tighten the white inguinal canal that is under the groin until it is uncovered/exposed. (Meaning: And then constrict the inguinal canal beneath the groin until it is visible.)
And then take the ray (likely referring to a surgical instrument, possibly a cautery) and place it on the prominent place of the inguinal canal, and press it towards the front of the belly.
And then sew the two prominent places on the end of the inguinal canal's ray, and join one to the other with a suture. (Meaning: And then stitch together the two prominent areas at the end of the inguinal canal using a suture.)
And then extract the end of the ray (instrument), and do not incise the inguinal canal completely, nor extend the wound, nor any other thing as I will let you know in the treatment of intestinal hernia. (Meaning:  Then remove the instrument; do not completely cut the inguinal canal, nor enlarge the wound, nor do anything else as I will explain in treating intestinal hernias.)
And then treat it with the treatment for wounds, and as you would incising the intestines, clean it and restore the wound until it is healed; because the inguinal canal is constricted and the prominence of the rupture is not aligned/reduced. (Meaning: Treat this like any other wound, and as you would when treating an intestinal incision; cleanse and repair it until healed, because the inguinal canal is tight and the hernia is not properly reduced).
And cautery is better in this place, because it is closer to health/healing. (Meaning: Cauterization is preferable here, as it promotes healing.)
Chapter 68.
Concerning the softening of the swelling of the testicles.
Many times the swelling of the testicles in some men is softened because the flesh that is between them is softened, and its appearance is foul. (Meaning:  Often, testicular swelling softens because the tissue between the testicles softens, and the appearance is unsightly/infected.)
Then it is necessary for the one who is charged with his cure to make the patient lie on his back, and incise all the swelling which is softened above the natural swelling; and then that he join the two lips with suture. (Meaning: The physician must position the patient supine, incise the softened area of swelling, and then suture the wound.)
And if you wish, sew up in the beginning the superfluity of the softened swelling with three or four stitches and with firm suture. (Meaning: Optionally, begin by removing excess softened tissue with 3-4 strong sutures.)
And then incise between the sutures; and then treat it with the treatment of other wounds until it is healed and the threads fall out. (Meaning:  Incise between the sutures, and then treat the wound as other wounds until healed and the stitches can be removed.)
Chapter 69.
On castration.
Know that castration is forbidden in our law, but because of that it is read to me that I remember it in this book; and its remembrance is not necessary except in two ways, of which one is so that this is in the knowledge of the physician when he is questioned about it, that he may know the treatment of that to which it happens; and the other way is that many times we have need of the castration of other animals for our benefit from them, such as of sheep, and of goats, and of pigs, and of similar animals.
And therefore I say that castration is done in two ways: either by abrasion, or by incision.
But in that which is done by abrasion, the way of the operation is that we cause the animal to be bathed in warm water until its genitals are softened and relaxed and hang down. Then grasp them with your hand until they are resolved and do not appear below the touch.
But in castration done by incision, it is necessary that you hold the animal and press the scrotum of its testicles with your left hand.
Then tie the suspensory ligament, and incise over each testicle with a cut until the two testicles are exposed.
Then incise those [testicles] after you have flayed each one. And do not place any kind of bandage on them except a fine bandage which is placed over the vessels.
And this manner of castration is better which is done by crushing, because strong crushing leaves no part of the testicles behind, so that the animal desires no more mating.
Then care for the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 70.
Concerning the treatment of hermaphroditism.
Hermaphroditism in men is according to two manners: one is when there appears in that which should be the space of the testicles, between the two testicles, a female-like structure, in which there is hair.
And sometimes from that structure urine flows, which is in the area of the testicles.
But in women it is a single type: it is that the vulva, above the mons pubis, is like two testicles of a man, somewhat smaller, externally shaped like a man's prepuce and two testicles.
And the treatment is of three types: two for men and one for women. And it is necessary that you incise those constricted tissues until the compression is released, and then treat them with the treatment for other wounds.
And in the second type in men, from which urine flows which is in the area of the testicles, there is no treatment or operation for it.
Chapter 21.
Concerning swelling and prominent flesh due to causes in women.
A slight swelling is constricted due to natural causes until it is pressed and made light by observation; and sometimes it is magnified in some women so much that it is spread as in men and extends to intercourse.
Then it is necessary that you hold the excess swelling with your hand or with a bandage and cut it.
And do not overdo the incision, especially near the root, so that a flow of blood does not result.
And then cure it with the treatment for wounds until it is healed.
Moreover, a fleshy growth is flesh that grows in the mother's womb until it fills it, and quickly emerges externally resembling a tail (and because of this, some initially call this "tailed disease"); therefore, we must incise it as we incise a swelling, and cure it until it is healed.
Chapter 72.
On the treatment of sciatica.
Sciatica is when the woman's vagina is not perforated, or the opening is small. And this is either natural or accidental.
Accidental is due to a preceding illness, and that is caused either by insufficiently formed flesh or by a subtle or thick scar.
And it is formed either in the front of the mother, or on her sides, or in her upper part, or in her lower part, and it prohibits coitus, conception, and childbirth, and very quickly it prohibits menstruation.
And the diagnosis of this is made by observing the womb; if the obstruction is apparent, it is close; but if it is not apparent, then you seek it with your fingers or with a probe.
But if the obstruction is due to a thin scar, and is close to the two labia, then you incise and break it up.
And it is that you place powders on the two labia, and then place your index fingers of both hands together.
And the woman should be on her back, her thighs spread, and then spread the two labia forcefully until that thin scar is broken and the obstruction is opened.
Then take wool and soak it in oil and place it on the area, and keep it moist throughout the day so that the incised area does not consolidate (heal too quickly).
But if the scar is thick and coarse, then you must incise it with a broad spatula or with a myrtle leaf.
But if the obstruction is due to proud flesh (exuberant granulation tissue), then suspend it with an uncia (a unit of weight, implying a weight to apply tension), and incise it.
And have with you the medicines that stop the flow of blood without biting (i.e., cauterization), such as acacia, dragon's blood, and incense, mixed with egg white.
Then administer a wide lead cannula so that the wound does not close up too quickly.
And keep it there for some days and apply some dry poultice.
And then it should be treated with the other healing methods until it is healed.
And sometimes it happens in the birth of other growths, and then it is necessary to incise in this manner. But if this is due to a hard abscess, the hard abscess which is in the mother should not be treated with iron in any way.
Chapter 73.
Concerning the treatment of hemorrhoids and fistulas and red swellings that occur in the vulva of women.
Hemorrhoids are a swelling of the openings of many veins from which much blood continuously flows; and when hemorrhoids are old, fistulas are formed.
And already I have mentioned in the division the types of these and their signs, and I will say thus which ones receive a cure and which ones do not receive a cure.
Therefore I say that hemorrhoids and fistulas, when they are in the womb of the mother and do not appear externally, then in those cases no treatment should be done with iron (i.e., cautery or surgical instruments).
But those which are at the orifice of the womb, upon which one feels (them), are those that are cured (treatable).
And it is necessary that you place the woman in a cool place and then extend the fistula with forceps, or with a rough cloth, and incise them at the roots.
And then sprinkle on those (wounds) to stem the flow of blood one of the incisive powders without biting (i.e., causing further irritation), such as acacia and similar things.
And then bathe her feet for an hour, and then place her in cold water.
And if the flow of blood persists, then make her sit in a decoction of sumac and pomegranate bark and weld and similar things.
And then take barley flour and mix it with honey and sour wine and make a plaster with this and apply it to her back.
And if the blood is stopped, it is good; and if not, apply two cupping glasses, to the breasts without scarification.
And then take wool and soak it in coriander juice or in houseleek juice or in rue juice with old wine and apply it to the place; and then treat the place with ointment until it is healed.
And treat that until it is healed.
Chapter 74.
Of the perforation of a fistula, an accident in the mother.
Many types of abscesses happen in the mother, such as cancer, and stone abscess, and ulcerations, and buboes, and herpes, and hemorrhoidal fistulas, and fissures, and boils, and other abscesses. And I have already discussed these illnesses, their types, their signs, and their cures in the divisions (chapters/sections).
And it is necessary that I speak in this treatise of a hot abscess which comes in the mother (pregnant woman) when it is among the abscesses which collect pus, and if you wish to know in what manner the operation on it is done with iron (a cautery).
Then you must consider if the pain of the abscess in its beginning is acute with throbbing and phlegm and fever, and sometimes it reddens and if it affects the feeling (sensation), then do not treat it with perforation, and cure it with that which helps with maturation until the acuteness of the symptoms ceases.
And then you must make the woman sit on a stool that has two supports; and she reclines on her side and adjusts her feet to the part just below the belly, and her thighs should be spread, and she places her arms below her knees, and bind each with a suitable bandage.
And then the midwife follows from the right side and provides the instrument with which the opening of the mother (the birth canal) is opened, the form of which will come in those matters that are done next.
And it is necessary when you wish to administer this instrument that you place with the ray (likely referring to a light source or visual guidance) the cervix of the woman's womb, and that which of the instrument enters the womb should not be more than the cervix of the womb and harms the patient. But if the instrument is larger than the cervix of the womb, then it is necessary that you place powders on the texture of the cervix of the womb so that the entry of any instrument into the cervix of the womb is prevented.
And it is necessary that you place the vertigine (likely a turning or twisting motion) which runs in the instrument from the upper side and that the attendant holds the instrument and revolves the vertigine until the opening of the mother (birth canal) is opened.
And when the incision appears, and is touched with the hand and is soft and moist, then it is necessary that you dry that area with a clean cloth. (The Latin "spatum" seems to have been interpreted as a type of cloth.)
And when the pus is evacuated, then it is necessary that you place in the wound a wick submerged in rose oil or green oil in which there is some stypticity; and place the compress on the section in the cervix of the womb.
And place outside the womb and on the vulva clean wool submerged in water in which mallows have been boiled.
And then it should be cured in about two days with an ointment which is suitable for this, until it is healed.
And sometimes the womb and the wound are washed in such a way that it is rinsed with water and honey, and then with water in which the root of lily or of birthwort has been boiled, and then returns to the ointment.
And if the wound is deep inside the concealed womb, then it is necessary that you refrain from treating it with iron, taking care of it with what we have said in the division (chapter/section).
Chapter 75.
To teach midwives in what manner living embryos should be treated when they exit in a non-natural way (i.e., miscarriage or premature birth).
It is necessary that the midwife know at the beginning the form of natural childbirth by its signs, and it is that when you see the woman strain in the lower parts and desire to smell the air, and furthermore is lightened by what is upon her of the birth, and hastens the exit of the embryo, then you know that this birth is according to the natural form, and it is the exit of the child head first, and the afterbirth with it or suspended from or hanging from the umbilicus.
Therefore, when you see those signs, then it is necessary that her belly be compressed so that the embryo descends easily because when they descend head first, the afterbirth descends with them, and is cleansed of those superfluities with good cleansing.
But that which is other than this birth is unnatural and unfavorable, for when the embryo is expelled feet first and its body is expelled before its head and feet, and one of its hands, or its foot, or its head and hand, or its belly, and very quickly it is turned on its neck, resembling unfavorable presentations.
Therefore it is necessary for the midwife to be wise and skillful in all these matters, and to guard herself from error and deception.
And I am showing all the forms of these, and what skill is needed in them so that it may be signified thereby and be established thereupon.
When the fetus is expelled head first by natural excretion, and this is strenuous for the woman and the birth is difficult, and sometimes her strength is already weakened, then have her sit, or have the women support her, and evaporate her genitals with cooked fenugreek and warm oils.
Then the midwife inserts a small, thin spatula between her labia and with it tears the secundines, or scrapes them with her fingernail until the moisture within is removed. And press the woman's belly until the fetus descends.
But if it does not descend, then the woman should be bathed with a poultice of chickpea flour and fenugreek flour with vinegar.
Then, during the bath, instruct her to be compressed and make her sneeze with a sternutatory; and hold her mouth and nose for an hour, because the fetus will be expelled immediately.
And if both arms of the fetus are expelled first, then you must gently and gradually reduce them. But if it does not obey the treatment, then place the woman on a stool and raise her legs, and then rock the stool gently on the ground; and the woman should be held to prevent her from falling during the rocking.
And if the arms are not returned and the fetus dies, then incise them and extract the fetus's remains, or tie its body to its arm with a cloth and extract it gently, for it will be expelled.
When the fetus presents feet first, then you must push them upwards, and gently turn the fetus, and gently grasp it, and gently take one foot and extend it little by little.
But when it has come out between the thighs, then you command her to strain, and make her sneeze forcefully, for it will be expelled if God wills it.
And if it is not expelled by what we have recounted, then you return the fetus little by little until you place it in its natural position, for then it will be expelled easily.
And if it is obstructed by all that we have recounted, then take marshmallow root and dissolved gum, and pound it all well in a mortar, and gently apply this to the vulva of the woman, at the lowest part of her belly. And gently make her sit in lukewarm water, up to her hypochondria (the area below the ribs).
And when you see that the lower parts of her are softened, then make a fumigation of myrtle for her and apply it to her. And when she has had the fumigation for an hour, then make her sit on a chair, and gently make her sneeze and hold her breath, and lift her up, and press the lower part of her belly with gentle compression, for the fetus will immediately be expelled.
Thus [proceed] with the fetus, holding it laterally with one of your hands. Let [the reduction] be managed with your hand; but if you cannot reduce it, then raise the woman and make her walk.
And if she cannot walk, then make her lie on the bed, and shake her thighs with a vigorous shaking, and then administer laxatives which I have recommended from mucilages.
And if childbirth has begun, then make her sit on a stool, and then work on the reduction with your hand, and grasp the fetus according to its natural form, and make the woman sneeze and command her to strain until it is expelled.
Thus [manage] the fetus on the neck, spreading your hands, and the surface of it is against the belly of its mother. Let the midwife hold the hand of the fetus and then turn it little by little, and let that be done with ease.
And when it is grasped, then place on the cervix what we have recommended of oil and mucilages.
And then instruct her to strain little by little, and when you have done that, then make her sit on a stool and instruct her to be inclined on her right side, and then instruct her to strain; and make her sneeze with a concoction [or remedy]. The prepared fetus exits on its side.
When the mother's horror subsides in this manner, consider, and then you incise the secundines with your fingernail until it is emptied of moisture.
And if the fetus is suspended over the face and neck, then you incise it at the umbilicus, so that it is not retained and dies.
And then gently push the fetus inwards until you place it as needed, and tell the woman to strain several times, and it will be expelled easily, if God wills it.
The exit of twins or multiple fetuses. Know that twins are often born, and sometimes three or four are born, but this is rare and seldom happens; but there are causes for this in nature, and they do not live.
And these, even if they are multiple, share one placenta, and are separated by a membrane, distinguishing them within the umbilical ligament of each one. And sometimes they are expelled as if it were a single fetus by easy natural expulsion, and sometimes they are expelled by difficult expulsion as we have recounted.
And if any of them are difficult to deliver during childbirth and arrive at the state of unnatural presentations we have described, then you should treat them as we have recounted.
And if the midwife is skillful and does this with ease and skill, and with patience, and is ingenious according to each presentation as it is prepared for her by her skillful bringing to health.
A recollection of that which is formed in the womb from embryos and is aborted.
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and more than ten are formed in the mother, and it has been verified by me that one woman aborted and expelled six, and another ten, all formed, which was caused according to the measurements of the glorious wise men (or learned physicians).
And all this must be within the knowledge of the midwife, because it easily appears similar to this, and therefore she should oppose it with what is necessary.
Chapter 76.
Of the extraction of the fetus when it is dead.
When you treat a dead fetus with what we have said in the division on medication and it has not been expelled, and you are forced to use a manual operation, then you must consider if the woman is of sound constitution and is not suffering from any of the accidents which threaten her with loss.
Then the woman should be positioned on the bed on her back, with her head inclined downwards and her thighs elevated.
And the assistant should hold her from both sides, or she should be tied to the bed so that her body does not extend during the extraction of the fetus.
Then you moisten the mother's vulva with humid oils and with mucilage of mallow, fenugreek, and linseed.
Then let the midwife anoint her hand with those oils and mucilages, and gently insert her hand into the mother, and carefully search with her hand for the location where the umbilical cord is attached to the fetus.
And convenient locations for this: therefore you should consider if the fetus is positioned to descend head-first, and then you should grasp the umbilical cord at its eyes, or neck, or mouth, or palate, or under the chin, or at its clavicle, or in the appropriate locations near the ribs, or under the hypochondria.
But if the descent is feet-first, then at the heel, or in the middle of the back.
And it is necessary that you hold the umbilical cord with your right hand, and place its curvature in the space between the fingers of your left hand, and insert your hand gently and place the umbilical cord in any of those locations which we have mentioned, until you reach the empty space [presumably the amniotic sac/vagina].
Then add and place in opposition to it another cord, or a third if necessary, so that the traction is equal, and not solely an extension along its straightness.
Rather, it should be pulled evenly from all sides, so that its extraction is facilitated, just as is done in the extraction of a tooth.
And it is necessary in this that the area between them [presumably the birth canal] be softened; and if any part of it [the baby] is retained, then it is necessary that the midwife lubricate some of her fingers with oil and insert them carefully and loosen what is retained with them.
Therefore, if the part of the fetus is expelled, then the cords should be moved to a higher position; and do this until the whole fetus is expelled.
But if one hand emerges before the other, and it is not possible to reduce it because of its constriction, then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped around it; and then extract it until the whole is expelled, and incise it either at the joint of the hand or at the elbow.
And thus you should proceed with the other hand and the two feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or there is constriction in its exit, or there is collected water on its head, then it is necessary to insert a sharpened spatula between the fingers and perforate the head and extract the water, or to break it with an instrument that is called an "alundat," the form of which will come with the forms of other instruments in the chapter which is near this one.
And similarly proceed if the fetus has a naturally large head: and then it is necessary to incise it with a scalpel, or to grasp it with the alundat as we have said; and then extract the bones with forceps.
And if the head is out but constricted below the clavicle, then it should be incised until the fluid which is in the chest is discharged.
But if it is not complicated, nor does it yield to extraction, then you should incise the head immediately as much as is possible for you.
And if the lower part of the abdomen is abscessed or dropsical, then it is necessary that you perforate it until the fluid that is within it runs out.
But if the fetus is on its feet, then its extraction is easy and its direction to the mother's birth canal is easy.
But if it is constricted around the abdomen and chest, then it is necessary that you extract it with a cloth which you will have wrapped around your hand, and that you incise the abdomen and chest until that which is within them flows out.
But if the other limbs are obstructed and the head is turned, then the midwife inserts her left hand; but if the mother's birth canal is open, then she inserts her hand into the womb, and searches with it for the head, and draws it with her fingers towards the mother's birth canal, and then inserts one or two fingers into it, and pulls it with them.
And if the mother's birth canal is constricted by a hot abscess which harms her, then it is necessary that it not be disturbed; rather it is necessary then to administer infusions of humid, oily substances. And administer plasters; and bathe the patient in waters that soften and moisten.
And if the fetus is on its side, then if possible, it should be turned: and that which we have said for a living fetus should be administered.
And if that is not possible, then let it be incised in pieces, and extracted.
And it is necessary that you leave no part of the afterbirth inside.
And when you have completed all your work, then administer that which we have said concerning the treatment of hot abscesses which may occur in the mother.
And if bleeding occurs, then bathe in styptic waters and administer the other treatment.
And I have seen a woman in whose womb was a dead fetus, and soon after that, she became pregnant again, and soon after that, the fetus died again. It happened to him, after a long time, that an abscess formed in his navel; and it swelled until it was opened, and it began to discharge pus.
And I was called to his care, and I treated him for a long time, and the wound did not heal.
Then I applied plasters of strong attraction to him, and I extracted a bone from him, and after a few days, I extracted another bone; and I was very surprised, since there should be no bones in the belly.
And then I considered that they were bones from the dead fetus.
Then I cleansed the wound, and extracted many bones from him, the woman being in good condition, and she lived in this way for a long time,  a small amount of pus still draining from him.
And I did not consider this strange, but because in this there is science and help so that the skillful physician administers a cure with his hand.
Of the forms of the instruments that are necessary to extract the fetus.
The form of the incision with which the mother is opened:
This is the form of a constrictor in which the [uterine] ligaments are grasped, which grasping has two incisions at the two ends of two poles/rods. However, this incision should be finer than the constrictor, and be made either of ivory or of boxwood.
And let the width of each of the said two rods be two fingers, and the thickness of each of the two be one finger, and the length of each is a palm and a half; and in the middle space between the two rods are two attachments of the same wood, which are firmly fixed, of which the length is half a palm and a little more, and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions are inserted into the mouth of the womb so that it is opened with them when you rotate the two previously described incisions/rods.
Another form similar to that one, but more subtle and lighter:
Let it be made of ivory or boxwood, in the likeness of forceps. However, at its extremity there are two additions, like branches, and the length of these additions is about a palm, and the width of each is two fingers.
And when you wish to open the womb with them, then make the woman lie on the bed with her legs hanging down, having opened what is between her thighs. Then open your hand with the instrument, just as you would with scissors, gradually, according to how much you want to open the mouth of the womb, until the midwife does what she wants.
The form of vertigo which the ancients described:
The shape of the instrument with which the fetus is expelled:
The shape of the instrument with which the large head is held back.  (The "2" likely represents a superscript or annotation in the original text, possibly indicating a variant or clarification.)
And it resembles forceps which have teeth at the end, like tongs, and sometimes it is made long like forceps in this shape, like tongs, which have teeth like saw teeth, with which it is incised and held back. The shape of a small instrument:
The shape of a hook having a spine/point.
The shape of a hook having two spines/points.
The shape of a spatula having two ends with which the head is severed/cut.
The shape of two broad spatulas.
All those instruments are exercised, and when the types of those are more multiplied and prepared by the artisans, then it is easier in its operation and more valuable among men because of its quantity/number.
And do not disregard any of those instruments which you may have prepared, for the need for them is not avoidable/inexcusable.
Of the extraction of the afterbirth.
When the afterbirth is retained after childbirth, then you should instruct the patient to hold her breath, and then make her sneeze forcefully, and press your hand over her mouth and nostrils, and if by this it comes out, it is good.
And if not, then take a pot and pierce a hole in its lid and place herbs inside it, opening the mouth of the mother; herbs such as rue, mint,  anise, chamomile, dried Arabic gum, cassia lignea, and centaury. All these herbs, or some of them, should be submerged in water in the pot, and place that over the fire.
Then place a reed cannula in the mouth of the mother, and hold it there so that the vapor reaches the mother's womb.
And then make her sneeze as we have said, and the afterbirth will be expelled quickly.
But if after this treatment it remains and is not expelled, then order the midwife to submerge her left hand in sesame oil or mallow poultice, and then insert it into the vagina and search for the afterbirth with it; and when it is found, then take it and remove it little by little. But if it is attached to the womb of the mother, then insert her hand as we have recommended, and when she has found the afterbirth, extract it little by little as instructed, so that the mother does not faint from the violent traction. Furthermore, it should be turned to the right and left sides easily.
And if the mother's mouth is constricted, we have already recommended treatment with sneezing and herbal oil medication.
But if it is not expelled by all the means we have recommended, then be careful not to tire yourself with repeated attempts at extraction; however, what has come out of her at the mouth of the mother, bind to the woman's vulva, and then cauterize it with ointment and tetrapharmacon; for that will cause it to fester in a few days and it will dissolve and be expelled.
But when it festers, a foul odor rises to the head and stomach, and this harms the patient; and then fumigations suitable for this should be administered.
But some of the Ancients have already tested fumigation with hellebore and dried figs.
Method for the instrument with which a woman is fumigated for the retention of menses and the afterbirth:
Let it be made of glass or brass.
And let the thin end be placed in the vagina and the wide end over the fire, and the fumigation placed on the embers of the fire.
And let her hold it until that fumigation is consumed and then another.
Concerning the treatment of an imperforate anus in a child.
Many children are born and their anuses are not perforated, which are thus narrow and obstructed. Then it is necessary for the midwife to perforate that stricture with her finger; and if not, perforate it with a sharp instrument, and be careful not to touch the rectum.
And then place on it wool soaked in wine and oil; and then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if you fear that it is obstructed, then place a cannula in the opening for several days, and remove it when the child wants to defecate.
And sometimes anal obstruction occurs due to the consolidation of a wound or abscess; then it is necessary that this consolidation be incised, and then it should be treated with what we have said about the treatment and the lead [likely referring to lead-based ointments].
Concerning the treatment of fistulas that develop below [likely referring to anal fistulas].
The fistulas that develop in the lowest part below are knotty and thick, which develop near the anus externally or in the space of one side [likely referring to the location of the fistula relative to the anus].
And it is one fistula or many.
And when that knot is old and discharges watery white humidity or subtle pus, and sometimes some of these fistulas are penetrating.
And those penetrating ones are known because of what comes out of them through discharge and by flatulence; and sometimes the patient suffers from discharge, and worms easily come out of them.
And some of these fistulas sometimes penetrate into the space of the bladder, and into the meatus of the penis; and some penetrate into the joint of the hip and into the buttock.
And what distinguishes a fistula penetrating into the anus from one that doesn't, is that you insert your finger into the anus, and insert a thin probe of copper or iron into the fistula when there is no tortuosity.
But if there is tortuosity in it, then insert into it a thin probe of lead or horsehair until you feel the probe or hair on your finger.
And if you don't feel it at all, nor does any matter of discharge, worms, or flatulence proceed from the opening, as we have said, then know that it is non-penetrating, and then attend to its cure.
And when the fistula penetrates into the bladder or into the meatus of the penis, then its indication is thus: urine from that [area] and prohibition of it so that the place may be consolidated with medicine.
But if it is penetrating into the joint of the hip or into the bone and causes pain in the circumference, then its sign is that the probe penetrates there if there is no tortuosity in it into the bone, and causes pain in the circumference of the part; and that from it comes putridity constantly; and that medication is of no use in it, nor is it consolidated with ointment.
And in all those penetrating fistulas there is no complete cure, and the cure of these is the labor and vanity of foolish physicians, upon which they are fixed.
But in that which is not penetrating and not old, healing with iron is hoped for, according to what I will relate to you, and this I have already proven.
And it is that the patient lies between your hands on his back and raises his legs upward, his thighs inclined towards his belly, and then introduce a lead or bronze probe, if there is tortuosity in the fistula, until you know the place where the probe penetrates.
And if the patient feels it around the anus, then you should insert your finger into the anus.
And if you feel with your finger the probe already discovered by itself penetrating, other than what you feel between your finger and that abscess or flesh, then you know for certain that this is penetrating; and then do not work on that, for there is no healing in that, as we have said.
However, some say that it is healed slowly in some men.
And the medication by which improvement is hoped for is that you ignite a fine cautery according to the width of the fistula, as has been described, and insert that ignited cautery into the fistula until it reaches around the anus; and then repeat this two or three times until you know that all those fleshy fistula-like growths resembling a bird's quill are cauterized.
And then treat it with poultices wrapped in linen until all the cauterized flesh sloughs away, and then treat it with flesh-generating and consolidating medicines, and if it is healed, good; and if not, then it will not be healed at any time by any other treatment.
And if the probe enters, and does not penetrate beyond your finger which is in the anus, and between that and the probe there is a palpable thickness of flesh or abscess, and you see the fistula in such a way that it is the surface of the skin, then dry the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and you will see it dry with the probe, and let it be in the fistula until the incision reaches the place where the end of the probe arrives, and through the probe it is freed and removed.
And then cleanse those fistula-like tissues which resemble a bird's quill, and let no part of them remain.
But if you see a flow of blood occur between you and your work when you cauterize those tissues, then the best and most gentle instrument is to cauterize with fire or with a sharp medicine, because cauterization with fire accomplishes two good things: that is, the cauterization of those affected tissues, and the staunching of the blood and the drying of the humors.
And then treat it with poultices wrapped in linen or crushed flax until the area produces pus, and then treat the wound with ointments that generate and consolidate firm flesh.
And these are medicines in which there is astringency and drying; when that is restored it is healed and we do not fear recurrence.
But if the fistula penetrates to the rectum and extends from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the rectum and explore it with that.
And so if you feel the fistula and between you and it there is a membrane of fat or flesh, and it is suitable for penetration, then there is no operation on it except by way of trust and hope.
Which cause is to be managed in one of three ways: either cauterize it as we have said; or dry it until you reach its cavity so that it is possible for you to insert the poultices and the appropriate cure; and do not proceed much with incision lest you cut the muscle containing the rectum, and that you harm the patient in such a way without their will; and then treat it with what we have said for it will heal quickly; or that you elongate that membrane and clean it after drying it near the rectum, as we have said, either with a probe or with another instrument of sharp extremity, and then clean those fistulous tissues which are in the incision where you can with probing; and then strive to effect the healing of the whole wound with incision, and if the orifice above is not healed, and the fistula remains open in the part of the rectum, and will be easier for the patient. And sometimes the fistula ends in this manner: it is when you insert the probe into the fistula, and it is in the side of the rectum around the surface of the body with the tail and in the margin of the rectum, then take a probe with a perforated end, which is like a needle for preparing skins in this form:
and insert it into that fistula wrapped with five threads or around them; and then insert a probe with thread into the fistula until you reach its cavity.
But if it is penetrating into the margin of the rectum inwards, then you extract the thread from that hole so that you insert your finger into the rectum; and extract the end of the thread and join the two ends together, and tighten each, and leave it for a day or two, and when you incise the thread further into the flesh, tighten it well so that those tissues are incised which are between the two ends of the thread and that they fall away.
And then treat the wound until it is healed and consolidated.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate, however you are prepared to do so, except that it should be very carefully done, because it is not for you to do this so as not to cut the muscle; and then treat it with that until it is healed.
The form of the pointed probe with which the fistula is probed: its curvature is very sharp and the other part is not sharp, so that you do not incise what is not necessary, as sometimes happens.
Of the suturing of hemorrhoids from which blood flows in their incision.
Hemorrhoids are of two kinds, for either they are between the buttocks like charcoal, or they are like bunches of grapes. And of these there are small and large ones, and blood flows from them constantly, and there is one, and there are many, and they are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However, those that are outside the anus are according to the months of little humidity, from which flows citrine water or a little blood with continuous discharge, and they are according to the color of the body.
And the treatment of that which is inside the anus is that you command the patient to sit and strain until the anus protrudes and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then you should grasp and lift them with hooks or hold them with your fingernail, and then incise them down to their roots.
But if the hooks cannot hold them because of their humidity and softness, then grasp them with a rough cloth or with cotton or with a wad of wool, and extract them with your fingers, and then incise them and sprinkle on them some hot powders near the incision, so that they act on them in place of cautery; or cauterize them as is described in the chapter on cauterization. And then treat them with the other treatments until they are healed.
And if the anus does not obey [the command to] protrude, then stimulate the patient with a small, mildly irritating suppository, and it should be washed with that water which is in the anus and which should gently protrude when the patient strains.
But if the hemorrhoids are outside the anus, then their cause is slight, and you should grasp them with your fingernail, or grasp them with a hook, and incise them, and then treat them with what we have said until they are healed.
And that which you have incised, you must suture as follows: take enveloped thread and insert it into a needle and then extract the hemorrhoids to the upper parts, and make it penetrate with the needle to the root on the other side, and wrap the two ends of the thread in the lower part of the needle, and it should be latitudinally; and tighten the hemorrhoid with a strong suture, and then knot the thread and remove the needle.
And do this to all, and leave one of them uncut, so that the superfluity of blood may flow from it.
And then place on the anus a cloth soaked in rose oil or cerate; and place that upon the anus and command the patient to rest. And then leave it until it falls off; and when it has fallen off, treat it with ointment or other treatment until it is healed.
And since they are old and that medicine is not effective, then it is necessary that you scrape them with the point of a spatula or with your fingernail until they are moist and the outer layer is removed from them, which prevents their consolidation; and then treat them until they are consolidated.
Then remove them with a more forceful scraping than the first, until they bleed much and are excoriated; and then treat them until they are healed.
Know that a felon, that is, a whitlow, is nothing but a red nodule, according to the color of the body, similar to the head of a nail, and is found throughout the body and especially in the parts below the feet and on the fingers, and pain arises from them because of the inflammation.
And then it is necessary that what is in the circumference of the whitlow be incised, and held with forceps or with an instrument, and be eradicated from its root; and then treat it.
And if you wish, cauterize it according to what has been described in its chapter.
Moreover, a wart is a small, rough, fistulated, round eminence on the surface of the body, and many times it appears on the hands of children.
And its cure is that you extend and incise it, or that you constrict it with a thread or with a tight bandage so that it falls off. And if you wish, cauterize it, as we have said, with fire or with a sharp medicine (caustic).
Moreover, a mole is a small, hairy or fistulated, large eminence on the surface of the body, going far into the skin, and when the cold finds it, it is felt in it similar to the biting of an ant, and it is found all over the body.
And its cure is that you take a cannula of goose quill or vulture quill, and it should be strong, and place it over the mole until the circle of the cannula contains the parts of it; and then rotate your hand with the cannula until you incise what is in the circumference of the mole, and place it into the depth of the flesh, and so you eradicate the mole from its root.
And if you wish, do this with a brass or iron cannula according to this method:
Let the upper part of the cannula be drawn out to a fine point, blunt (that is, not perforated), wrapped so that the burning of it is prevented and its rotation (is facilitated).
And if you wish, cauterize the mole as was previously mentioned in the cauterization of a boil in the chapter on cautery.
Concerning the instruments that are used to administer a clyster.
Sometimes a clyster is made of silver, or latten (brass), or cast or hammered bronze; and sometimes it is made of these instruments, small and large, according to those to whom they are administered, for that which is administered in the treatment of small children should be small; and for those whose bowels are weak or painful, the clysters for them should be small.
The form of a large and a small clyster:
The head of its cannula, to which the bag is attached, should be wide in this manner; and let there be a distinction where the bag is tied, like this.
And the lower extremity, which is inserted into the rectum, should be blunt (that is, not open), very smooth, tapering to a point; and on one side of it let there be two holes, and on the other side one hole, and let the width of the hole be according to the thickness of the fingers and a little larger.
And let the bag, in which the medicines are placed, be of animal bladder or sheepskin, and its length be one palm (handspan).
And let it be perforated with many holes, thus arranged; and let the space between the holes be the thickness of a finger.
Then insert into those holes strong, raw flax threads, about ten or so; and attach the bag with them; and when the bag is placed in position, tie it tightly at the head of the clyster with two distinct ligatures, and then cauterize each hole with it.  The method of cauterizing this is that you fill the bag with the medicine, warmed according to the patient's tolerance; and be careful that it is neither too hot nor too cold.
Then let the patient lie supine on his back, and raise his legs and place a cushion beneath him, upon which is already placed a cloth or linen; and then anoint the end of the clyster with oil, or egg white, or fenugreek mucilage, or something similar; and then insert it into the rectum easily, and then let the attendant take the bag and squeeze it with both hands and vigorously until everything in the clyster is evacuated into the bowel; and then withdraw the clyster and the patient lowers his legs.
And if the medicine forces him to defecate, hold it as long as you can; and if he sleeps with it all night, it is more beneficial in its effect.
But if you administer this clyster for diseases of diarrhea and his defecation is frequent, then repeat the clyster until he is healed.
And if there are hemorrhoids or painful abscesses in the rectum, then the clyster should be very fine and soft, of the kind with which infants are clystered.
Concerning the cure of wounds.
I have already said in the division on the treatment of wounds what was appropriate to the subject, and I say thus the treatment of those with hand and with medicines according to their needs.
Therefore I say that wounds are diversified according to what causes the wounds and according to the location where the wounds occur.
And the causes by which wounds are often made are such as a fall from a height, or an incision by a sword or knife, or a blow with a lance, or with a stick, or with an arrow, or similar things from many causes.
But the wounds that are in the locations of the body are such as a wound which falls upon the head, or the neck, or the feet, or the abdomen, or the thigh, and similar to those of the limbs.
And I am recounting the treatment of certain wounds so that you may apply that reasoning and rule to other wounds.
And I will begin with simple head wounds, for the discussion of compound wounds will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore I say that when a simple wound occurs on the head and there is no fracture, then consider: if it is from a fall from a height or similar, and it has only wounded the skin and is a large wound, and you fear the occurrence of a hot abscess in that patient, then you should immediately bleed him and phlebotomize the cephalic vein, and do not delay this; and extract blood according to the patient's strength unless some reason prohibits phlebotomy, especially if the blood that has come from the wound is scant.
And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side from the wounded part; and guard the patient from repletion and from wine and heavy foods.
But if any cause prohibits bloodletting, then let his food be reduced and he should not approach meat or wine.
And place on the wound, if a hot abscess occurs there, a pledget soaked in rose oil alone or in wine in which a styptic has been dissolved.
But if it is certain that a hot abscess will form, then place on the wound, if it is recent, its own blood and, before the air changes it, the powder which I shall soon describe.
And if the air has already changed it, then apply some ointment until it is healed.
But if the wound is large or is from a sword cut or the like, and its lips are not joined together by themselves, then join them with sutures according to the method of suturing which I shall describe.
But if the wound has exposed the bone and hangs, and there is not retention of it but a slight suspension, then incise it and then treat the wound with medicines and restore the flesh in place of the bone, and order the patient to be fed on foods which are thin:  broths and soft foods and similar, until it is healed.
And if you find putrefaction in the bone and it does not adhere to the bone, then you incise all that which is rotten and remove it, and then treat it.
But if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which a flow of blood may occur, and it is not stopped by medicines, then you should investigate the artery, and if you find it uncut, then you should cut it with a spatula, or tie it; and if necessity compels you to do what we have said does not work, then you cauterize until the bleeding is stopped.
And if these wounds are simple and small, then their cause is light; therefore you should sprinkle on them this powder, for it consolidates them before the air changes them.
And the manner of making the powder is this: take olive oil one part, dragon's blood two parts, and unslaked lime three parts; let it all be well ground and sifted, and with it let the wound be filled, and let it be bound with a good bandage until it adheres well to it, and is coagulated with the blood.
And then bind it above with powders and leave it, and do not unbind it while it is thus unless it becomes suppurated, for the medicine is not eradicated from it until it is healed.
And sometimes this alone suffices, when you do not have olive oil and dragon's blood at hand.
And do this same operation on large wounds when it is necessary to apply it to them and they are fresh, and are recent wounds with their blood, which the air has not altered.
And if the air has altered them, and especially if it is in summer, then this powder is not suitable, but rather treat them with what we have said, so that you apply to them some digestive ointments, or that you apply to them powder from barley flour with water and honey until they exude pus; and then treat them with other cures until they are healed.
But if a fracture in the bone occurs with the wound, and it is small, then you extract it with tweezers.
And I will say the cure for head fractures in what follows.
Of the wounds of the neck.
But if the wound occurs in the neck, then there is no difference in medication between it and a simple head wound.
But if the nerves of the neck or an artery are incised, then if it is a nerve, there is no other remedy but to avoid treating it with something that has strong astringent properties, such as vitriol and things similar to it, because it harms the nerves and counteracts it. And do not apply anything cold to the wound, because the substance of the nerves is cold and continuous with the brain, which is the highest of all the members.
And if it is healed with mild medicines, such as washing the wound with fresh water, sometimes infused with rose oil or plain oil, and then, after washing with fresh water,  applying other softening poultices in this manner, and moist ointments.
But if the wound is large, then administer sutures, or draw the edges together, and then treat it with powders until it is healed.
But if the wound is deep, or if there is pus at the lowest point of the wound, then perforate it at the lowest point.
But if an artery is incised in the wound and bleeding ensues, then separate or cut it, or tie it off, or cauterize it if necessity demands it.
But if the vertebrae of the neck or all of them have been incised, and the neck veins have been saved, then you should join the edges of the wound with sutures over the neck bone and do not touch it; first, equalize it and return it to its natural shape, and then bind it with a suitable bandage, and leave it for some days; and then treat it with ointments which have styptic and healing properties until it is healed.
But if the wound has damaged the bone, then cauterize it, and then if there are bone fragments, extract all that you can; and what you cannot manage, leave it until the wound suppurates, and is separated and the injury is relieved.
And if the wound is recent, then sprinkle upon it the powder which has been recommended to you. But if a fistula has formed in the wound, then treat it with that which will be recalled in its chapter.
If it is a wound from a lance or knife, and you see its depth, then consider further if there is a discharge of air from the wound when the patient breathes; then know that the wound is mortal.
And if it is not deep and the wound is recent with its blood, then in the first hour you apply powder, and do not bind it tightly lest the blood be retained in its depth and return to the heart and kill the patient, but apply attractive ointment, and unbind it twice a day.
And if ointment is not at hand, then place old linen on the opening of the wound to absorb what comes from the humors; and have the patient sleep on the wound so that what is gathered there may drain.
But if three days or more have passed since the wound and it has not caused the patient spasms, tremors of the heart, or constriction of the throat, and you see his other dispositions good, then know that the wound is safe; and then treat it with calls and other treatments until it is healed.
But if its healing is delayed, and it remains open for a long time, then know that a fistula has formed; treat that then according to its chapter.
And if the wound is from a cut by a sword or knife, and is simple on the surface of the foot or back, then treat it with what is prescribed for sutures, if it is large, or with powder, if it is small.
But if damage has been done to the bone, and pieces have been cut from it, then you must clean the wound and try to extract those pieces, if they are separated. But if they are not separated, then leave them until the wound is suppurated, so that their extraction is easier.
And for other accidental wounds in other limbs, the treatment is similar to what we have described.
Of the wounds of the abdomen.
The ailment which happens in the abdomen is either great, or small, or moderate.
And a small rupture is treated differently, and it is such that their reduction is more difficult, if you do not take care of their replacement into their place immediately, because if it opens and swells, their insertion is made difficult.
Therefore the best rupture is moderate, because in that one the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is in the two species previously mentioned.
And know that the suturing of the abdomen is according to four methods, but two sutures are common which are suitable for the suturing of other wounds of the body.
And two other sutures are specific,  communicating with the others [sutures], however they are more specific in the suturing of the abdomen.
And I will recount to you each one with explanation and clarification.
Therefore I say that when the wound is small and some part of the intestine has emerged through it, and it is difficult to reduce it, then this is in one of two ways: either because of the smallness of the rupture as we have said, or when the intestine has become inflamed due to the coldness of the air.
And therefore when this is so, then it is necessary that you warm it in such a way that you submerge a sponge or cloth moistened in lukewarm water alone, or that you boil it in water with squill, cyperus, and spikenard, and bathe the intestine with it until the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes this is done with vinegar, which has astringent properties, and is better than water alone in resolving the inflammation.
And then, when you see the inflammation resolved, you moisten the intestine with water in which mallow and marshmallow have been boiled, for by this the entry of it is eased with little effort.
But if the entry of it is hindered despite this treatment, then incise the rupture a little with an instrument with which hemorrhoids are incised.
And the first treatment is better, when it is appropriate that there is no incision, and there is no need to dry it due to necessity and weakness.
Concerning the instrument, this is its form:
One part of it is sharply curved, and the other part is not sharp, and the end is thin, but not as thin as a spatula; rather, it should be slightly blunt.
And so, when the wound is enlarged and the intestine protrudes, then its reduction should follow its natural form, to its proper place, if possible in this way, for this is better.
But when the rupture is large, and is in the lower part of the belly, then you should make the patient lie on his back, and raise his legs higher than his head; and if it is in the upper part of the belly, then you should raise his head and feet higher than the lower parts.
Similarly, if the rupture is in one of the two sides of the belly, then always direct your attention and consideration to ensuring that the side where the wound is located is higher than the other.
And thus you should administer this treatment in both large and medium-sized wounds.
And in small wounds, apply what you have prepared, and then have the assistant place their compassionate hands between your hands, holding the entire rupture with their hand and aligning its lips, and then discover from that to that which would be the suture or other appropriate cause.  (This last phrase is a bit ambiguous due to the archaic nature of the text.  It seems to suggest that the assistant should ensure proper alignment for the stitching or other closure technique).
And this is the common method of suturing: first, take the needle, or several needles according to the size of the wound; then, leave the distance from the end of the rupture to the annular finger, and tighten one needle, provided you do not place thread in the two ends of the suture, with two ends of the thread that is below the suture of the inner parts, until you make it penetrate that part; and you have already joined two ends of the suture and two ends of the thread, and thus four orders are made. (This is a difficult sentence to render perfectly into modern English due to the archaic phrasing and imprecise anatomical references. The "four orders" likely refers to some sort of methodical approach to securing the stitches).
Then tighten with double thread in the circuit of the needle, and sometimes on two parts at once, and then join the two lips of the wound with a decent joining; and then leave the thickness of a finger and place another needle in that space, and then tie it with thread as you did with the first needle; and do not cease to do this with needles as needed until you have completed the entire closure of the wound.
And let the needles be of medium thickness, neither too thick nor too thin, for thin needles cut the flesh too easily; and thick needles are difficult to thread, and therefore they should be of a moderate thickness.
And the needle should not be inserted too deeply into the two ends of the suture, lest the flesh be cut too easily and the wound open before it heals.
And the sutures should not be too long, lest the wound be prevented from healing.
Then cut the ends of the needles so that they do not injure the patient during their sleep; and you will place on the suture powdered herbs from all sides, which will hold the ends of the needles; and leave those in place until you know that the wound is healed.
And this type of suture with needles is most convenient for small wounds, for in the suturing of these, one or two needles, or thereabouts, are sufficient.
But the second suture is common: and it is that you suture the four ends, of the wound and two ends of the lip, at once, with a needle around which is wound thread of suitable thinness and thickness.
Then, when with the needle you have penetrated these four ends, return the needle to the same side from which you began, so that the drawn thread falls from the highest part to the wound, so that the suture is like a cloth suture with which cloths are tightened.
And place between each suture the width of the little finger.
And this suture is known to men.
And with such a suture I sewed up the wound which had happened to a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife, and the disruption of the wound was greater than a palm, and about two palms of the middle intestine had come out, and the disruption was in the middle of the member; and then I reduced it after the intestine had been outside the wound for 24 hours.
And the wound was consolidated in the space of fifteen days or thereabouts, and I cured him and he is healed; and afterwards he lived for many years, and remained in good health.
And the other physicians had judged that he would not be healed; and it was by a miracle, for I did not cure him with ointment, for it was in a place where no ointment or any medicine was found, but I placed old linen on the wound twice a day and washed it frequently with honey water until it was healed.
But I will describe two kinds of sutures proper, according to the account of Galen. (Assuming "Paraula" is a variant spelling or corruption of Galen)
And it is that you begin with the suture of the [tail] (or possibly refers to a specific type of stitch), and insert the needle from the outside to the inside.
And then, as the needle penetrates the [tail/stitch] and that same laceration, following its width and length throughout the whole body, leave the end of the suture where you have inserted the needle; and make it penetrate at the other end, the needle from the inside to the outside at the other extremity of the wound.
And then, when you have made that penetration, then you make it penetrate again at that same extremity of the wound from the outside to the inside; and leave the end of the suture which is on this side, and make the needle penetrate at the other extremity from the inside to the outside.
And make it penetrate with that penetration with which you make it penetrate into the suture at the extremity of the wound which is on his side until all of that penetrates.
And then begin from that same side and sew that with the end that is from the suture on the other side, and extract the needle from the [tail/stitch] which you penetrated, and then return it to that [tail/stitch] and sew the end of the suture which is on the other side with this extremity of the wound; and extract it from the [tail/stitch] which is on his side.
And do this once and again, and do this once then another time until you sew all that which is outside according to that example.
And the sum of this manner of suture is that you sew with a skin suture with which you sew skins, so that you sew the suture once from the side of the extremity of the [tail/stitch], and that you leave it another time until you have completed it.
But the second proper type of suture, which I call "Gualia," and this is spoken of by him and recounted by him, is that you sew it according to the similarity with which some physicians sew it, so that you join each part to its corresponding part naturally; and apply the end of one side and the end of the wound to the other end of the side, and this is done as I have recounted to you.
So that you place the needle at the end of the wound near the outer part, and make it penetrate to the inner part in that place, and leave two ends of the suture; and then turn the needle and make it penetrate from outside to inside in two ends of the suture.
And then turn each of them, and make each one penetrate from the inside part to the outside part in two other ends of the wound which are on the opposite side.
And this manner of suture is better than the common, light suture, and it is the suture of which I previously made mention.
This is the discourse of Gualia.
And moreover, some say, the suture of the belly is rectified with two needles, that is, that you insert a thread into each of these, and begin with the insertion of the needle from the side that is towards you, and make it penetrate to the other side according to the suture of those who sew hides together.
And know that when the disruption is in the middle of the belly, then its suture is more difficult than in other places of the belly.
But the judgment of the medication of a wound is the judgment of other wounds.
The reason is that when you cleanse the fresh wound with its blood before the air alters it, and you return the intestine and sew it and prepare it decently, then place incarnative powder on it.
And if the air has already altered the wound, then place on it some ointments that are present to you until they form a hard scab and the threads fall out, and they are consolidated with cyprus and myrrh; and then treat it as the treatment of other wounds until it is healed.
But if you do not have the medicines present, then immediately place on it, as soon as it begins to suppurate, old linen, and change it twice a day, as you have been taught, until it is healed, for you do not need other medication in many situations when the wounds are simple.
But if you fear that the wound will communicate infection to the principal members, then it is necessary that you submerge lamb's wool in oil of good quality, or in rose oil, and place it in the area around the armpits and under the arms.
And if you feel pain or putrefaction in his intestines, because this often happens, then cauterize it with black, warm styptic wine, especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestine and an opening has been made penetrating its cavity.
And know that that which is in the large intestine is of easier healing than that which is in the small intestines.
And it is of difficult healing because the intestine which is called "jejunum" does not receive healing from a wound that falls upon it at all; and this is because of the great multitude of veins that are in it and because of the magnitude of those veins, and because of the subtlety of the body of that intestine, and because of its proximity to the nature of nerves.
But if that which proceeds from the wound is the web [likely referring to a membrane or tissue], and you obtain it fresh, reduce it according to your skill with which you reduce the intestines similarly.
But if it has passed into the space and has already become green or black, then bind it with gall over the place that is blackened, so that a flow of blood may come, because in the web are veins and arteries.
And then incise that which is beyond that ligament, and place two extremities of the gall hanging down from the part below the wound outside it, so that the attraction of it downwards towards the fall of the web and the emission of the arm of the wound may be relieved for you.
A remembrance/reminder of wounds when they happen in the intestine.
When a rupture occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it is possible that it may be restored in some men, because of this, for I saw one who had been wounded in his belly with a lance thrust and the wound was on the right side of the stomach, and the wound was old and had become a fistula, and from it came discharge and flatulence.
Then I pursued his cure, although I was not confident in his healing, but I continued to carefully treat it until it was healed and the place was consolidated (healed completely).
And I, after I saw the place consolidated, feared for the patient that some mishap might befall him in his belly; and no such ill accident occurred, and he remained in his usual good condition, eating and drinking, and then entered a bath and was exercised in his movements.
And some experienced people have said that when a wound occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it should be tied in this manner: that you take ants with large heads; and then bring together the two lips of the wound and place one of those ants, with its mouth open, over the two lips of the wound; then when it bites and clenches its mouth, cut off its head, for it will adhere and not become detached.
And then place another ant next to the first, and do not cease doing this with ants, one next to another, according to the size of the whole wound.
And then reduce (close) and sew the wound; and let those attached heads remain in the intestine until the intestine is healed; and no harm will come to the patient.
And it is also possible that the intestine be tied with fine thread which is made from the intestine of an animal, attached to it, since it is easier to thread with a needle; and it is done thus: you take the end of these threads from the intestine, and let it be well scraped clean, and then tie its end with the thread, wrapping it, and then insert this threaded needle and sew the intestine with it, and close it at the rupture.
And this method of suturing with ants is only a way of trust and hope.
But if the wound is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the small intestines, because then it is only a kind of alleviation, and not a complete healing of it.
Of the treatment of fistula and fistula.  (Note:  The word "zuchem" likely represents a variant spelling or a related term for fistula. The repetition suggests a clarification or emphasis on the condition.)
Know that every wound and abscess, when it ages and becomes old, and becomes an ulcer, and is not consolidated and discharges pus constantly and is not excised, then, in whatever member it may be, is universally called a fistula, and we call that a *zuchem*. (Again, *zuchem* seems to be a variant or related term for fistula.)
