And a fistula, in truth, is a hardened, durable, white knot, which is painless and which has a concavity like the concavity of a bird's claw; and for this reason it is called a knotted ulcer, that is, a claw.
And in some men it is moist, which discharges pus; and quickly the moisture is excised in a few hours. And sometimes this moisture is thick, and it is little, and it is coarse, and it is subtle. (Describing variations in the consistency of the discharge).
I have already shown all those causes, and their signs, and their treatment with medicines in the division (of the treatise).
And it is necessary that I make you know the treatment of a fistula, which is done by way of operation with the hand.
And know that fistulas occur in all the members of the body; for there are fistulas which reach the large veins and arteries, or the nerves, or the diaphragm, or the intestine, or the bladder, or one of the ribs, or one of the vertebrae of the back, or one of the joints composed by composition, such as those of the hand and foot; and those which cause damage to the proximity of a principal member and similar ones.
Therefore, that one of these fistulas which is according to this manner is one of the illnesses of difficult healing, and does not receive treatment, and occupation with it is labor and ignorance.
And if it is one of the fistulas whose depth does not reach those places that I have mentioned, and you hope and trust in its healing, and want to arrive at knowledge of it, then take a probe of bronze or iron, and if the fistula goes straight, and seek it with it.
But if the fistula is tortuous, then investigate it with a probe of fine lead, because lead, with the lightness of its body, is eased and bent along the tortuosity.
And if the fistula has many branching paths, it is not possible for you to determine its course with a probe.
Then, inject a cleansing liquid (or apply a medicated wash) through its orifices, because the liquid with which you inject it goes through the other orifices and cleanses them.
And then inquire carefully, in what manner it is possible for you to know if there are bones or nerves here, and if the depth of the fistula is far or near, or if it is a fistula which has many orifices and remains there; do all this with the sum of your strength, with your questioning, with which you question the patient, and the discovery of the pain through your compression with your hand on the place, and similar signs and accidental causes to the abscess.
And when you have considered all those causes with true judgment, then proceed to the treatment with confidence: and it is that you consider then if it is a fistula apparent and near, or in a safe place, far from a joint, or from a nerve, or from an artery, or from a vein, or from one of the places which I have told you.
Then you dry the fistula as described before in its description, and remove what is in that filtration and of corrupted flesh, and that which is grown in the orifice of the flesh, or of cartilage and the like, and cure it until it is healed.
But if the fistula is of great depth, and is straight, then it is necessary that you incise it in the front as much as is possible for you, and then cleanse it of all its corrupted flesh; and then apply cauteries wrapped in sharp medicines, and introduce them into the depth of the fistula, which you have understood with iron (meaning a surgical instrument).
And do this several times until the sharp medicine corrodes all that is rotten in the depth of the fistula from the corruption.
And then restore it with ointments which generate healthy flesh until it is healed.
But if it is not healed by this, then you cauterize it as we have recounted.
But if the cause of the fistula is bone and you are certain of this, then you should investigate it and dry it as we have recounted, if you are not prohibited by any vein, nerve, or principal member as we have said.
But if the bone is exposed to you and there is some corruption and blackness in it, then you scrape it until all the corruption is removed; and then treat it with something that will cause it to grow flesh until it is healed.
And if it is not healed and discharges pus as it did before, then know that you have not achieved the complete eradication of the corruption; and then uncover it again and meticulously perform the scraping and cleansing with your utmost strength, and then restore it.
And so, if it is healed, good; but if not, then know that that corruption is in the deep recesses of the body, which you have not reached, and then for you in that case there is nothing to do but leave it.
And if the corrupted bone is small, or it is possible for you to remove it, then remove it with fine forceps which are suitable for this.
And if there are many bones, then meticulously remove all of them, and with your strength leave none of them behind.
But if some of the moist tissues hinder you, and there is no fear or mistake in cutting them, then incise those ligaments or flesh if they are there, and free the bones.
But if you are not prepared to remove the bones at this time, then place something on the wound that will cause it to suppurate, and leave it until it has reached the bones, flesh, and ligaments, and keep the wound from closing, and keep the incision you made constricted during your operation.
Furthermore, if you are afraid, place on it a cloth soaked in crushed rue with oil, or a cloth soaked in Egyptian ointment, or in one of the green ointments, for when those ligaments rot, and the bones are separated from them, their removal and extraction is easier.
But if it is a large bone, such as a leg bone or thigh bone or similar, and only its surface is corrupted, then scrape it with a rasp only superficially until the blackness and corruption are removed, and then repair the wound.
But if the corrupted part is large, and the corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to close that and incise everything up to the place where the corruption has reached, and then treat it until it is healed.
And I recount to you what happened to a man in his thigh so that you may use this as an example and aid in your operation.
There was a man young in age, around thirty years old, who experienced pain in his thigh, and from the inner parts, a sickness spread through the matter within the body until the matter reached the thigh, and it was a matter of an abscess with a large abscess, and there was no external cause for it.
And then time passed with that because of the mistakes of the physicians until the abscess was opened, and many materials flowed from it, and its healing was delayed until the leg was fistulated, and many orifices were made in it, all of which discharged pus and the humors of the body.
And then many physicians treated him for about two years, and there was no wisdom in them but in the art, until I was called.
And I saw his leg and the matter flowing from those orifices in great quantity; and the body of that man was quite emaciated, and his color was jaundiced.
And then I inserted a probe into one of those orifices and the probe reached the bone.
And then I examined all the orifices and found that they reached from all parts of the leg.
And then I carefully incised one of those orifices until I uncovered a part of the bone, and I found it corrupted, and it was already earthy, corrupted, and blackened; and a part of the leg was corrupted as far as the marrow.
And then I sawed away that which was exposed and prepared of the exposed bone.
And I believed that in the bone there was only that corruption which I cut away and sawed, and I had already removed it.
And then I applied the restoration of the wound with consolidative medicine for a long time, and it did not heal.
And then I again investigated and uncovered the bone a second time, after the first uncovering, and I found continuing corruption in the bone.
And I sawed away what appeared to me to be that corruption, and then I was unable to effect its cure and restoration, and it was not restored.
And then I uncovered it again and did not cease to incise the bone little by little, bit by bit, and forcibly attempted its restoration, and it was not restored until I had incised about a palm's breadth of it, and that I extracted with its marrow; and then I restored that with medicines, and it was lightly consolidated and healed.
And further iteration in its operation and section is not necessary except for the disposition of its fragility and for the lack of its tolerance and fear of death, for syncope afflicted him at all hours due to an excess of evacuation.
And therefore it is healed by a complete healing, and nothing is in place of the hard bone flesh; and its disposition in its body is made good and its strength returned; and it is restored to its functions and caused him no impediment in walking nor harm in any way.
But if a bone protrudes from the body already excised, then you must treat that in this manner: that you take a ligature and bind that to the extremity of that protruding bone, and command someone to extend it upwards; and place that woolen ligature thicker than the first ligature; and then tie that over the flesh that is beneath the bone, and extend its extremity so that you draw the flesh to the parts below, and you uncover the flesh over the place which you wish to saw so that the saw does not injure the flesh.
And place a piece of wood or a board decently beneath the bone, for when you do this, the saw is not prohibited from cutting the corrupted flesh.
And it is necessary that the sawing be over the corrupted area little by little, for if corruption is in the concavity of the bone and does not appear on its surface to the senses, force the sawing of it another time.
And if the bone is corrupted and not protruding, with one part continuous with the other, and the corruption is in the middle or in a part of it, then you uncover the flesh from all parts completely; and then place wood underneath, and then saw it from the first part where the corruption is until the sawing is continuous with the other part.
And let the sawing be according to the location of the corruption, little by little, as we have said.
And if the corruption is in the joint, incise that corrupted joint, and saw the bone where it is continuous with the other part.
And if the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then it is not in the reason but in the ingenuity [or skill/cleverness].
And if the corruption is in the chest of the hand or foot, it is very difficult; however, it is necessary that you inquire into the corruption in whatever manner appears to you, and that you open that, and clean that according to each disposition which is possible for you, and with each tool it should be rectified for you, when it does not cause you harm to a vein or nerve.
And know that the incisors and saws for incising these bones are many according to the location of the bones, and the preparation of those, and the thickness of those, and the subtlety and the size and the smallness of those, and the hardness and their rarity; therefore it is necessary that you prepare for every kind of operation an instrument convenient to that operation.
And know that this operation signifies to you the type of instrument of which you have need; when with you there is great study and long and deep knowledge of the ways of this art, and experience of many kinds of illnesses, because then you will already have for yourself that which is convenient for the instruments for every illness.
And I am showing you at the end of this chapter the number of instruments which you will have simply, with which you will proceed to your practice which you will test upon others. Shape of saw:

Of the incision of extremities and the sawing of bones.
If the extremities become diseased due to an intrinsic or extrinsic cause, or if you have treated that corruption with medicines and the treatment is not effective, or you see the corruption spreading through the limb without any apparent cause, then you must incise that limb to the point where the corruption has reached, so that the patient may escape death or a worse affliction which is a greater impairment of the limb.
And the sign of this affliction is that the limb turns black as if it were burned by fire, or the blackness spreads until the putrefaction reaches that limb, affecting it and potentially the whole body; therefore, treat it with incision.
Similarly, if the cause of the corruption is the impact of some poisonous substance, such as a sea scorpion, viper, or shrew, and the like.
And if the corruption or injury is in the extremity of a finger, then you should incise the finger and not allow the corruption to spread to the hand.
Similarly, if it affects the hand, then you should incise it at the root of the palm, and not allow the corruption to spread to the forearm.
And if it affects the arm, then incise the arm up to the elbow joint.
And if the corruption spreads beyond the elbow, then in that case the patient's death is certain; and administering any other treatment beyond that point is futile.
Similarly, if corruption affects a toe, then you should incise it up to one of the ankle bones.
And if it takes hold in the instep of the foot, then you should incise the entire foot. But if it ascends to the knee, then you should incise the leg up to the knee joint.
And if the corruption has already reached the knee, then there is no remedy for him but resignation, and to leave the patient to death.
And the manner of incising a limb or cauterizing it is that you should tighten a ligature on the part below where you wish to incise, and tighten another ligature above the location; and let the assistant extend a ligature to the parts below, and let the assistant extend another ligature above; and you separate the flesh which is between the two ligatures, so that all the flesh is exposed; and then it should be incised or cauterized.
And it is necessary that a clean cloth be placed on all parts so that it does not touch the healthy area, and that the patient receives a proper incision and a hot poultice.
But if a flow of blood occurs while you are working, then you should carefully cauterize the affected area, or apply some styptic powders to it; and then return to your treatment until it is complete.
And then bind the injured limb with a suitable bandage, and treat it until it is healed.
And I recount to you what happened to a man in his foot, this same accident which I have recounted to you, which is that a blackness occurred in his foot with a burning that resembled a fire burn.
And that corruption occurred first in his toe until it spread to the whole foot.
And then that man prepared himself, because he saw the healing progress in the limb with such vehemence that he suffered from pain and burning, and he incised that foot up to the joint, and it healed.
Long afterwards, the same corruption occurred in the index finger of his hand.
And then he came to me, and I tried to repel that excess with what I possessed in the way of medicines, for the purification of his body; but the excess was not repelled and began to spread to another finger until it reached his hand.
And he asked me to incise his hand, but I did not want to do that, hoping for the repulsion of that excess, and more than that, I feared death from the incision of his hand, because the patient's strength was failing.
And after he departed from me, he returned to his land, and it came to me that that man prepared and incised his whole hand, and he was healed.
And this narrative is added only so that it may be a help in the treatment of this disease, and that it may be a sign by which it may be recognized and according to which it may be treated.
Concerning the treatment of sinuses and the nature/quality of clysterization with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in fleshy members and the space of the abscess is extended until it forms a collection of pus, and then it is broken and perforated, and the pus comes out of it, and the place remains empty as if it were a vessel, and the skin that is over it is like thin cloth, and it has not reached further corruption, and does not affect the bone or nerve or ligament, it is called a sinus, and it is not called a fistula.
But however, if the space is extended so that the corruption affects any member, then it is called a fistula and not a sinus.
And its cure is that you incise that skin completely as it is rolled up, especially when it is thinned and made like cloth, and make sure that it does not adhere to the place because of its corruption.
But if you suspect that the skin will adhere and that the corruption has not reached a further stage, and there is thickening of the skin, then you treat that with a clyster.
And it is that you consider the sinus, and then if it is large, and the pus from it is foul-smelling, then clysterize it with an Egyptian medicine which is made of oil, sour wine, and resin, taking equal parts, and they are mixed in a vessel, and they are heated over the fire until the medicine becomes thick and reaches the consistency of honey.
Then you take what is necessary from it and dissolve it with water and honey; and clysterize the sinus with this, and constrict its opening, and leave the medicine in it for the amount of two hours.
And then extract that with a syringe; and do this for so many days until the sinus is cleansed and the foul smell is removed.
And sometimes it is clysterized with lye water, when this medicine is not present to you; and it is that you take the ashes of wood, and put water over them; and then filter that and clysterize with it until you are sure that the sinus is clean.
And if the patient does not tolerate the Egyptian medicine nor the lye water, then clysterize it with water and honey, and to which crushed resin is mixed, and clysterize with wine and honey mixed, for it is the property of honey to cleanse and wash, and the wine causes it to adhere to the sinus, and especially when the wine has an excess of astringency and dryness.
And when you have done this several times and are sure that the sinus is clean, then clysterize it with something that causes the flesh to grow there.
And it is that you take diafinicon ointment and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine; or that you clysterize it with other medicines and ointments that have the properties which I have already written in the treatise of ointments.
And if the orifice of the sinus is narrow, so that the clyster cannula cannot be inserted, then enlarge it little by little with a small iron instrument, and place in it a small tent wrapped in Egyptian ointment or basilicon ointment until it is enlarged.
And similarly, if the orifice is wide, then you should approximate its lips with sutures and leave an opening sufficient for the clyster to enter without addition [of further enlargement].
And if the orifice from which the pus flows is situated higher than the lower parts, then drain it at the lowest point so that the pus flows downwards, because when the pus is confined in the depth of the sinus, the flesh will not regenerate there.
And if it is not possible for you to drain the sinus into the lowest surrounding bone as desired, then ensure that you prepare the member with a preparation by which the pus flows lightly as is fitting, and that nothing is retained in it to obstruct the flow of pus.
But the cloth that is over the orifice of the sinus, that one you should change every hour so that the pus that accumulates there may exit. And so that you may know if the pus that flows from it is much or little, or is digested, or is undigested.
And with this, consider the location of that sinus, whether the patient feels pain there or not, and if there is an abscess there.
And as your treatment is prolonged in this manner, and you see that the matter that is expelled onto the orifice of the sinus is already adhering, or is becoming adherent, then on the third day loosen it; and when you loosen the dressing, then change the cloth for another and renew the ointment.
But if near the sinus there is a subtle poison, then do not despair of its adherence, for its healing is near, for there are some men in whom the generation of flesh in their wounds is slow, and there are others in whom the opposite is true.
And if undigested pus has issued from the sinus for many days, then know that the sinus will not adhere; and if the adherence of the sinus is delayed and the cause prolonged, then you should adjust the drying quality of the medicines, and if the medicines are moist in their substance and in their virtue, they are dried as with diafinicon ointment when superfluity of addition of zegi is adjusted in it.
And the best remedy with which it is cured, is that you take diafinicon ointment and dissolve it in rose oil, and let old wine be mixed into its substance, and mix that well with it, and use that.
And it acts similarly in that case, when there is no pus: diafinicon ointment, honey cooked until it thickens, and use it; or take myrrh, aloes, and incense, grind everything and powder it over the honey while it is on the fire, then apply this on a cloth and bind it over the sinus; and apply that with the honey which you have cooked until it thickens, and powder it on a cloth and bind it.
And sometimes long birthwort and roots of lily of the valley (and it is lily of the valley) and barley flour and centaury are administered with it, and use these simples or compounded, grind those, sift them and powder them over the honey which has been mentioned; and use it.
And if the sinus is prolonged, and is not healed by this treatment, then know that the corruption is in the depth of it, and it has already reached the bones or other hard parts or ligaments; then you cure it with fistular treatment, as has been said above.
Concerning the treatment of paronychia, and worn nails, and added finger incision, and section.
Paronychia is an abscess which arises under the nail of the finger or toe of the hand or foot, and quickly arises in other fingers, and as it is prolonged and its cure is less prized, or it is suppurated from a hot abscess, or is corrupt, and pus comes out until it corrodes the root of the nail, and quickly corrupts the whole of it, and quickly its corruption penetrates to the bone until there is a foul odor, and the extremity of the finger becomes swollen, and its color becomes dark.
And therefore when you have this with that which we have said in the division and you do not find success with your treatment, then it is necessary that you incise with iron all the superfluity that remains of the nail and then cauterize the wound there, because much cautery is beneficial in this.
But if the bone is sound and the nail is healthy, and the additions of the nail, emptied, already compress the flesh in the underlying parts, and begin to prick and injure it, then it is necessary that you place a thin blade under the angle of the nail that pricks the flesh, and push it up and incise that flesh easily, and put on what remains of the flesh corrosive burning medicines until all is consumed; and then cure that with ointments until it is healed.
But if the corruption already penetrates the bone, then it is necessary that you incise that bone and extract it, for at no time is it healed if there is corrupt bone in it.
And if you see the corrosion and corruption go into the finger, then you incise it at one of the joints according to the need, and then cure it until it is healed.
But if a blow or friction happens to the nail, and there is vehement pain in it, then it is necessary that you first bleed the patient, and then cut the nail with a sharp instrument with a transverse cut from above downwards, and be careful that the cut does not reach the flesh that is under the nail, for you will cause the patient vehement pain, and it will be a cause of the generation of superfluous flesh in the place; and then cure that place with something that alleviates pain.
But a finger that is born in the hands of some men is quickly all fleshy, and quickly has bones in it, or quickly has a nail.
And some of them are born at the root of the joint, or their birth is at some joint of the fingers. But that which is born at some joint of the finger does not hurt, and that which is born at the joints is quickly painful.
And of those that are born fleshy, the excision is easy, and it is that you incise that one below its root with a broad instrument.
But of that which is born at the root of the joint, the cure is difficult; and therefore beware of its excision.
But in that which is born on a finger between the joints, it is necessary that you first incise its flesh with a round incision down to the bone, and then close it with one of those saws suitable for this, and then treat it until it is healed.
But consolidation which happens to the fingers between their knuckles, either by the birth of a man born thus, or by the consolidation of a wound, or by burning with fire, or similarly, then it is necessary that you cut that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural form.
And then place between them cloths soaked in rose oil so that they are not lightly consolidated, and separate them, or place between them a thin lead plate until it is consolidated as is necessary.
And similarly, if consolidation occurs in some fingers with the palm, then you cut that consolidation according to how it is formed and suitable to the shape of the limb.
Concerning incisions of vices, there are several.
Vices are varicose veins, and they are thick, convoluted veins full of melancholic superfluities which appear in many parts of the body, and most often in the legs, especially in the legs of travelers, laborers, and those who carry heavy loads.
Then it is necessary that you administer a purging of the body of black bile several times with strong expulsion, and then bleed the patient from the basilica vein.
But the cure of them with iron is according to two methods: one is that they are incised and that black blood is drawn out, and the other method is that the vein is raised and entirely extracted.
The incision is done in this manner: first bathe the leg with very hot water until the thick, feculent blood is resolved from the leg. Then bind the patient's leg tightly from the highest part of the thigh to the area below the knee with a bandage, and then cut the vein in one, two, or three places with a wide incision. Then, with your hand,  raise the black blood from the lower part of the leg upwards, and from above downwards, until the desired quantity of blood has been released—enough to weaken the strength of the disease.
Then bind it up, and instruct the patient to abstain from foods that generate black bile.
And repeat the phlebotomy treatment when the veins are full and this harms the patient.
But their elevation is according to this method: you who shave the patient's leg, if there is much hair on it, and then place it in a bath and bathe the leg with hot water until it reddens and the veins become prominent; or, if a bath is not available, have the limb heated through vigorous exercise, until the limb is warmed.
Then cut the vein in the area of the vein, lengthwise or behind it below the knee, or at its lowest point below the ankle.
Then open the vein with an incision and scrape the vein on all sides until it feels sensitive, and it is from its appearance that you see this redness leaning toward blackness.
And then, when you have opened the vein, you see that which is white, like a cord.
Then you place a thread under it so that it is raised and pulled away from the vein, and suspend it with a golden hook.
Then make another incision near that incision for three fingers' breadth, and scrape the vein from the area above the vein until it appears.
Then lift it and incise it in the second incision near the heel, and then extract it and lift it until it comes out through the second incision, and then extract it from the incision that is above it.
And do this until you extract it from the incision that is highest or most superior of all the incisions, until it is all extracted; and then incise it.
And if it does not obey you in being extracted and lifted, then insert a needle with strong double thread and tie it and extract it, or insert a thread under it and rotate your hand in all directions until it is pulled out. And be careful that it is not incised; for if it is broken, it is made difficult for you to lift it and it becomes harmful to the patient.
And when you have completely removed it, then place on the wound site wool soaked in rose-wine and olive oil, both rose-scented and unscented, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the intestine is contracted, having a tortuosity to which there is a twisting in parts, and it is not straight as we have said, then it is necessary that you make incisions on it at each part of its tortuosity and at the places of its twisting, and then suspend it with hooks until you lift it completely.
And be careful in your incision that you do not cut the vein and that you do not injure it, for it will be difficult for you to lift it; and use your prudence in this.
The form of the incision with which the intestine is cut:
It is not sharpened like hooks, nor is it of a sharp extremity so that it does not injure the vein; and it is of a thick, flexible lion [likely meaning "shape" or a similar descriptive word; "leon" seems to be a scribal error], for if it were thin, it would cut the vein by its thinness; therefore it has superfluous thickness as we have said.
Of the extraction of the vein of the leg.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot lands, such as al-Ategi in the land of the Arabs, and in the hot and arid lands of India, of little humidity, and in which neither grass nor trees grow.
And it is easily generated in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is from putrefaction that comes from under the skin, just as serpents, worms, scabies, and maggots are generated between the skin and the flesh.
And a sign of the beginning of this vein is that vehement inflammation occurs in the leg; and near the place it is blistered; and then the vein begins to emerge from the place of that blister, which resembles the root of a plant.
And when its extremity appears, then you should wrap around it a piece of lead weighing one to two drachms, and tie it, leaving the lead suspended from the leg.
And as many times as there is any cause of it on the outer parts, wrap it in lead and tie it. And if the growth is prolonged, then you should incise a part of it and wrap the remainder; and do not incise the root until it has emerged, for if you incise it, it is retracted and enters the flesh and causes an abscess and putrefaction in the place and a bad ulceration.
Therefore it must be treated gently and extracted little by little, until it is completely expelled and no cause of it remains in the body.
And from this vein has come forth in some men a growth that is five palms and fingers in length, and it has already reached me that one of twenty palms has come forth.
And if it is incised at the time of its treatment, then insert a probe into its opening and perforate it with a long opening to the body, until all that is in the matter is evacuated, and treat the putrefaction of the place for some days with medicines; and then cure it with the cure for abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, and especially when it appears at the joint of the foot or in the foot, and many orifices come to it, and a branch emerges from each orifice.
And therefore cure it in the way we have said in the division and with what has been said above.
Concerning the incision of worms generated under the skin, and it is called bovine illness.
This illness is called in some lands bovine illness because it often happens to cattle, and it is a small worm which is born between the skin and the flesh, and goes throughout the body rising and falling, and is shown by its movement from limb to limb until it breaks the skin and comes out.
And its generation is due to the putrefaction of certain humors, just as worms, snakes, earthworms, and ascarids are generated in the belly.
And it is feared for its malice, for when it goes into the body and is elevated to the head, and has reached the eye, then very quickly it enters it and destroys the eye, and this happens many times.
Therefore, when you wish to cure this and extract it, then it is done only by observing its movement and appearance to the senses, and then it is necessary that you bind that which is above it and that which is below it with a strong bandage and constriction, and then dry it above and extract it.
And if it is deeply embedded in the flesh and you do not find it, then you apply a cautery with fire to the place until it dies, and the greatest fear is its corruption, by which it corrupts the place, as we have said.
But if you see it has reached the head near the eye, then bind above the eyebrow with a strong constriction, and then dry above it, and extract it; and the patient must purify their body with medicines that evacuate bad putrid humors, and guard against foods that generate putrefaction.
Concerning the incision for the disease *nafir*.  (Note: "nafir" appears to be a disease name not readily identifiable in modern medical terminology.  Further context would be helpful.)
This illness is called *nafir* in our land, and it is a pain which comes in some limbs, and then is moved from limb to limb.
And I have seen that, as I will recount to you. I was called to a sick woman in a village, and I uncovered her arm and saw a small swelling in the superficial vein of the arm, and after it had remained for an hour, I saw that swelling moving like a worm climbing upwards, but more lightly than a worm, as if it were quicksilver when it runs from place to place.
And then the pain left that place and fixed itself in the muscle, and there it remained for about an hour.
And then it ran to the other side until it reached the other arm.
And then I realised that it was moving throughout her body, as I saw; and I was very astonished by its change of location and its speed from limb to limb. And I had seen this before with my own eyes in this manner, however I have seen many who find pain shifting from limb to limb. But I did not see it with my eyes as clearly as in the woman.
And I thought it was because the woman was old, with a dry body and exposed veins, and in this way that shifting windiness appeared noticeable.
And it is necessary that it does not appear according to this observation in those who are delicate and with moist bodies and hidden veins.
And if you want to cure him, and the patient feels that pain, then it is not apparent to your eye as we have said, then you press on that area gently and dry it until that constricted windiness is expelled, and cauterize the place.
But if you do not see it with your eyes, then you treat it with chest percussion and with things that expel windiness, and drive it away with foul-smelling pills and pills of scammony and the like.
On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diversified according to their types, and according to the places in the body where they lodge, for their diversity is such that there are those with large points, and small ones, and concave ones, and blunt ones, that is, solid ones; and those that have three edges, and those which have barbs; and those which have feathers.
And of the signs in the brain, if an arrow lodges there and penetrates the bone, and emerges from the cranium which is over the brain, for it causes sudden faintness, and dimness of vision, and vertigo, and redness in the eyes, and inflammation and redness of the tongue, and spasm and confusion of mind, and vomiting of bile, and speech is quickly cut off and the voice is destroyed, and there emerges from the place of the wound a white moisture resembling pus, and matter from it similar to the watery substance of flesh.
And therefore if these signs appear to you, then abstain from the cure and the extraction of the arrow, unless it is already outside.
But the signs of the arrow when it lodges in the heart, are that it is near the left breast, and one feels as if it were fixed in something hard, and not in something empty; and very quickly the movement of the arrow is similar to the movement of the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound, and there is coldness of the extremities, and cold sweat, and fainting; and therefore know that death follows without any doubt.
And the signs when the arrow has wounded the lung are thus: frothy blood issues from the wound, and the vessels which are near the neck are inflamed, and the patient's color is changed, and he breathes with great difficulty, and desires the inhalation of cool air.
And if the arrow lodges in the diaphragm which is in the chest, then the pain is near the lower ribs, and the breathing is labored with intense pain and with sighing, and not all the muscles of the limbs are affected.
And if the arrow lodges in the stomach, then very quickly some undigested food comes from the wound.
And if the arrow lodges in the abdomen, and is fixed there, some tear comes from the wound, or the membrane, or intestine which is ruptured, and there is no hope of cure without extraction of the arrow.
And if the arrow lodges in the bladder, and urine flows out, and some matter progresses from it to the outer parts, and strong pain quickly comes upon the patient, then know that it is mortal.
But the other members, such as the face, the neck, and the throat, and the shoulders and the armpits, and the vertebrae of the back, and the clavicle, and the hip, and the leg and similar members, are saved according to the best dispositions when the arrow does not strike a vein or artery and is not a poisoned arrow.
And I recount this to you that I have seen of the nature of these arrows, so that you may signify from this about your cure.
The reason is that in one man an arrow fell into the tear duct of his eye at the root of his nose, and I extracted it for him from the other side, below the lobe of his ear, and he was healed, and no harm came to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew which had fallen into him in the lobe of his eye, below the eyelid, and the arrow was already hidden and I found only a small extremity with which it adhered to its shaft, and it was a large arrow of the sharp arrows of the Turks, with a lion-shaped head, in which there were no barbs.
And the Jew was healed, and no ill accident befell his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Christian man which had fallen into his throat, and it was an Arabic arrow, and it is one which has barbs; and then I incised upon it between two guides, and it was already submerged in his throat; and then I was subtle with it, and I extracted it, and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man which had fallen into his belly, and I thought that he would die from it; and therefore, since thirty days had passed and nothing had changed in his condition, I incised over the arrow and extracted it, and he was healed, and no ill accident befell him.
And I saw another man into whom an arrow had fallen in his back, and the wound had healed over him, and after about seven years, the arrow emerged at the root of the thigh.
And I saw a woman into whom an arrow had fallen in her belly, and the wound is healed within her, and nothing has changed in her condition, and the woman found no impediment in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man into whom an arrow had fallen in his face, and the wound is healed and the arrow broken, finding no great pain, and many similar to these.
And I extracted the arrow from the prince of the king's man that had fallen into the middle of his nose and had already inclined a little to the right side, and the whole arrow was hidden.
And I was called to his cure concerning the arrow wound within three days.
And then I found the wound from the arrow very narrow; and then I probed it with a slender probe, and did not feel it; but he felt a prickling under his right ear, and then I suspected that that prickling was from the point of the arrow.
And I plastered the area with a plaster which has attractive and digestive virtue, trusting that the area would fester so that the location of the arrow would appear to me, so that I could operate on it.
And no significant accident occurred in the area that the arrow had reached; and I continued with the plaster on him for many days and no accident occurred; and the wound was meanwhile consolidated, and the patient refrained from extraction for many days until one day he felt the arrow within his nose and reported this to me.
And then I applied a sharp corrosive medicine to the wound for many days until it was opened, and with a probe I examined it, and found the tip of the arrow, which was very thin and adhered to the shaft.
And then I carefully applied the sharp medicine to the opening of the wound until the point of the arrow appeared visibly to me, and I spent time with this for a period of up to four months.
And then, when the wound was enlarged, and it was possible for me to insert forceps into it, and remove it, and I did not succeed in this way, then I did not cease to refine and devise methods for this, until one day I managed with ingeniously made forceps, as the form of these will be shown at the end of the chapter, to extract it; and then I restored the wound.
And some doctors judged that the cartilage of the nose would not be restored, but I restored it and the wound is healed, and the patient is completely healthy, and he suffered no ill effects.
And I recounted to you the method of extraction of certain arrows so that you may establish a rule and understanding on this matter that I have not described, for the details of this art and its definitions are not comprehensively covered in narrative or included in this book.
And skillful artisans compare the small with the large; and the present with the absent; and find new operations and instruments because of the unforeseen occurrences of strange causes in the art.
Therefore, I say that arrows are not expelled from the members into which they are fixed except in two ways: either by attraction from the place where they are lodged; or by the opposite action from the other side.
And that which is expelled from the place where it entered, is an arrow embedded in fleshy tissue, and is drawn out and expelled.
And if it does not yield to removal at the time it is discovered, then it is necessary that you leave it for some days until the flesh surrounding it softens, and its extraction and removal are facilitated.
And similarly, if it is embedded in bone and does not yield to removal, then leave it for some days and repeatedly attempt its extraction and movement each day, and eventually it will be expelled.
And if it does not yield to removal after some days, then it is necessary that you perforate the area around the arrow in that bone on all sides with a thin drill until the area around the arrow is widened, and then extract it.
But if the arrow is hidden in the body and is hidden from sight, then you should search for it with a probe, and if you feel it, then draw it out with some instrument that is suitable for its extraction.
And if you cannot reach it because of the tightness of the wound and the length of the projectile, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein there, then you should incise the wound until it is enlarged enough to allow the arrow to be extracted.
And if it is held by barbs, then you should free the flesh in which it is embedded from all sides with all the skill you can muster; and attempt this, if you cannot otherwise, by the excision of the flesh at the barbs and by twisting them until they yield.
And when you perform the extraction of the arrow, wherever it may be, then use twisting with your hand and forceps in all parts until it yields; and proceed with great subtlety so that the arrow is not broken and that a difficult extraction is not made for you.
And if you cannot manage it immediately, then you should wait for a few days until the parts surrounding it can soften, and then try again, for it will then be easier. But if you encounter excessive bleeding, then you should apply the treatments we described in the relevant chapter.
And be careful about cutting a vein or nerve, or tendon, and use all possible skill to remove the arrow; and do this with ease, calmness, slowness and firmness, as I have recommended to you.
And therefore you should make a counter-incision so that the patient is aligned with the position of the arrow, because that is more helpful. But if that is not possible, then administer what you can, according to the circumstances.
But if arrows are protruding from the opposite side, or if any part of it projects outwards, or if the tip of the arrow causes swelling due to the feeling in the part above the deep wound and sometimes that prominence; then you should make an incision there; and the incision should be the size that the forceps can grasp; and then pull it out, for it will then be easily removed.
But if it is lodged in the bone, then twist the blade against it until the arrow is loosened from the bone, and widen the opening yourself, and then extract it.
And if not, leave it for a few days, and then try again until it comes out.
And if the shaft of the arrow is within, then push it all the way through.
But if the shaft has fallen off and you want to administer a push, then introduce, if it is blunt, a concave instrument so that the concavity fits onto the arrowhead, and then push it through.
But if the arrowhead is concave, then push it through with a strong instrument entering it, because the arrow is aided by this.
And if the arrow is poisoned, then you must carefully remove all the flesh in which the poison is, if it is possible for you to do so, and then treat it with what is appropriate.
And if the arrow is lodged in the foot, or in the belly, or in the bladder, or in the side, and is close enough that you can touch it with a probe, and it is possible for you to cut it out, then cut it out and be careful to avoid cutting a vein or nerve, and remove it, and then dress the wound if necessary, and treat it until it is healed.
The form of forceps with which arrows are extracted:
Let the ends of their jaws be like the beak of a bird, made like a saw when they grip the arrow so that they do not let go of it.
And of these there are many types, large, small, and medium-sized, according to the size of the arrow and its smallness, and the size of the wound, and the tightness of the location.
like a ray, so that its entry into the concave arrow is eased and it is pushed with that.
Of phlebotomy of the veins.
The veins from which it is customary to perform phlebotomy are thirty, of which sixteen are in the head; that is, two pulsating veins which are near the ears, called *alhasisain*; two veins that are in the two apparent temples; and two veins that are in the two tear ducts of the two eyes, which are called *aspicientz*; and the vein prepared in the middle of the forehead, and the vein that is at the end of the nose; and two veins that are at the neck; and two veins that are in the upper lip; and two veins that are in the lower lip; and — these are the veins that are called *algelic* —; and there are two veins that are under the tongue. But the veins that are phlebotomized in the arms are five; for of these there is one cephalic, and it is next to the wild vein; and the people call it "the vein of the head", and it is a thick vein and is composed of a branch of the basilic, and of another branch of the cephalic; and the people name that one "vein of the whole body", and the basilic is the vein which is situated in the domestic vein, and is named "citillar", and the common people call it "vein of the belly"; and the vein of the arm, and it is situated on the forearm, and it is that in which phlebotomy is performed, and it is that which appears above the elbow by manifest appearance; and the *salvatella*, and it is the vein that is between the auricular finger and the little finger, and it has two branches in it.
And in the foot and leg there are three veins; one of these is below the curvature of the knee of the wild vein; and the second is the saphena, and its location is below the leg of the domestic vein; and the sciatic vein, and its location is below the leg of the wild vein; and in the leg there are other veins similar to those.
The benefit of opening the veins near the ears is for chronic catarrhs, migraine, headaches, and old, malignant head ulcers.
And the method of bleeding those veins is as I have recounted to you: it is that the head of the patient should be raised, and the patient's head should be rubbed at the occiput in the area of both veins with a wide cloth with good friction; then the patient should tighten their neck with a bandage until the two veins and their locations appear near the ears in two depressed places of the head; and seek them with your finger, and if you feel the pulse of those veins with your finger, bleed them there cautiously.
Then you take the lancet knife, which is sharp, and you insert it under the vein in the incision until the blood comes out. Then raise your hand with the vein and the incision to the upper parts and incise the vein with a sharp incision. And let the length of the incision be the amount of two fingers joined, or thereabouts; and let blood flow as much as you wish.
Then bind it with powders and leave it until it is healed.
And already, at the beginning of the book, the incision of these and their cauterization has been described.
But for the two arteries in the temples, the benefit of phlebotomy is for chronic and sudden severe migraine, and persistent ophthalmia, and a flow of acute superfluities effused into the eyes.
And the way to phlebotomize those is as I recounted to you:  constrain the patient's neck so that the two veins appear to the touch by manifest appearance and their pulse is felt beneath your finger; and then you bleed with a lancet.
Then you raise the skin above the vein at the upper part with your index finger, and insert the phlebotomy instrument underneath, and raise the vein upward; and incise it just as you did the other two veins; and let the blood flow according to your need.
And then loosen the patient's constriction, and place your finger on the vein for an hour, and then place cotton and powder on it and bind it tightly above, and leave it until it is healed.
And already the recollection of them, and their incision, and the drawing (of blood) has been preceded at the beginning of the book.
Therefore, bloodletting from the frontal vein is useful for old facial ailments, and for migraine, redness, and foul-smelling ulcers.
And the manner of his phlebotomy is as I relate to you; that is, that the patient should tighten his neck with a bandage until the vein appears; and then take the instrument which is called a fossor, of which this is the form:
Place the prominent point, which is at the head of the fossor, upon that vein and strike it with a hammer or other similar object, and let the blood flow according to the quantity you desire; and then loosen the patient's binding and tighten it until it is healed.
And sometimes it is opened with a lancet, however, it is not fitting that the lancet be of a sharp point like the other lancets, rather it should be of a broad point, and bleed according to the crosswise direction, because the bone is near and would easily be broken by the lancet when it is sharp.
But bloodletting from the two veins that are at the lacrimal ducts of the two eyes is useful for scabies of the eyes, for redness and swelling in the eyes, and for facial ailments.
And the manner of the phlebotomy is that the patient should tighten his neck with a bandage, and then you should bleed him, and you should be standing above his head.
And let the phlebotomy be done transversely rather than lengthwise, with a small lancet, because in that place there is no flesh; and if the lancet is sharp, its point will be easily broken.
And then make the blood flow as much as is necessary for you, and place cotton on it, and bind it for a night, and then unbind it.
But phlebotomy of the vein of the nose is beneficial for acute fever and severe headache, and for facial ailments, migraine, and redness that occurs in the face, especially when it is chronic.
And the manner of his phlebotomy is that the patient should tighten his neck and then hold his nose with your left hand, and take a long, thin lancet and insert it into the middle of the nasal bridge in a straight line, because the vein is not palpable there, and because the blood will immediately begin to flow.
So that you gently move your hand with the small lancet and let the blood flow according to your need; and then bind it for a night, for it is lightly restored.
But bloodletting from the two jugular veins is helpful for the constriction of the throat, the beginning of leprosy, many diseases, impetigo, and bad, corrosive ulcers, as well as herpes.
And the manner of bloodletting from these veins is that the patient should tighten each one with a bandage below his neck; and the surgeon should stand above the patient's head, and the patient should be seated on a stool.
Then bleed the vein lengthwise with a wide lancet little by little; and then extract blood according to a measured quantity or according to what the need requires; and then do likewise with the other vein.
Then untie the bandage and tighten the two veins with a moderate constriction so that the patient is not suffocated, and leave it until morning, for the wound is healed.

Concerning cupping and the qualities of using them.
Cupping glasses are made of horn, or of brass, or of glass.
And cupping is done in two ways: one of them is with scarification and blood extraction, and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping that is without scarification is done in two ways, for it is either with fire or without fire.
And the cupping glasses that are administered with scarification and blood extraction are applied to fourteen places of the body, of which one in which cupping glasses are placed is the occiput, and that is the back part of the head; and the middle of the neck on both sides together; and cupping glasses on the chin below the jaw, below the mouth; and cupping glasses on the two shoulder blades; and cupping glasses on the hypochondrium, and that is above the end of the ribs; and cupping glasses on the two flanks, and these are on the two sides; and cupping glasses on the two hips; and cupping glasses on the two thighs.
And cupping does not extract blood except from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and because of this it does not diminish the vital force in the same way as it does with phlebotomy; nor should cupping be done with or without fire in any illness caused by repletion until the whole body is evacuated.
But if necessity requires cupping for illness or for a continuous condition, we administer it at any time, at the beginning of the month and at the end of it, and in the middle of it, and at any time.
Therefore, when you see any of these conditions, and especially if it is in the third or middle part of the month, then we order that cupping be done after two or three hours of the day have passed.
And the benefit of cupping the nape of the neck is that when it is applied, it relieves heaviness in the head and that which is settled in the eyes; but this should be done after evacuation of the whole body.
And this cupping is in the place of phlebotomy of the cephalic vein.
And it is not advisable to use it on one who is of cold constitution, or in whom there is a catarrh, for it causes great harm to such a person.
And therefore it is not advisable to use this on the elderly, and on those whose heads are afflicted with cold illnesses.
But to the one on whom this is continuously applied, forgetfulness is caused; and therefore you must instruct the cupper to place his hand slightly below the cupping area, fearing the production of forgetfulness.
But cupping with alkahel (likely referring to a specific substance or method) is in the place of phlebotomy of the dark vein (vena fusca) and the basilic vein: and therefore it benefits asthma, and the constriction of the throat, and the tearing of the throat's muscles, and cough, and fullness.
And it is necessary that the alkahel cupping be applied slightly higher, for if it is placed lower, it produces weakness in the body and in the stomach.
And cupping with alhagdahan (likely another specific substance or method) benefits accidental pains in the head and ophthalmia, and migraine, and suffocation, and pains in the roots of the teeth; and it is in place of phlebotomy of the basilic vein.
And it is necessary that you instruct the cupper not to press his hand during scarification, lest he cut the artery and cause a flow of blood.
And cupping below the chin benefits ulcers in the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and similar oral diseases, and is in place of phlebotomy of the veins in the two lips (aliheric likely refers to the labial veins).
But cupping that is done over the two shoulder blades benefits caphatan (likely a specific ailment or symptom), which is caused by an excess of heat.
And cupping of the two inner parts of the ankles benefits as much as phlebotomy of three veins: that is, the basilic, median cubital (fusca likely refers to this, as it is a darker vein), and cephalic veins; because it draws blood from all those smaller veins that are in the flesh, and those small veins from the other larger veins until the attraction reaches the three veins.
And instruct the cupper not to proceed excessively in scarification, because the area is devoid of flesh, and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
And there is a cupping glass with which one cups over the buttocks, and it benefits hemorrhoids of the anus and deeper ulcers.
And it is necessary that you instruct the cupper that it be a large cupping glass, and that it be of brass, because the location requires strong suction; and thin cupping glasses are quickly detached and should be scarified with scarification.
But cupping of the legs diminishes pain through manifest depletion, for they draw blood from the whole body, and benefit chronic pains of the joints and of the uterus, and of the bladder; and they provoke menstruation, and benefit boils and swellings; and they stand in place of phlebotomy of the two saphenous veins, and of the two popliteal veins (alahorcoben likely refers to the popliteal space).
However, they greatly weaken the whole body and cause syncope in many men.
And of the popliteal cuppings, the benefit is similar to the benefit of cuppings of the two legs.
And the quality of the placement of the cupping glasses is that you place the cupping glass initially empty and draw it by moderate suction, and do not prolong the placement of the cupping glasses, but place them lightly, and remove them carefully, so that the humors come to the place by equal distribution. And do not cease to iterate this and continue until you see the place redden and swell and the redness of the blood appear.
And then you scarify and repeat the suction little by little, and consider the patient's constitution.
Because that man who is of tender flesh and rare pores, it is necessary that you scarify him once and no more, lest the place be ulcerated.
And instruct the cupper to enlarge the scarification a little, and to moderate the suction with ease.
And if the blood is thick, then it is necessary that you scarify twice, for in the first time the way of thinning is opened to the blood and to its watery part. But in the second [scarification], to the thorough investigation acquired by the extraction of the thick blood.
And if the blood is very foul, then let a third scarification be made so that it achieves the desired outcome/effect.
Finally, when we want to extract the blood little by little or a small amount, one scarification is enough for us.
And if we want extraction of much blood, we make many scarifications; and if we know that the blood is thick, then it is necessary that we scarify with a deep scarification.
And the term "scarification" refers only to the depth of the incision.
And if the one whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are tight, it is necessary that the places for cupping be anointed with emollient, aperient, and resolvent ointments; if it is in summer, such as aloe oil, or violet oil, or sweet almond oil, or poppy seed oil; and if it is in winter, then narcissus oil, or lily oil, or chamomile oil, or blood oil, and similar ones.
And if the excess [humor] is thick and cold, then the oil should be amaranth oil, or balm mint oil, or aloe oil, or anise oil, and similar ones.
But if the patient has large pores and tender flesh, then the use of oil should be prohibited, and for such patients, the cupping sites should be washed with vinegar or cold water with henbane, or poppy water, or bordelais water, and similar things.
And he whose blood is of excessive humidity, wash his cupping sites with sour wine, or with myrtle and sumac water, and similar things.
And for the one whose superfluities are thick, wash his cupping sites with old wine, or with  water of self-heal (or sanicle), which is majoram, or a decoction of anise, or chamomile, and similar things.
And the cupping should be done before a bath; rather, it should be administered about an hour or two before the bath, and he should sleep after the cupping.
What is it that the person to be cupped or bled ought to observe before cupping?
It is necessary to consider at the beginning: if the person to be cupped or bled is choleric and has sharp blood and inflammation, then he should take cooling things, such as sour pomegranates, and endive in sour wine, and lettuce, and dried borage, and julep, and similar things.
And give him foods such as poultry, and the meat of domestic birds, and foods seasoned with acids, and the like.
And for the one whose complexion is cold, he should drink syrup of almonds, or syrup of dried borage seeds, and sumac, and very fragrant wine between the months of November and December; and he should be advised to eat and drink sparingly, and give him foods such as poultry and hempseed and peas and small birds and doves.
And it is necessary that on the day of cupping and bloodletting, his drink should be greater than his food.
And it is necessary that at certain hours, some men be given theriac alpharac, or medicines of musk, that is, nutmeg, or theriac before cupping or before bloodletting or afterwards, so that the principal members are comforted and the blood is thinned; and they should not take those things hot.
But the cuppings that are without scarification should be cuppings that are placed on the liver, and on the spleen and gout, and on the navel, in the areas of the knees, and the urinary bladder, because these members do not tolerate scarification on themselves.
And it is not desired for those [members] except the drawing of blood from a member, just as we place a cupping glass on the breast in the illness of a blood flow from the nose; and we use this so that cold, invisible windiness of the member is dissolved, just as we place it on the belly and navel; because this rarefies the member and warms it and reduces the pain because it resolves windiness.
And it is placed on the bronchi when they suffer from obstruction or swelling, for by virtue of strong attraction it easily opens the obstruction and removes the swelling from its place.
And similarly it acts when it is placed on the liver and spleen because of windiness enclosed within them.
And these cuppings are administered empty with suction alone; and sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of lukewarm water in illnesses of the spleen.
And this is so that the cupping glass is filled and is larger; that is, with hot water alone or water in which herbs suitable for this purpose have been boiled.
Method of cupping administered with fire: let the width of the opening be two fingers, according to how we have formed it; and the quantity of the depth be half a palm; and let it be on the side of him, in the place that is in the circumference, half of him, the hole be small according to the quantity that a needle enters into it.
Let it be made of bronze, copper, or citrus [possibly referring to a type of metal or alloy], with thick edges, smooth, even, and polished so that it does not injure the limb because of its placement on it.
And let there be in the middle of it a transverse bar of bronze or iron, on which a candle with fire is placed.
And now let this cupping glass be larger than the one we have described; and the smaller one smaller than that, according to the illnesses and the age of the one who uses it.
And the cupping glasses for infants and thin people are different from the cupping glasses for young people and those with large bodies.
And the manner of placing this cupping glass with fire on the limb is that you should extend the wick with a decent wood fire before a small wax candle, and place that on the middle of the transverse bar in the middle of the cupping glass, so that the fire is lit around the area underneath, so that the patient's body is not burned.
And then it should be placed on the patient's limb, and the finger should be on the hole that we have mentioned, until when we hold the cupping glass and do not need to remove the finger and the vapor escapes through that hole, then the cupping glass will immediately be detached.
And then ignite the wick according to its nature, and repeat this if you need to.
But a cupping glass that is used in the illness of pleurisy, with water, which has no transverse bar or hole, and is not used unless it is filled with water, and is placed on the limb only.

Chapter 97.
Leech application is not administered to many limbs, but only to those limbs where the application of cupping glasses is not possible, because of their small size, such as the fingers and gums and similar; or to a limb devoid of flesh, such as the fingers and nose and similar.
And the quality of using those leeches is that you have found leeches that are in clean, sweet waters of a stagnant pool.
And then they should be left day and night in fresh water until they are hungry, and so that no matter remains in their bellies; and then the body should be evacuated at the beginning with phlebotomy and cupping glasses; and then rub the patient's limb until it reddens; and then they should be applied to it.
And when they are full and fall off.
And if it is possible to draw blood from the area with a cupping glass, it is even more beneficial.
And if not, then wash the area with wine vinegar and then with lukewarm water; and it should be rubbed and squeezed.
And if the flow of blood is prolonged near the application of the leeches, and it is oozing, then you dip a linen cloth in cold water, and place that on top until the oozing is stopped.
And if the bleeding is excessive, then powder crushed vitriol, or gallnuts and similar styptic substances, over it until the bleeding stops; or  halves of roasted beans are placed on the area, and left until the beans adhere to the place, and the bleeding is stopped.
And it is necessary, if needed, to iterate the leeches, when it is possible to change them.
And if the leeches are prevented from attaching, then rub the area with fresh blood or prick the area with a needle until blood appears; and then apply them, because when they sense blood they immediately attach.
And when you want them to fall off, powder a little crushed aloes, or salt, or ash, on them, and they will immediately fall off.
Thus begins the prologue of the third part: On Restoration. This chapter is often necessary in the art of medicine; and it is the restoration of fractures and accidental dislocations in the bones.
Oh, son, know that foolish physicians and the common people boast of this chapter, those who never looked at the books of the Ancients nor read a single letter of them.
And for this reason, the manner of the learned in our land is kept private; and I have never forgotten this at any time; and I have not acquired this knowledge except through long reading of the books of the Ancients and study of their understanding, until I extracted the science from them.
And I have applied myself to experimentation and study throughout my life.
And I have described in this chapter all that my knowledge contains and upon which my experience is based; since I have abridged it for you and freed it from lengthy occupation; and I have abridged it for the sake of brevity; and I have shown it for the sake of clarity; and I have formed in it many forms of instruments that are administered in it, as is done by way of added explanation, just as I have done in the two preceding chapters.
Chapter One.
Summaries and collections concerning bone fractures, those which are necessary to preface before we begin the discussion of broken limbs and their various types. Therefore, I will say in the prologue of this chapter the summaries of the discourse; and the chapters concerning the understanding of which, at the beginning, the artisan and practitioner must be grounded in the truth of these things; that is, you and those who are ready to know or learn this noble art other than yourselves.
Therefore I say that when a fracture, dislocation, sprain, or fall occurs to someone, then you must initially attend to it with phlebotomy or purging, or both together, unless some cause prohibits it, such as weakness of constitution, or if the one to whom it happened is a child, or an old person in decrepitude, or if it is a time of intense heat or intense cold.
And furthermore, let him be content with a diet of cold herbs and the flesh of birds and rabbits; and we prohibit rich meats and filling foods and all food that fills the veins with blood, until you are sure of a hot abscess, and if you do not fear an effusion of matter in the limb.
And then return the patient to their former regimen, with which they are accustomed.
And so, as the broken bone begins to be restored, then the patient should be fed nutritious foods, thick, heavy, and viscous, such as foods made from wheat boiled in water, and rice, and heads [presumably heads of grain], and feet [likely animal feet], and bellies of cows, and eggs, and fresh fish, and thick wine, and similar things; and with this regimen, the consolidation of the fracture is made more lightly and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in men who are in their first youth and in the decrepit, cannot be rejoined and consolidated according to their nature because of the dryness of their limbs.
However, nature adjusts and brings to the broken bone from every part some substance similar to glue, in which there is thickness that adheres and binds it until they are completely conjoined and bound together, until they reach the end of strength and firmness, like fire at the beginning, until some cause does not prohibit it from its work.
And for this reason it is necessary that you give the patient foods in which there is heaviness, viscosity, and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the types of fracture are diverse according to the diversity of the limbs, for a fracture of the bone of the leg is different from a fracture of the bone of the head; and a fracture of the bone of the foot is different from a fracture of the bone of the arm; and similarly all the other limbs are different from each other.
And I come with a remembrance of every type of fracture, explained in its own distinct chapter.
Many different kinds of fracture in themselves. And the types of bone fracture are diversified in themselves; for there is an arcuate fracture, besides those that happen in the stars; and sometimes a fracture is made according to the length of the bone: and there are fractures of the sagitta (arrow) and stars and additions proceeding and not proceeding, just as it is done in the pen when it is prepared to write, and there is a fracture with a wound and disruption in the head, and there is a small fissure fracture.
And to each type belongs its own appropriate remedy or treatment in its restoration, as will be its remembrance distinctly in its own place [chapter].
Many signs of bone fracture. And by this a bone fracture is known: its crookedness, and the prominence and appearance of it to the touch, and that which we perceive when we compress it with the hand.
And therefore, when there is no apparent crookedness in the place, nor is anything felt by your touch when you touch the bone with agitation and the patient does not find great pain, then there is no fracture here.
However, it is possible that there is a crookedness or small fissure, so it is not necessary that you move it with your hand and with complete compression; however, place upon it the medicines of which you will find mention near this point which is suitable for the place; and then let it be held tightly by a subtle bandage.
And know that if the place is curved and is not split in two, or another thing which does not happen to it as a result of being moved, as if the whole part were separated from its counterpart, then you should begin at your leisure to rectify it before an abscess occurs there.
And if a hot abscess occurs there, then leave it for some days until the abscess ceases; and then dress it according to whatever manner you can, with lightness and skill.
And know that the restoration and healing of it is easier than of a bone in which a wound has occurred.
And then bind it as the remedy for it suggests.
But if the bone is wounded, then extension of that broken limb is necessary from two sides, if it is the hand or the foot; or with your hand if the limb is small; or with two cords or with one cord and your hand.
And let your position be such that you place the limb in a dressing according to its natural shape; while the parts of the broken limb are extended, then you must make those additions return to their places with skill and in whatever way you can.
And take care, according to your strength, that no pain or injury to the patient occurs through your operation; and strive to the best of your ability to apply one bone to its counterpart, according to their best fit.
And it is necessary that at that time you skillfully touch that with your hand, and if you see anything amiss there, rectify it and dress it to the best of your ability.
And avoid vehement extension and compression, as many foolish physicians do, who often cause a hot abscess by their operation, or cause a contraction in the limb, as we have seen happen through their operation many times.
And the quality of the binding of the quieted limb is that which I recommend; so that you know that broken limbs are diversified in their smallness and in their largeness and in their form.
And for those that are small, such as the arm and the finger and the penis and similar to those, then the wrapping should be of very fine soft cloth.
But for those that are large, such as the thigh or the leg, there should be a broad and firm wrapping, because the broad bandage connects the large limb and binds it on all sides with an even constriction, so that no blemish enters it.
Then begin, after your equalization evacuation, to place a bandage suitable for this, over the place of the fracture with three or four wrappings depending on what the limb requires; and tighten your hand a little with the bandage.
And then come with that to the most superior part of the fracture site, and tighten less than you did your binding with which you bound the fracture site. And then lengthen yourself with a wrapping of the fracture site a little, and soften the binding little by little, until you take from the site a large part.
And then take another bandage and wrap it again over the broken place with a wrapping, and then come with the wrappings to the lower part of the fracture; and let your operation in the tightening of the wrapping and in the softening of that be as we have said in the first wrapping above.
And then place between the layers of bandage linen or cloth with which the crookedness of the fracture is padded, if there is crookedness in it; and if not, do not place anything there.
And then place another bandage over it, and then bind over these bandages well-made splints immediately, if there is no inflammation or hot abscess in the place, and then place on it that which dries the abscess and which reduces the inflammation; and leave that for some days; and then you tighten the splints over it.
And let the splints be of half a reed of smooth, wisely-formed cane; or let them be of pine wood; or of palm branches; and let the width of each splint be according to this form and this figure:
However, it is necessary that the splint which is placed over the fracture be a little thicker and smoother than the other splints, and the length of the splint according to how much it is needed for the limb in size and smallness; and then tighten over the splints with another bandage according to your first tightening; and then bind over it with thread decently as we have said of tightening, and it is so that your tightening should be greatest at the place of the fracture, and the farther it is from the fracture, the less the tightening.
And it is necessary that the threads be moderate in thickness and fineness, and be of linen; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen by the operation of some fools placing their threads of twisted rope, and it is a great error, when the tightening lacks equality.
And likewise, very fine threads are not convenient, because you will not be able to achieve with them what you want by tightening, and it is not necessary that there be less than a finger's breadth between splint and splint.
And if the patient is harmed by the ends of the splints near the binding in those places, then place under those some carded flax or wool until it no longer causes him harm.
Sometimes a fracture is accompanied by a wound, and a disruption occurs in the skin, and I will return with a discussion of that in the chapter according to its singularity.
And know that not every broken limb is bound with splints on the first day; the reason for this is that when the limb is large, it is not necessary to place splints on it until about five days or seven or more, according to your assurance of the advent of a hot abscess.
And this is the manner of the plasters with which the Ancients restored, which are placed on fractures, dislocations, and sprains.
A method of common plaster, a shortening of the restoration of fractures, suitable for many complexions and especially for women and children, because it is of that which neither heat nor cold overcomes: and it is that you take powdered mill dust, and this is the pith of the flour that falls from the movement of the millstone against the walls of the mill.
And make it thus as it is, except that you sift it with egg white, and make the confection of it neither thick nor thin; and then use it.
