Another method of plaster restoring fractures, dislocations, and sprains: take of asafoetida, of labdanum, of acacia, of elecampane, and wild pomegranate root, of each one dram; of myrrh and aloes, half a dram; of aloes wood, two drams; all should be crushed and sifted and mixed with aloe water or with egg white if the patient's complexion is warm; and then use this plaster, for it is of good composition and restores lightly broken bones, and benefits many men.
Another method of plaster which benefits fractures and sprains: take wild pomegranate root and marshmallow root, one dram; of myrrh and aloes, half a dram; of acacia, six drams; of Armenian bole, two drams.
All should be crushed in a mortar with much grinding, and be sifted, and be mixed with water or with egg white and be administered.
A method of plaster for joints and bones separated from their places, and if pain occurs in those and in fissures and fractures: let soiled wool be taken and submerged in sour wine and boiled oil and be placed on the area.
But in this plaster there is no restorative virtue, it rather draws out hot abscesses and expels pain.
Another method of plaster that restores a broken bone: take fresh fig leaves and wild poppy leaves; pound everything and make a plaster from them.
A method of a brief plaster administered for the restoration of a bone fracture and when you want resolution of the remainder of an abscess: take roots of marshmallow, chamomile, and violet flowers,  pea flour, of each one part; let all be crushed and mixed with wine, if the limb is not inflamed.
But if it is inflamed, then mix that with moist coriander water or with water and use it.
Another method of a stronger plaster for resolution than the first, when a hard abscess occurs during the restoration of a limb: take roots of marshmallow and flaxseed, and pomegranate, and melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, of each one equal part; let all be crushed and mixed with willow water or with fresh water or with wine, all this according to the quality of the limb and the restoration of its color.
However, Hippocrates did not say in his book that it should be placed on the broken limb during its restoration except a cerate made of wax and oil and nothing else; and he recommends that it be of medium consistency between thickness and thinness.
But in Wales it is seen that they are placed on the broken limb during its restoration, and the substances which are drying with little heat are like myrrh, incense, and similar to those substances which we have mentioned.
Method for a plaster that helps with injury and pain: take millet, linseed, shaved human hair or bird down, and marshmallow, and salt, in equal parts; let them be crushed and sifted and mixed, and let a plaster be made.
But the amount of time the bandage should remain and when it should be loosened, is that you should consider if no pain or itching has occurred and the bone is not broken or dislocated; then you should not loosen it for many days. And if severe pain, itching, or inflammation occurs at the site, then you should carefully loosen or unbind it immediately, and remove the plaster.
And take linen cloth and a moist sea sponge, and submerge it in lukewarm water, and with it wash the area until the itching ceases and the pain is calmed; and then let the limb rest for an hour; and then place on it clean wool submerged in sour wine and oil or rose oil, and bind it on for a night until you are sure of a hot abscess, and that the inflammation of the limb is reduced, and its pain is eased.
And then repeat that subtle binding and small plaster, and do not tighten it as much as your first constriction, but refine it until it is healed.
And if you see that the abscess, redness, pain, inflammation, and all the symptoms are completely reduced, and you judge that plaster and binding are necessary, then you should repeat it in the same way as you did at the beginning.
And if none of the conditions we have mentioned occur in the limb, then do not unbind it before three or four or five or seven days; and sometimes all this is left for twenty days, depending on what appears to you upon unbinding the limb, as we have said, until the fracture adheres, and approaches the coagulation of the flesh upon it.
And then adjust the binding more tightly than in your first binding, and adjust it to the thickening of the patient's flesh, as the healing progresses.
But if you see the site of the fracture drying and thinning more than is appropriate, then you should know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it; and therefore, moisten it with lukewarm water every time you unbind it; and do this every three days; and loosen the binding a little, and through this operation, nourishment will flow to the limb, and it will heal easily.
But what foolish restorers of a fractured limb do, when it is not set correctly at the beginning, and is set crookedly, is a mistake according to their practice, and a great danger, and if it were right, the Ancients would have enlarged upon it in their books and would have worked with it.
And I have not seen anyone among them do this, wherefore it is more reasonable not to do it.
Chapter Two: On fractures when they occur in the head.
The types of fractures are many, and their forms are diverse, and the causes are distinct, for some fractures are made by a blow from a sword, and the blow breaks the bone completely into pieces, which are below the bone, just as it happens in a tree trunk, and for this reason this type is called *ascial* (a type of fracture); or the sword cuts into a part of the bone, and cuts into the flesh but the incision does not pass to the back of it; and this type of fracture is called *levatio absoluta* (a type of fracture).
And the wound of these two fractures is either large or small, and of a fracture that is comminuted or attrited; and its cause is a blow with a blunt object or a fall on a stone, and similar things.
And this fracture is either penetrating, approaching the periosteum which is under the bone; or it is on the surface of the bone, and the wound of these two fractures is either large or small.
And there is a fracture that is hidden in the bone, as fine as a small hair, and for this reason this type is called a capillary fracture.
And there is a fracture that is made either by a fall or by a blow from a stone and the like, which causes some lamina of the bone to enter the inner parts, and is made in a concave place, just as happens to the thin plates of bronze when they receive a blow and a part of them is driven into the inner parts.
And this happens mostly in the bodies of moist bones, such as the skulls of infants.
And in all these types of comminuted fractures, the pieces are like separated stars. And I will bring everything back to its place with repositioning.
And we will know all these types of fractures by detection on those and by investigation of them with probes and removal of corrupted flesh that is on them.
And a hairline fracture is known because it is uncovered on the bone, and is cleaned, and we pass a probe over it, because the fracture appears black.
But in the treatment of a fracture, consider the patient's symptoms first, and then if you see by the symptoms that it signifies by manifest signs of danger, such as vomiting of bile, and prostration, and loss of consciousness, and loss of voice, and syncope, and acute fever, and swelling of the eyes and redness of them, and similar symptoms in greater intensity, without doubt it is not healing.
And if you see no unusual symptoms, and you expect recovery, then you begin its treatment.
The reason is that if the wounded person has come to you at the beginning as wounded, and this is in the winter days, then you must study the setting of the bone before the fourteenth day, according to all conditions.
And if it is in the summer days, then you must attempt to set the bone before the seventh day, before what is under the bone is corrupted by the pus, and before the symptoms that we have mentioned occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the membrane covering the brain, and is with small, ground-down pieces, then you must incise the comminuted and ground-down part, as I shall explain to you.
And so you shave the head of the wounded patient, and uncover the bone according to custom and manner as much as possible according to the shape of the wound, and that it is light on the patient.
And if you encounter, after the uncovering of the bone, a flow of blood or a hot abscess, then you oppose it with what is necessary, and that is to fill the place with cloths soaked in rose-wine and oil, and let the wound rest until the abscess is settled and you are sure of the flow of blood; and then begin with the circling of the bone and its removal.
And this should be done according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you incise the bone with a narrow incisor, and this is its shape:  And then use another incisor like this one, a little wider than that one; and this is its shape:
And then use yet wider incisors than the second.
And it is necessary that you have several different incisors, some of which are wider than others; and some of them are shorter than others, and let their ends be sharp, and let them be of Indian or hardened iron.
And use them easily with percussion on the incisor so that you do not hit the head and injure it.
And if the bone is strong, hard, then it is necessary that you perforate its circumference before you use the incisors with the drills, which are called non-penetrating drills.
And they are not called so except because those do not reach the limit of the skull bone next to it, because the drill has a round end or edge on what is below its sharp head, similar to an edge or a small circle, which prevents it from being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take a large number of these drills, each suitable for the thickness of each bone, so that you have at your disposal for all parts of the skull a drill whose end is sharp, the length and shortness being according to the thickness of that part of the skull.
And this is the method for three types of drills:  The way to perforate the circumference of the broken bone is to place the drill on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated; then move the drill to another location, and place them so that the distance between each hole is the width of a finger or thereabouts.
Then incise with incisors what is between each hole in the bone, and make it as thin as possible in order to remove it easily, as we have said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand or with some other instrument that you will have prepared for this, such as small pincers and forceps.
And you must take great care that the incisor does not touch anything in the brain.
Then, when you have encircled the bone by drilling, and it is separated from the brain if it is continuous with it, and you have extracted it, you must scrape and smooth its roughness and any remaining bone with another instrument similar to an incisor, but it must be thinner and finer than the other incisors.
And if any small, splintered bone fragments remain, then remove them easily with whatever instrument you have; and then treat the wound with liniments and ointments that we have mentioned.
And the other method of operation is easier, less frightening, which Galen and others praise highly; and this is his discourse: it is necessary for you at the beginning to begin to uncover the part of the bone at the location where the fracture is most severe and most evident, until, when you have uncovered that part and have placed under it the tip of this slow and careful incisor, of which this is the form:
And it is such that the lenticular part is broad and does not cut anything, and the sharp part is divided into two edges lengthwise, like a saw; so that the lenticular part is applied to the brain with its surface, the sharp edge cutting into the bone.
Then strike the incisor on one side with a small mallet until you have incised all the bone easily as it is turned, and you are certain of the periosteum so that no accident occurs in any way, even if the physician were the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, even if he were asleep.
Then, if any matter attached to the bone remains in some part of the bone, incise it with the lenticular end of that incisor, and remove it easily, for it is removed without harm and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate the periosteum, and what is severed or removed from the bone is its surface, and roughness and small splinters remain, then you must shave off that roughness and remove those splinters with fine rasps, of which you have prepared a number, of different sizes, so that you may be able to use the most suitable ones in each place and the most appropriate of them according to the way you carry out your operation on that broken bone and its shape.
And you must use in your work with which you rasp the bone, at first the broadest of those rasps, and then use successively the finer ones, and do not cease to do this continuously until you come to using the finest and most delicate of all those.
But in all other small, hairline fractures and subtle fractures, you must administer treatment according to what you are doing to rectify them.
And it is a matter that is not hidden from all those who have studied this art. And this is what we have shown in writing concerning large fractures.
And if the panniculus (skin and subcutaneous tissue) is retracted when you reduce the bone, then you must take linen cloth according to the size of the wound, and immerse it in rose oil, and place that over the opening of the wound; and then take another folded or tripled cloth and immerse it in wine and rose oil and place that over the first cloth and do this as gently as you can, so that you do not press on the wound; and then apply a broad bandage over that, and do not bind it tightly but only enough to hold the wound in place.
And leave it with this treatment for one or two days until you are sure there is no hot abscess; and then unbind it and then apply medicines that draw out excess moisture, such as flaxseed, barley flour, and powder of henbane and birthwort, and similar things.
And it is that you make a powder of these medicines to be sprinkled on the wound, once they are dry; and in general, apply to it any medicine whose property it is to cleanse and not to bite (i.e., cause further irritation).
And take care, according to your skill, that the wound in your treatment be clean; and that there be no dirt from ointment or anything else; nor allow any virulence to gather there; for when it settles on the panniculus of the brain, it corrupts and poisons it, and from this, a malignant evil will befall the patient.
And it happens in the wound of the head, as the bone is cleaned and especially when its care is neglected, and there is blackness on its surface; and therefore, consider then if the accidents that we have mentioned befall the patient, know that it is perilous without a doubt.
And if blackness does not occur except from the medicine that is placed on top, and it is by the virtue of that medicine that this blackness is produced, then you should take one part honey and three parts of rose oil, and with these, moisten a cloth, and then place it over the wound; and then treat it with the appropriate healing remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Of the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken except in two parts together, or one of them sometimes, because in it there are two bones, for the lower part is cartilaginous, and it is not broken and nothing happens to it except abrasion, twisting, and contusion.
But if one of its two parts is broken, then you should insert your little finger into the nose, and you should adjust that fracture inside with your index finger, and with your thumb outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And let this be done by you with care, and be watchful that you do not cause the patient pain through your operation.
And if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and the finger does not reach it, then it should be set with the end of a rod which has a small thickness.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you do likewise.
And strive for its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible; and if not, then around the seventh or tenth day you should treat the resulting abscess with cautery.
And then insert into the end of the nose a pledget of linen, if the fracture is in one part, or two pledgets, if the fracture is in two parts; and let the pledget be of such size that it fills the opening of the nose.
And I say that some of the ancient restorers said that the pledgets should be soaked in boiled oil and changed every day, but I have not seen this; rather, the pledget should be soaked in egg white mixed with powdered barley; and then leave the pledget until the bone is set and the cartilage hardened.
And sometimes, instead of a pledget, a goose feather, enveloped in linen cloth, is inserted into the nose; and let it be [done] when you have a more severe nasal fracture and the patient is not prevented from breathing.
And this method is not necessary for you; and if you wish, do this [use the feather], and if you wish, do [use] pledgets.
And if another time a hot abscess occurs, plaster the nose with cerate or with cotton soaked in sour wine and rose oil, or with diachylon [plaster].
But if a hot abscess does not occur, then you should plaster that [the nose] externally with sesame flour and cooked lentil powder with egg white; and then place linen cloth over it, and do not bind the nose with anything.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or are comminuted, then you should make an incision over them and extract them with instruments suitable for this; and then sew the incision, and treat it with that which incarnates and consolidates, and with plasters suitable for this.
And if a wound occurs inside the nose, then you should treat it with pledgets and administer lead canulae until it is completely healed.
Chapter 4.
Concerning the restoration of the mandible when it is fractured.
When the lower jaw is broken and its fracture is without a wound, observe then if the fracture is only external, and is not broken in two and is bent inwards at the broken parts, then its recognition is easy.
And it is necessary, therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, that you insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly if the fracture is in the left mandible, then insert the index finger of the right hand; and push with that the hump of the fracture inwards, easily towards the outer parts, with your other hand on the outer part of the bone; thus decently equalizing that.
And if the fracture of the mandible is already separated into two, then it is necessary that you administer extension from both sides according to straightness until its equalization is possible.
And if there occurs concussion of teeth with disruption, then strengthen those which you hope will remain with gold or silver wire or silk; and then place over the broken mandible a cerate, and then place over it a doubled cloth, and place over the cloth a well-made large splint, or a piece of sole leather equal to the length of the mandible; and then bind that above according to how the binding is prepared for you and how the application is appropriate, until it is healed.
And command the patient to use rest and quiet, and give him sorbitive foods.
And if you estimate that the shape is already altered in some way, then you should examine it and unbind it on the third day; and then rectify what is already altered in it and plaster it with powdered millet, with egg white, or with bean flour, after you have removed the cerate, and place wool over the plaster.
And then, when this plaster is attached and adjusted over it, and the disposition of the bone is not altered, leave it, do not unbind it until it is healed and strengthens the fracture; for many times this fracture is healed in three weeks.
And if another time a hot abscess occurs, then administer what we have said many times in its sedation until it is healed, and the abscess is resolved.
And if the fracture is with a wound, then consider if a piece of bone is separated or splintered; then subtly remove those splinters with whatever is suitable for removing them from the wound.
And if the opening of the wound is narrow, then widen it with phlebotomy according to your need.
And then, when you have removed those splinters, and nothing remains of them, then sew the opening of the wound if it is wide; and if not, place on it one of the plasters that is suitable for it and heals it, until it is healed.
Chapter 5.
Concerning the restoration of the clavicle when it is broken.
Often the clavicle is broken in the front part above the muscle, and this fracture is done in one of three ways.
Because it is either broken, or separated, or separated into two parts without splinters; and it is easy to restore it; or it is so that splinters occur in it, and it is difficult to restore it; or it is so that the fracture is with a wound.
And the operation in that case, when the fracture is without a wound, is that you have two assistants present, so that one holds the aid and follows the broken clavicle, and the other extends the neck towards that part.
Then align the fracture with your fingers until its shape is properly made, and there is neither a small prominence nor a concavity in it.
And if you need more extension, then you should place under the armpit of the patient a roll of cloth or wool, and let its size be according to your need; and extend and elevate the clavicle and tighten the roll with your hand until you align the fracture as needed.
And if you cannot pull the extremity of the clavicle to the outer parts, because it has reached the acromion, then the patient should be supine on their neck, and you should place a moderately sized cushion on their muscle.
And the assistant presses the muscle downwards until the bone of the clavicle which is on the acromion above is elevated, and then you rectify the fracture and align it with your fingers.
But if you feel splinters of the clavicle and they are bothersome, then you should incise over it and extract those splinters easily.
And if it is retained in the bone, then you should carefully incise it with one of the scalpels which you have prepared for this, after having placed under the clavicle the instrument that serves as a support; and this instrument is of wood or iron, of which this is the form:
And it is similar to a spoon, of which this is the concavity; and let its width be according to your need, according to the size of the bone or the smallness thereof.
But its length should be as long as is possible for you during the operation; that it has two ends like you see, and one end is wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound which you have incised when you extracted the splinter from the bone is wide, and there is a risk of a hot abscess, then you should approximate the two edges of the wound with sutures.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear a hot abscess, then you should fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the size of the wound's opening.
And if a hot abscess occurs, then dip a cloth in sour wine and rose oil and wine, and place it on that.
And ensure that the splint is not moved from over the broken bone; and for this reason you must see the patient each day, that when the ligature is softened or you see that the splint has been moved, then you rectify this and tighten the bandage.
Then place the patient's pillow under their neck, and place a small cushion under their shin at night on top of the pillow; so that with this the injured limb is raised, and thus the fractured clavicle is elevated by the muscle; or let their arm be tied to their neck.
And do not untie the bandage unless a boil or abscess occurs at the site, until twelve days have passed.
And then renew the plaster if you see the need, and adjust the bandage and restore it, and leave it until the fracture is healed and the clavicle is consolidated.
And it is strengthened and healed, depending on the case, in twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some people it is done in less time than that.
Chapter 6.
Concerning the restoration of a shoulder fracture.
The shoulder blade is broken at its lateral point, and not shattered, only the edges [are broken].
And so when the point of it is broken—and it is broken in the middle of its point—and is only known by touch, then according to what the form of the fracture is, force the preparation of it and reduce it to its natural form in every way possible to you.
Then apply to the area a powder made from finely ground egg white and lion's wool; and then apply a powder of doubled cloth; and then apply to it a thin, smoothly-laid bandage of fine linen, according to the size of the whole shoulder blade or a little more.
But if the bandage leaves a cavity in any part of the shoulder blade, then you fill that cavity with lion's wool, and then tighten the bandage evenly.
And then bind it above with a long bandage, with decent constriction and firmness of the bandage until it is not moved from its place.
And examine the binding each day; and whenever it is loosened, bind it again and tighten it, and even out the bandage if it has moved from its place.
And lay the patient on his healthy side.
And the shoulder blade is restored in twenty days or in twenty-five.
And so when this number is complete, then you loosen the binding, and you should know for certain that this is of the bones whose beginning is not feared nor their dissolution.
And if it proceeds from the location of the collarbone and extends under the armpit, then you incise over it, and remove it, and proceed as we have said in the fracture of the clavicle, with cautery of the abscess if it occurs there.
Chapter 7.
Of the restoration of a fractured foot.
The foot is sometimes broken in the middle, and this heals slowly; but its extremities heal much faster because they are more compact and less fragmented.
And among the accidents of a fractured middle of the foot is that it inclines inwards, causing great pain, difficulty breathing, coughing, a strong fever, and vomiting blood; and the cavity in the broken bone is evident and not hidden from perception.
And its restoration is that the patient should be placed supine on his back, with a cushion placed between his shoulders; then press on his muscles, and adjust his bones with hands on both sides, and be careful in the operation and in the setting, in every way possible and appropriate, until the bone's shape is restored as it should be.
Then apply a plaster and lint, and over that place a splint of thin willow or alder wood, or something similar in lightness, after wrapping it in cloth; and be careful in binding it over the broken bone so that it is not too tight; and bind it in a circular manner around the back of the foot sometimes, and tighten it with a suitable binding; and check the binding frequently, and tighten it when it loosens.
And if necessity demands loosening of the bandage due to corrosion, itching, pain, or an abscess that develops in the area, then loosen and unbind it, remove the plaster, and rectify the problem with appropriate treatment; and reapply the plaster, if it is necessary; and adjust the binding until it is healed.
Chapter 8.
On the restoration of ribs when they are broken.
Know that a fracture of the ribs only happens in the thicker parts where there are two; and in their extremities in the front part, no injury happens to them except because they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of it is not hidden from the feeling of its examination with the fingers.
And the restoration should be such that you equalize the fracture with your fingers as much as possible, until it is set as needed, and then plaster and bind the broken bone with a bandage, if you need to do that.
And if the fracture of the ribs is inclined towards the inner parts, then the patient suffers vehement pain and a stabbing sensation, like the stabbing pain that happens to someone with pleurisy, and because the bone pierces the membrane; and it causes difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting of much blood; and this is difficult to cure.
And already the Ancients have devised many ingenious things for this.
And of them it is said: it is necessary that you give the patient foods that produce inflation and flatulence, so that the belly is inflated and extended, and the fracture is pushed outwards.
But we would avoid this so that it does not lightly cause a hot abscess, if it does not happen; and if it already happens, then help with it.
And some of them say that a cupping glass should be placed on top and that it should draw strongly; and it seems reasonable, but however, it is feared that the cupping glass will draw superfluities to the place because of the weakness.
And some of them say: the place should be covered with wool that has been submerged in hot oil, and powders should be placed between the ribs until it is filled, so that the bandage is even as it envelops that roundness; and then the patient should be treated with a treatment for pleurisy with diet and medicine.
And if a vehement cause constricts the patient, which he does not suffer, and the bone pushes the skin with a painful puncture, threatening the patient, then it is necessary to cut open the place, uncover the broken rib, and then place over it an instrument that preserves the callus, the remembrance of which is mentioned before, and that we incise the bone easily, and extract it.
And then we adjust the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with stitching, and we treat it with plasters until it is healed.
And if an abscess occurs inside, then you should take and mix powders in rose oil and place it on the area, and the patient should be treated with what is appropriate for the internal abscess.
And let him be supine on the side on which he sleeps, until he is healed.
Chapter 9.
Chapter 9: On the restoration of fractures of the spine and neck.
Sometimes a fracture occurs in the bones of the neck, and later leads to their attrition, and similarly in the vertebrae of the back. And when this happens to someone and you want to know if he will be healed or not, then consider: for if you see his hands already softened and numb and dead, and he cannot move them, nor extend nor flex them, and when you constrict them with your fingers or prick them with a needle and he feels no pain in them, then know that he is not healed, and in most cases this is mortal.
And if he moves them and feels constriction with the fingers and puncture, then know that the marrow of the bone is saved and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if he has something similar in the vertebrae of the neck and you want to know if he will be healed or not, then observe his feet.
Then if you see that they are already softened, the same thing has happened to them as happened to the hands, and moreover, when he lies on his back, he experiences flatulence and defecation without his will, and sometimes he lies on his back and wants to urinate and cannot, then know that it is mortal, and then do not work on his cure.
But if none of these things happen, the cause is better and milder.
And the cure for him is that you should, to calm the hot abscess, put on the affected vertebra plain rose oil, or with boiled egg yolks; apply this several times a day until the hot abscess is calmed.
And then apply one of the comforting and stimulating plasters, and bind it tightly; and order the patient to rest and remain tranquil and not to sleep on the side where he feels pain until he is healed.
And if, due to the erosion of the bone, a splinter or something that has separated from it occurs, then you must saw it off at the bone and remove that bone; and then join the two edges of the wound with a suture if it is large; and then treat it with some incarnative ointment until it is healed.
But if the back of the hip bone is broken, and it is the end of the [hip] socket, then you should insert the index finger of your left hand into the anus, and with your other hand, set the broken bone as evenly as possible; and then apply a plaster and bandage, if you have them, and then bind it tightly.
And if you feel splinters broken inside, then you should make an incision there, and remove them, and treat the wound with the previously described treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Of the restoration of the hip.
The hip bones are rarely broken, and if they are broken, then their fracture is nothing but a separation at the extremities of those bones and they are split lengthwise and lean inwards; and the patient experiences pain and numbness at the location, and the leg is numb when it is fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over it until it is over the fracture, which is the shape of it.
And if the fracture is only at its extremity, then you bind that according to what is prepared for you by equal binding until it is similar to its natural form.
But if the fracture is lengthwise or inclines downwards, then you make the patient lie on their stomach until the restoration of that fracture is prepared by you. And then when you have bound it, place a plaster on it, and then place a splint of wood or whalebone over that, and bind it tightly so that you do not fear a puncture of the fracture or movement of the splint.
And bind the concavity with something that fills it until it takes the binding equally, and command the patient to sleep on their back or on their healthy side.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then you cease the extension of it and its restoration until the hot abscess is calmed, and place on it that which calms it as is previously described; and then return to its restoration, and bind it as is necessary.
And if splinters occur in the bone or are comminuted, then you remove them.
Chapter 11.
On the restoration of a fracture of the clavicle (collarbone).
The clavicle is that which is between the shoulder and the neck.
And if it is broken, then its restoration is in one of two ways, of which one is that you take a piece of wood of moderate thickness according to this form:
And let two bandages be tied at both ends, and then let it be suspended from a raised place.
And seat the patient on a stool; and then place his broken arm on the wood until it reaches his armpit, in the middle of which the curvature of the wood should be attached.
And then hang some heavy object from it, or let the assistant stretch it downwards; and then let the physician hold the fracture together with his hands until the fracture is properly reset.
And the other method is that the patient lies supine on his back and suspends his arm from his neck with a bandage; and then order two assistants, that one of them hold what is above the fracture with his hands; and the other hold the lower part; and each of them should extend to his part.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then tighten a bandage around the fracture, and another bandage over that, and let each of the two assistants extend towards their respective parts.
And so if the fracture is close to the extremity of the muscle, then it is necessary that half of the bandage be placed on the armpit and the other below the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, then it is necessary that the bandage be placed on that place and on that elbow.
Swallow it easily and without difficulty, and then, when it is swallowed as it should be and is applied with good application, tighten it unless a hot abscess occurs.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then leave the binding until the seventh day, and place on it soiled wool soaked in sour wine and rose oil until the abscess subsides, and then you tighten it.
And the manner of tightening it is that you place a plaster on the fracture, and then place a bandage of new cloth over the plaster; and then adjust the arm on that support, and place your open hand over its muscle.
And place the cloth and tighten the arm on the support so that the arm is in a stellar position, unless some cause of the broken bone prohibits this.
And then use splints.
And it is that you place on the fracture that splint which is wider and stronger than the other splints; and place between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with the addition of three fingers on each side.
And then tighten the splints with the bandage which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your bandage over the fracture site should be tighter. And the longer the fracture, the less tight the bandage should be.
And so if you see the placement of the splints and the bandage as we have said at the time when you restore the limb to its proper place, then you do so.
And if you fear a boil forming, then leave the bandage and splints in place until the seventh day, as we have said.
And then examine the bandage in all parts for three days so that no itching or inflammation occurs at the site; or it is forbidden for food to reach the limb due to the tightness of the bandage; and then correct all this as we have said.
But if you are confident that none of this will happen, then do not untie the bandage until three days or more.
And let the patient's sleep be on their back, and their hands should be on their stomach.
And place on the splint a poultice full of wool, evenly spread, and check every hour of the night and day that the shape of the broken limb is not loosened or dissolved, or that the bandage is not softened; therefore correct all this with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order which we have previously mentioned, so that the food is light at the beginning until healing begins; and then it is appropriate to make their food thicker.
For it is customary for the splint and his leg to be bound for forty days; and then they should be unbound, and a bath should be administered, and it should be medicated with ointments which rectify that.
And if the fracture is badly crushed, then you should not unbind the bandage and splints before ten days or before two months.
Chapter 12.
Of the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called two focils; of which one is small, and it is that which is next to the little finger, and the other is large and it is that which is placed below the little finger, on the lower side.  (Note: "focils" likely refers to the radius and ulna.)
And very easily the larger focil is broken alone, or the smaller one, and very easily they are broken together.
And when the small focil is broken above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the focil is broken below, the fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when both bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the smaller focil above, then it is convenient for the physician, before its restoration, to apply slight extension easily until it is aligned.
But if it is the larger focil that is broken, then it is necessary to apply a more forceful extension.
And if both focils are broken together, then you must apply the extension much more strongly.
And it is necessary that you place the shape of your hand during its restoration and extension stretched out on a cushion, and let the thumb of the hand be in the upper parts, higher than all the other fingers, and the little finger lower than the other fingers, and the patient be lying on a square cushion.
And let there be a cushion in front of him at his height or level, so that the patient does not suffer strain.
Then let the assistant extend the arm from the lower part with his hand or with a bandage, and another assistant extend it from above similarly.
Then the physician should align the bone until he reduces it according to the best configuration he can.
And if in the fracture of the bone there is a jagged edge, then you must forcefully reduce all the pieces to their place with your skill.
And if a separated, jagged piece appears which punctures the skin and there is no hope for its restoration, then you should saw it off and remove it according to the manner which we have described above.
But if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, I have already devoted a separate chapter to this, and therefore take the treatment from that.
And if an abscess occurs near the beginning of your restoration, then you should apply a cloth with a cerate made of rose oil and white wax, and let it be of medium consistency between thick and thin, and bind the cloth tightly over it with a thin bandage until the abscess subsides; and then remove the cerate and apply a plaster prepared from powdered millet with egg white, and then apply a compress; and let the compress which is placed over the fracture be thicker and firmer.
And know that the number of compresses on the arm varies in the major arrangements, whether the fracture is in one bone or in two bones together.
Then apply your bandage over the fracture site firmly and forcefully, and wherever the bandage is closer to the parts above the fracture or to the parts below it, apply the bandage a little more loosely, as was previously mentioned at the beginning of this chapter.
And the cloths that are wrapped around the fracture should be soft and moist cloths, and not hard or rough, and the thread with which it is bound should be of a consistency midway between fine and thick, as we have already recommended.
And examine the limb and the bandage after a few days. And if any cause arises that requires rectification, such as itching that occurs in the limb, then you should bathe the limb with warm water until that itching ceases.
And leave the limb unconstrained until it rests, and then reapply the bandage.
And if the fracture is now softened and the bone is reset and similar to the others, then rectify all this with your skill.
And further consider whether nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb by an overly tight bandage, for then you must loosen it a little and leave it so for a few days until nourishment flows to it, and then tighten it again.
But if the patient does not experience any of the conditions we have mentioned, then the bandage should not be loosened for about twenty days or thereabouts.
Then the patient's hand should be suspended by its own agitated movement, and make the patient sleep on their back.
And know that this fracture is healed in thirty days or in thirty-two days, or very quickly in twenty-eight days, and all this depends on the constitution and the disposition of the [patient's] strength/vitality.
Chapter 13.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the little finger and of the fingers.
Know that the little finger and the knuckles of the fingers rarely suffer fracture, and what happens to them is mostly attrition (wear and tear).
And when a fracture or attrition happens to the hand, then it is necessary that the patient be seated and lie down before him evenly, and then place his hand upon that which is extended [i.e., the injured hand].
And then the assistant extends the broken bones, and the physician joins them until they are joined with a good union.
And then it is necessary to apply a plaster and lint, unless a hot abscess occurs; and then apply a bandage over it according to the size of the area, and you have already wrapped it in linen.
But if the fracture is in the inner parts of the palm of the hand, then make a kind of ball of cloth, and instruct the patient to hold it with his fractured palm; then it should be bound with a long cloth.
And let the bandage be of leather with cloth in which it is softened, so that the leather is applied to the palm; and tighten it as necessary.
But if the fracture is in the outer parts, then it is necessary to place a bandage above and another bandage below on the palm, so that the hand is open and straight; and then apply the splint, as the hand is rolled in a small cradle between the fingers with bandages.
And if a fracture occurs in any of the knuckles of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, it should be straightened as necessary and then bound to the palm; and if you wish, place a small straight splint on it so that the fracture is elevated and not moved.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as in the middle finger, or in the index finger, or in the ring finger, or in the little finger, then it should be straightened and tied to the finger next to it; or all should be tied together continuously, which is better; or place a small splint on it, as we have said for the thumb.
And consider, in your treatment, and regarding it, the disposition of hot abscesses and oppose — with your operation — that which is necessary when any of those conditions occur, as the description of them iterates.
Chapter 14.
On the treatment of a fracture of the thigh bone.
The thigh bone is often broken and is felt according to whether it is broken in the front or the back part.
And its treatment is done in this way: a bandage is tightened above the fracture and another bandage below the fracture, and the patient is laid prostrate on their stomach and face.
Then the attendant stretches the entire bandage to its proper extent, according to the nature of the fracture, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if it is closer to the hip joint, then let the bandage be close to the knee on the side of the patella, so that the extension is upward, and the other bandage below the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture is close to the knee, then let the bandage be placed near the knee, and let the extension be towards the lower parts.
And then the doctor aligns the bone with both hands until it is reduced to its natural form and the bone is joined by a good union; then it is necessary to apply a plaster and bandage, unless an abscess occurs in the limb. But if an abscess occurs there, then you leave it for a few days until the abscess has matured and subsided, and then return to your treatment.
The bandage should be made thus: you must wrap a firm bandage around the fracture, and fold it two or three times, removing any excess; and then wrap the leg until it reaches the ankle and the hip joint.
And insert a long piece of felt between the thigh and the leg near the knee on the lower parts, and let the ends of the felt come from the upper part of both sides, and then wrap around the leg and thigh whatever excess bandage remains.
And then place splints on the thigh at the site of the fracture, and place one splint over the bone of the leg.
And then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the bandage is secure.
Then begin with a firm bandage in the middle over the fracture site with three or four wraps, and the farther you move from the fracture site, the looser, lighter, and softer your bandage should be.
And then extend the two ends of the felt to the lower parts until it reaches the ankle, and tighten both ends of the splints on the other side so that the bandage is not moved from its place.
And then leave the bandage on it, provided that it does not cause corrosion, hot abscess, swelling or anything similar to those in the limb; and if any of those things occur, then carefully loosen it, and rectify what happened as soon as possible, as we have said many times.
But if there is a piece of bone in the bone that protrudes, then you must reset it as best you can; and if not, cover it and keep it in place, and treat it with what was mentioned before until it heals.
And sometimes this fracture of the thigh is tightened even after splints have been applied, as we have said in the case of the arm and shoulder.
However, in the restoration done in this way, lameness does not occur to the patient; and if it is restored alone, without the other leg being applied to it, then it is necessary that he limps from that time on.
And know that the thigh is held tight for 40 days or a little more or a little less, and all this depends on the diversity of complexions and other dispositions.
Chapter 15.
On the restoration of the fracture of the kneecap.
Know that a fracture of the kneecap heals slowly, but it often causes attrition (or wearing away).
But if a fracture occurs, then it is only a fissure or hole in its parts, and this is with or without a wound, and everything about this is accompanied by sensation.
And its restoration is such that what is broken of its parts is joined together with said parts until it is aggregated and conjoined as much as possible by equalization, ease, and fabrication.
Then apply a plaster and place a round splint over it, if you need one; and bind it above with a suitable bandage.
And then consider all the provisions which we have recommended in other fractures, such as hot poultices and similar things, so that all accidents are opposed with what is convenient for it until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
Concerning the restoration of a leg fracture.
Know that in the leg there are two bones, of which one is thick and is called by the name of the tibia; and the other is thin and is called the fibula.
And the same kinds of fractures happen to these [two leg bones] as happen to the two bones of the arm, and therefore its restoration is done in the same way as the restoration of the arm, and the operation is the same.
And if the two bones are broken together, the leg is deformed in all parts.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is deformed in the front parts.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you will find it below, and it is one of those things that are not easily managed.
Then administer extension, reduction (setting), and ligation of the splints according to that method.
However, if the leg fracture is large with many splinters, the extension should be less and gentler, and relieve it with your skill.
And in the leg, there is a certain procedure similar to that for the arm.  When you have set the splints and completed your operation, then you will take pieces of pine wood which are placed under the bandages that are placed on the splints and are made of palm branches; choose those of small thickness and not the thin ones, and let their length be according to the length of the leg, from the knee to the ankle.
Then wrap each one in two cloths according to its length, and place one on one side of the leg and the other on the other side, and they should extend from the knee to the lowest part of the foot.
Then tie the two splints in three places: at the two ends and in the middle. For with this construction the leg is prevented from bending to the right or left, and remains in a good position.
And now a small wooden splint, according to the length of the leg, is applied; and let the leg be placed on it so that it preserves its position.
And especially it is necessary that you do this in a fracture, and when there is a wound specifically.
And then examine the leg each day and study it carefully, and watch it for abscesses and inflammation or other similar conditions; and if any of these conditions occur, then treat it with what is necessary until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Of the fracture of the talus and the bones of the foot and toes.
A fracture of the whole talus does not often occur; but fractures of the bones of the foot do occur sometimes; and fractures of the toes occur rarely, and they only suffer attrition in cases of injury.
And if a fracture occurs in the bones of the foot and sometimes those bones are elevated above each other, then the patient should place his foot on the ground prepared as previously described.
Then place one foot on the elevated part of those bones, and then press and manipulate them until they return to their places.
Then apply a plaster and lint on top, and place under the sole of the foot a small board with two flat ends.
Then bind that with the foot with a descending bandage, after you have wrapped it with cloths and other necessary things.
And when three or four days have passed, then untie the bandage, for you will find the bones set, whether they were fractured or dislocated, and the operation on them is as you know.
But if some say they are fractured, then restore them and manipulate them as I have recounted to you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then place a splint on the fractured finger according to its length, and let it be a little longer than it; and place under the foot that board which I have described to you, and bind it with a suitable bandage.
But if what is fractured of the bones is two or three or more, then you place on each finger a splint of its size wrapped in linen cloth, and bind the board to the lowest part of the foot, and let it rest on one of the ends of the board, outside the sole of the foot, so that it holds with good retention.
And it is convenient for you that you not forget that you consider all the things that I have told you in the other fractures of the accidents that we have mentioned; and oppose yourself to every accident with what is appropriate.
Chapter 18.
Of the fracture of the woman's hip and of the pubic bone and of the man's penis.
When the hip of a woman is fractured, then make her lie down in a reclining position, and gently incline her to one side, and she should be supported from behind.
And then the midwife fills the hip with cotton, and it should be made into a ball in her hip; and then the woman should be bandaged, and her hip raised little by little, for all this is dislocated until the part of the hip is brought back into place like a ball and the broken bone is set.
And then apply powders to her hip, and when she wants to urinate, remove the cotton easily and then she urinates, and reposition and return it according to the method by which you set it initially, and then return it to its first position.
And do this for seven days or thereabouts, for then it will be healed.
And if you wish, take a pig's bladder and attach a reed tube to its opening; and insert the whole bladder into her hip, and then inflate it through the tube until the bladder is inflated inside the hip, for this will help the fracture to reset.
And then it should be filled with cotton and remain so for some days as we have recommended, until it is healed.
And when the bone of the pubis of a man or woman is broken, then administer for its restoration and healing that which we have recommended for the hip bone.
And that which is right is not hidden in these nearby fractures which happen infrequently, especially to one who studies and uses and understands this my book well; for that fracture shows you the best methods in its restoration and binding, and therefore understand well.
But in the case of a broken penis, take the gum of a gooseberry, and place the penis in it; and then place a cloth bandage over it, and let it be wrapped, and then let it remain for three days or thereabouts, and it will be healed.
Chapter 19.
Of the restoration of fractured bones when there is a wound.
It is convenient for one who suffers a fracture with a wound, and especially if the bone is large, such as the thigh bone or the humerus, or similar, that you take care of and bleed him immediately if the conditions for bloodletting testify to this as we have said above.
And if the wound flows with blood, then it is necessary that you attend to stopping it such that you sprinkle crushed egg whites on it, if no other cause is present.
And then begin to restore the fracture on that same day, and do not prolong it, unless an abscess occurs.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then you leave its restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess has subsided, and do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all, for you will cause bad accidents.
But if the broken bone is prominent above the skin, uncovered, then it is necessary that you carefully reduce it and align it with your hands easily and with little extension.
But if extension is not prepared for you and the alignment of it with your hands, then you reduce it with your hands with this instrument; and it is an instrument which is made of iron, the length of which is seven or eight fingers, and the width of which is according to the size of the wound; and for this it is necessary for the physician to take three or four of these, according to the quantity needed for it in the treatment in every kind of fracture.
And let it be round, and let it be of little thickness so that it is not bent by the compression on it during the operation.
And let it have a sharp end which has a margin at its ends; and let the upper part of it be of a tapering thickness, and from the middle of it to the ends.
And this is its form:
And it is named in Greek "labre volentz atelarem," that is, a small spatula.
Therefore it is necessary that its sharp end be placed raised above the end of the prominent bone.
And push that in at once until the bone returns, and let it be equalized with some instrument.
And then strive to equalize the ends of the fracture between them, and if the broken end of the bone is thin, and does not receive the instrument well, then it is necessary that you incise the end of that bone until the instrument has power over it.
But if you cannot reduce the bone completely with what we have recommended, then incise it with whatever is suitable from the incisors which we have mentioned; or saw it with one of the saws in whatever way is possible for you.
And then scrape away what remains on the bone of roughness and small splinters.
And when the bone is reduced, and after the reduction the patient finds vehement, persistent pain, then know that the bone is not returned to its natural place; and then do what you can to provide great relief to the patient.
When your restoration of the bone is complete, then submerge a cloth in black styptic wine, especially if it is summer, and do not place on the wound cerate or anything else containing oil, lest it cause putrefaction and corruption there.
And then administer stitches at the time of your completion of the bone's restoration, and leave the wound uncovered so that you may incise the opening with forceps and use dressings according to the size of the wound.
And guard yourself with all caution that you do not constrict the wound with the fracture, for many times foolish physicians have done this, and caused death or corrosive illness, that is, gangrene, upon their patients. And if your stricture is light, soften it, differently from the stricture of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or a bad wound, and you fear some bad accidents which we have recounted, and you find pain in the place, then it is not fitting that you place stitches there, but apply a dressing of firm cloths instead of stitches and bind it with those.
And when, after a day or two, the wound begins to generate pus, then remove the cloth which you had placed upon it with wine, and then administer poultices, ointments with which it is our custom to treat wounds, such as tetrapharmacon ointment and similar things.
And it behooves you to unbind the bandage and examine the wound each day, morning and evening, until it is consolidated and closed, and it is necessary that the limb be positioned in such a way that the pus flows downwards easily.
If many days pass and the wound is neither consolidated nor is the pus discharged from it, then know that here there are collections of small particles, for which it is necessary to explore the wound with a probe. And of those collections, remove and extract those which are separated; those which are not separated and press upon the limb and cause pain, you must incise as far as is possible for you.
And if there happens to be swelling or inflammation in the wound—that is, *stiomenios*—or another kind of corruption and putrefaction, then it is necessary that you treat each of those things with what is appropriate, the treatment of which is described in its chapter.
And this is what my discourse should address, and you should pay attention: when a large bone is fractured, such as the bone of the thigh or the humerus and similar large limb bones, and there is no traction or extraction of it, for many times death ensues from this; therefore leave it until it suppurates, for it will soon fall away by itself after twenty or thirty days; and then treat the wound if it seems right to you, and if not leave it.
Chapter 20.
Chapter 20: On the treatment of the swelling that occurs at the site of a fracture.
Often this swelling occurs at the site of a healing fracture, especially near the joints, and it distorts the shape of the limb; and it quickly prevents the limb from its natural function.
Then consider if the swelling is recent and apply to it medicines that are styptic, such as aloe, incense, myrrh, cerecloth, and acacia, and similar ones; so that you take some or all of these, and mix them with styptic wine, or with egg white, or with sour wine, and apply this to the swelling on a pledget, and bind it firmly in place.
And leave the bandage tight; do not loosen it for many days; then loosen it and apply another, until the swelling is reduced; or bind a thin sheet of lead over it, because lead has the property of reducing anything that is swollen in the limbs.
But if the swelling has already become hardened and constricted, and necessity compels its removal carefully, then incise it in its most superior part, and incise the superfluous and prominent part, or scrape it with some instrument until it is reduced; and treat this until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
Chapter 21: Of the restoration of a fracture, when it is restored and the limb remains thin and weak, not according to its nature.
When a bone fracture is set, and the limb remains thin and weak, this happens for many reasons: one of which is excessive loosening of the ligaments and their binding not as it should be; or by excessive tightness of the bandages, such that nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb; or by excessive and superfluous embrocation; or by excessive movement not at the proper time; or by a lack of blood in the patient's body and their weakness.
And the cure for this is to give the patient nourishing food and to make his heart cheerful so that his blood is multiplied; and administer baths; and instill joy and happiness in him.
And then apply a poultice to the limb, so that the poultice draws much nourishment to it, or continue embrocation with warm water until nourishment flows and returns it to its natural form.
Chapter 22.
Chapter 22. Of the treatment of fractured bones when they are set crookedly and the proper operation is prevented.
When, after a limb has been set, there is a return of crookedness and swelling at the fractured bone and joint, and because of this the limb is deformed, but the limb is not prevented from its natural function, then you should not accept the word of someone who believes that limb is newly fractured.
But there have been many foolish physicians and bone-setters who have done this in our land; and this operation is very reprehensible, leading to great fear, the sign of which is mortal.
But if the lameness and swelling are recent, then they should be bathed with tepid water in which are boiled softening herbs, such as mallow leaves and roots, melilot, and similar things; and a softening plaster should be applied, such as well-made diachylon; or a mucilage of mallow root should be made and mixed with white clay or with oil of chamomile, and applied as a plaster; or ripe figs should be crushed with pigeon droppings, and similar things from among the medicines called consolidating emollients.
And sometimes it is resolved with gentle, frequent rubbing with moist hands, and movement of the limb in every direction should be administered at all times.
But if the crookedness has progressed and become severe and hardened, and there is need to treat it with iron, then the overlying skin should be incised and the continuity of the bone dissolved, and that which superfluously swells with the bone should be cut away with fine scalpels, and relief should be provided with care and attention.
Chapter 23.
Concerning the discourse on dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of one of the joints from its place, wherefore movement is prevented, and the shape of the limb is distorted, causing great pain and injury to the patient.
Therefore, when a dislocation happens to someone, it is necessary to immediately strive for its reduction, and not delay that reduction, for if it is delayed, the place becomes inflamed and very quickly the reduction of the dislocation becomes difficult.
And for this reason, it should not be moved or stretched at the time of inflammation, because many times it causes spasms and intense pain.
But when this happens, then you should attend to the phlebotomy of the patient, and allow it until the inflammation subsides; and then the limb should be bathed with warm water and oil, and then it should be reduced with ease.
And every limb should be treated in such a way that it will return to its proper place.
And I have already ordered the chapters on dislocation according to what preceded on fracture; from the uppermost part of the body down to the lowermost part.
Chapter 24.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
If the two jaws are dislocated, and the dislocation of both is done in one of two ways: either they are moved from their places by slight movement, and are softened a little; or they are dislocated by complete and full dislocation, to the extent that they are softened at the joint, until saliva flows from the patient and they cannot retain it nor close their mouth, and their tongue is entangled in their speech.
But when the dislocation is slight, then in most cases it returns by itself with little intervention.
But if the dislocation is complete and full, then it is necessary that reduction be administered lightly, and not delayed at all.
And it is that the assistant holds the head of the patient, and the physician inserts the thumb of one hand into the root of the dislocation of the jaw inside the patient's mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or that he inserts both thumbs together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers of his hands are outside with which he pulls; and he commands the patient to relax their jaws and allow them to move from each side; and the physician pulls the jaw and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, and especially if both jaws are dislocated together, then administer a vaporization with hot water and oil, until the reduction of both is made easy and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And when they return and are aligned, and the patient's mouth is closed and not softened, then place on those powders of cloth with cerate, and let it be made of wax and rose oil; and then it should be tied easily with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on their back, and their head should be between two pillows, so that it does not hurt on the right or left side; and do not force them to chew anything; before their food should be very soft, until when the pains are lessened and the jaws are healed, then let them eat what they want.
And this should be administered easily, and do not work on opening the mouth during eating and drinking and speaking, until the jaw is healed and healthy.
And if the reduction of the two jaws is difficult when they are dislocated at any time and are not returned to their places, then many times fevers and persistent thirst occur, and the patient's abdomen is quickly bloated, and they quickly vomit pure bile.
And when you see this, then know that it is dangerous; and many times the one to whom this happens dies within a few days.
Chapter 25.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the clavicle and the extremity of the muscle.
The clavicle is not dislocated inwards by its connection with the ribs; and sometimes it is dislocated outwards, and this is evident to the touch.
And its restoration is that the patient lies on his back and extends his arms. Then compress the area with your palm with firm compression, for it will return.
Then apply a plaster and powders to that area and bind it tightly.
But the extremity of that which is the elbow and is continuous with it, is not dislocated in any way; then it must be reduced and bandaged as we have said and as is appropriate for you.
Then apply a plaster, powders, and a bandage to it, and instruct the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until it is healed.
And with this same treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter 26.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that a muscle is dislocated in only three ways, one of which is that it is dislocated from the upper part to the lower part.
And the second is that it is dislocated to the upper part of the muscle, and this is done slowly; and it is not dislocated to the back parts by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated to the front parts by the nerves.
And it is mostly dislocated and protrudes from the lower parts below the nipple, and especially in those who have little flesh, it is easily dislocated; but in those who have much flesh, it protrudes with difficulty.
And it quickly happens to some men through a blow or fall, and their muscle is inflamed with a hot abscess, and it is thought to be dislocated because of this.
Then you must prove this before you are completely certain of it, and then you proceed with its cure.
And the dislocation is known when it is in the lower parts below the nipple; so that you distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the healthy muscle, because you find a manifest difference between them; and you find in the head of the muscle a concavity, and below the nipple, near the touch of the head of the muscle, like an egg, and the patient cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor move it with all movements.
If it is dislocated near the feet or above, and you then find a manifest touch, and it is not hidden.
And the reduction of this dislocation is easily done when it is fresh and the patient is a child.
And the reduction of it is that the minister raises his hand to the upper parts; and then places his hands below his nipple, and raises the joint strongly up to its place, and the minister raises his hand, and extends it upwards; and then places it below because it returns easily.
But if by what we have said it does not return, and from the beginning that the dislocation happened, many days have passed, then it is necessary that the patient be bathed in warm water and an emollient and softening embrocation be administered, such as cooked roots of marshmallow, fenugreek, and melilot in water, and it should be administered.
And then the patient should lie supine on his back and a wool cushion be placed under his nipple, which should be of a consistency between softness and firmness, and then the physician should place his heel on the cushion, and raise the head of the muscle with force, and pull the patient's hand so that it is not pulled downwards, and another minister should hold the patient's head so that it is not pulled downwards, because it immediately returns to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it according to this method:  that you have a man present who is taller than the patient, and he stands on that side, and inserts his arm under the patient's armpit and raises his armpit upwards until the patient is suspended in the air, and another minister pulls the patient's hand from under his belly. But if the patient is light, then it is necessary that something else be suspended with him, so that it weighs him down, because the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is reduced easily according to another method: that a long pole be fixed in the earth, the head of which is round in shape, like a mortar pestle, neither thick nor thin, and then it is placed under the patient; after cloths are placed on top of the pole, and the patient stands along the length of the pole, and then his hand is extended downwards on the other side; and his body is pulled strongly from the other side, because the joint returns to its place quickly.
And if its reduction is difficult for all the reasons we have mentioned, then use this treatment: that you take a pole whose length is two arm-lengths, and its width is four fingers, and its thickness is reduced at the end near the armpit in this shape:
And then, on the round head, soft cloths should be tied so that the pole does not injure the patient.
And then it is placed under the armpit of the patient, and the hand or arm is extended over the pole down to the lower parts, and the pole is tied above the shoulder and arm and the extremity of the hand.
Then the arm is placed on a step of a ladder according to its width, and the hand is extended to the lower parts, and the rest of the body is suspended from the other side, for the joint enters immediately.
And when its entry is complete in whichever way is possible for you, then you should place under the armpit a ball of wool of equal quantity; and then apply a plaster prepared from powdered mollium [likely a softening agent] and egg white over all the muscle, so that it is wrapped around, and then tighten under the armpit with a decent bandage; and the bandage should be wrapped over the plaster, and the patient's hand should be attached to their neck, and left, and the patient's hand should not be moved for seven days.
And it is necessary that the patient's diet be meager until the limb is strengthened, for it is easier for its healing.
And then the diet should be increased after seven or five days, and some movement should be exercised; and then if it is firm and not softened, then it is healed.
But if the joint is dislocated many times due to humidity that affects it or another cause, then a cautery with three holes should be administered to it, as previously stated in the chapter on cautery.
And if all this is done and after seven days the bandage is untied and the joint is not firm, and the plaster and the bandage are reapplied several times, and it is not firm and falls and cannot raise that arm to the upper parts, then know that its nerves, which are at the head of the muscle, are incised or stretched or softened, and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
Of the dislocation that is made between the foot and the ankle or the back parts, the reduction is made with impulse and extension with the hands, until it returns [to its place].
And let another bandage be applied to it, and treat it until it is healed.
And if during the healing process heaviness in the limb and slowness in its movement occur, then let the patient use a bath many times until that heaviness is relieved and it returns to its former nature.
Chapter 27.
Concerning the treatment of a hip dislocation.
Know that the hip joint is dislocated with difficulty and similarly returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated in all directions, and more so to the front than to the back, and its dislocation is not hidden from you because it is evident to the sight or to the touch, according to whatever form it is dislocated. And when you align the dislocated hip with the healthy one, this is clearly evident to you, for the joint is concave and cannot bend the leg, nor touch with it its own muscle (thigh).
And it is necessary that you attend to it, and reduce the dislocation immediately before a hot abscess develops, for if a hot abscess develops, its reduction is difficult, and it is not easily completely healed; and especially when it is in the posterior parts, for it is worse than all other types of dislocation, and more painful, and often death results from it.
And its restoration, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the patient's leg should be extended with two hands, and their thigh should be expanded, and the doctor's hands should be above and below the hip, and he should push the joint with the thumbs of his hands together, or with the root of his palm until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is in the front, then return it by bending the leg with your hand, until you strike the hip with the base of your palm.
And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer vigorous and very strong extension.
And it is so that two assistants extend the arm, and two assistants hold it firmly so that it is not moved during the extension.
And then the arm should be rotated in every direction, after it has been wrapped with a long, folded cloth over the patient's hand, or a bandage.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil so that this may aid in the lubrication of the joint with ease; and then push the joint with strong pressure until it returns to place.
And after the reduction, it is necessary that a plaster be applied which possesses styptic and drying properties with egg white, and it should be tightly bound with a descending bandage, and the arm should be suspended from the neck, and left thus for some days.
And afterwards it should be unbound; and then if the joint is set in its place, then you untie its bandage and leave it.
And if you see that the joint is not well-knit, then you should reapply the plaster and bandage, and leave it for some days until it is firm; and afterwards unbind it.
But if  severe pain occurs during the reduction of the joint, and the patient's condition is agitated, then administer moist heat in a bath and gentle friction, and compression, until relief is obtained; or apply a poultice of marshmallow, that is, something very fatty; and then bandage it, and leave it on the bone day and night. And afterwards remove it, and place it in a bath; and when it is sufficiently softened, then rub the joint with a temperate friction, and afterwards reapply the marshmallow, that is, a very soft poultice, once, and a second time, and a third. with the application of a bath, until it is calmed.
And if you wish, place over it a moist and warmed ox-hide, bound tightly over it; and do this several times, because it is moist and restores it to its proper state.
Chapter 28.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated wrist, that is, of the joint that is between the arm and the hand.
A dislocated wrist is sometimes easily reduced, unlike other joints; however, speed in reduction is necessary at the time of dislocation, before the area becomes inflamed or a hot abscess forms there.
And the reduction of its dislocation is that you place the patient's wrist joint on a table, and the assistant extends the patient's hand, and the physician places his hand or palm over the prominence of the joint, and manipulates it until it returns to its place.
However, it must be considered whether the palm is already facing the belly of the hand; then the patient places the back of his hand on a table during extension and reduction. And if the dislocation is towards the back of the hand, then the position of the patient's hand should be inward on the table, so that the physician's hand rests on that prominence of the joint.
