And so if it returns immediately, it is good; and if not, then compress it with a poultice to reduce the inflammation and leave it; and do not repeat this, for otherwise, you will not be able to reduce it once several days have passed.
However, if the joint remains crooked and the patient experiences no pain other than a weakened hand unable to grasp anything, then you should know that the nerves are pinched or crushed. In this case, there is no remedy except to cauterize near the affected area, and quickly apply a bandage.
Then, once the limb is set, apply the poultice we have described, and let it remain compressed for five days; afterwards, unbind it and let the patient's hand be exercised.
But if movement is restricted and some swelling occurs, then soothe it with warm water and massage several times until it is relieved.
Chapter 29.
On the treatment of finger dislocations.
Sometimes fingers are dislocated in every direction/part.
And when a finger is dislocated towards the back of the palm or the belly of the hand, then extend the finger and reduce the dislocation with your hands until it returns to its place.
Then support the head of the finger, and suspend it downwards from the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it like that for two days.
Then suspend it and extend it until it is straightened on that same day.
And when night comes, bind it according to that method, and do not cease to unbind it during the day and exercise it with movement, and bind it at night; and do this for some days until it is tight/strong.
And do similarly if it is dislocated on the palm of the hand, and bind it to that part and perform your first operation until it is healed.
And similarly do this when it is dislocated in other parts.
Chapter 30.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When a complete dislocation happens to any of the vertebrae of the back or neck, and many vertebrae are dislodged from their places, then in that case there is no cure, for death threatens the patient.
And a sign of this is that the patient's excrement is expelled involuntarily, and they cannot retain it; and many times some of their limbs are weakened, or both legs, or both arms, or one of these; or one vertebra is dislodged from its place, because many times it is dislodged and many times the displacement of these happens in four parts.
Therefore, that is already dislodged to the rear parts and is called yellowness (jaundice, or possibly referring to a discoloration).
But the cure for him is that then you consider if the yellowness (jaundice) comes from purulence (pus), because then in that case there is no cure, nor is there any healing at all.
But in that which comes from a fall or from a blow or similar to those, the Ancients already described the methods of cure with extensive discourse, of which the most returns with profit; but I have already abbreviated that, whose brevity excuses the excess of it, because of my proximity to my intention in my exposition, with which I have exposed and formulated this from various instruments of that with which I will show this and expound that.
And of those, only that which occurs in the two (presumably referring to specific body parts mentioned earlier) properly is cured with what I recounted.
And it is that the patient should be extended on his face on a suitable, flat surface near a wall, and a soft underlayment should be placed under him so that his feet are not pressed.
And then a straight pole should be placed, fixed in the earth at the head end of the bed near the patient's head, and another pole at the foot end of the bed near his feet.
And the attendant should hold the pole, and it should not be fixed in a pit; and another attendant should hold the other in the same manner.
Then wrap a strong linen bandage around the patient's head and below the ears; and extend the end of the bandage to the pole which is by his head, and tie it there; and then tighten another bandage around his hips, thighs, and legs; and then fasten all the bandages and tie them to the other pole which is by his feet.
Then each attendant should extend the bandages to the poles, and the two poles and the bandages should not be removed from their places where they are fixed, but nevertheless, they should not be fixed as we have said [previously - implying a level of tension and adjustability].
And the physician should place his palms on the spine with firmness until it returns [to its proper position], or place a board on it, and then adjust his feet on the board until it returns [to its proper position].
But if it does not return by this method, then take a board whose width or length is about three braccia, and make a hole in the wall which we have said is near the patient, into which the end of the board fits. Then place half of the board on the spine, and the physician place his feet on the other end, and press firmly until the spine is compressed and returns to its place.
And if you wish, use a lathe which is turned by hand; and you should place in the ground near the patient's head, at the back of the neck, two poles, the length of each being one arm's length, and similarly two poles near the patient's head, at the back of the neck; and the distance between each pole should be one palm, and a hole should be made in each pole through which the lathe passes, and each should be firmly fixed in the ground so that it is not moved.
And there should be a round pole on which the lathe is mounted, and it should be rotated in the two holes of the two poles.
And at its end is a hole in which the pole is fitted, the length of which is one palm, together with that which is rotated; and the other two poles are similar to that one.
Then tighten the bandages that you have tied around the patient's feet at the hole which is near their head, and the bandages that you have tied around their thighs at the lathe which is near their feet.
And then near each lathe there should be a minister who turns the lathe with his hand, and the physician manipulates the vertebra as we have said.
Then, once the vertebra is returned and the area is manipulated, you should apply a drying plaster with egg white, and then lint; and then place over the plaster a piece of linen cloth whose width is three fingers or thereabouts, and whose length is sufficient to cover the area of the swelling, and the part of the healthy vertebra.
Then it should be bound with the bandage that we have mentioned, and the patient should be given light nourishment until they are healed.
And if any swelling remains in the area at the end of healing, then medicines should be administered that soften and smooth, with the application of the linen cloth for a long time, which we have recommended; and a lead plate is applied to that area.
And sometimes swelling occurs in the back of the spinal vertebrae, and it is caused by that dislocation; and that is a bone that already swells, so that no attempt should be made to treat it with this medicine, for it will quickly cause death.
Chapter 31.
On the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the muscle joint suffer only dislocation, and that does not happen to them which happens to other joints of small movement and concavity.
And the hip joint is dislocated in four ways, for it is dislocated inwards, outwards, forwards, or backwards.
And it is mostly dislocated inwards, and rarely forwards or backwards.
And a sign of its inward dislocation is that, when you align the patient's healthy leg with the affected one, the affected leg is longer and the knee more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend their foot without pain, and it is in that place that the pain is often inflamed with a hot abscess, because the head of the femur has already reached there.
And the sign of that to which dislocation happens outwards, is that its symptoms are contrary to these symptoms.
And the sign of that to which dislocation happens forwards is that he extends his leg completely, but nevertheless cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and if he forces himself to walk he cannot do so on the front part; and his urine is retained and his groin is inflamed, and he walks on his heel.
And the sign of that to which dislocation happens backwards is that he cannot extend the knee, nor can he bend it before bending the hip, and his leg is shorter than the other, and the groin is softened, and the head of the femur is in that place.
But reduction of the types of this dislocation is that you consider, then, if the dislocation is old and longstanding, that it is already set with having that nearby you administer its reduction and it remains according to its disposition, then it is not in that cure of everything; and therefore it is not fitting that in that case a presumption should be made.
But in the case of one whose dislocation is recent, and is one of the four types of dislocation, then attend to this that you return the joint and extend it inwards and outwards, and move it to the right and left, for it quickly returns and does not need other treatment.
But, if it does not return in this manner, then it is necessary that you prepare a strong assistant and extend his leg from the lower part, either with his hands, or with a bandage which is tied above the knee; and another assistant extend the hand of that one from above so that he places his hands below the testicles; and then tighten with a strong bandage on the head of the femur, and let another assistant hold the end of the bandage.
And let the extension be from him or from the inside part to the groin towards the part of the fork (presumably the pubic region), or from the back part to the part of the back.
And let the extension of all those be at once until the patient is lifted with his body from the earth and remains suspended.
And this type of extension is common to all four (types of dislocation).
And therefore, if the dislocation returns with what we have said, it will be good; and if not, then it is necessary for every type that which we shall say of its proper cure.
In the reduction of the hip itself, when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is in the inward parts, it is necessary that the patient lie supine on his sound side; and then place the ligatures on the root of the thigh in that place which is between the head of the thigh and the place which is below the groin; and then extend the ligature upward from the part of the groin to the highest part of the fork (pubic region).
And then another strong assistant grasps his arms, and presses the thick part of the affected thigh and extends it outwards with a strong extension, because then it returns to its place.
And this type is of easier cure, with which this limb is reduced, than the other types.
But if it is difficult for you and you do not succeed with this type of cure at all, then it is necessary that the patient's two feet be tied together with a strong, light ligature, above both ankles and both heels.
And let the length of each part be the quantity of four digits, and let the affected leg be extended more than the other by the quantity of two digits.
And then the patient should be suspended from the head of the beam, and let him be in the house; and let his height from the ground be the quantity of two arms' lengths.
And then command a strong young man to reduce the head of the thigh, and let another young man support the patient, and the other young man, reducing the thigh with strong pushes, because the joint returns to its place quickly.
But in the proper reduction, when it is a dislocation to the outside, it is necessary that the patient lie on his back as we have recommended in the case of the yellow fever; and let the ligature be tightened around the affected leg properly and around his feet.
And then two beams should be placed, one at his feet, and the other at his head; and then let adjusted beams be placed in the middle of the elevation on which wet cloths are wrapped, so that the patient is not harmed because the beam is between his thighs, so that during the extension to the lower parts it is not pulled.
And then each attendant should extend on his side, and the physician with his hand guides the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the front parts, then it is necessary that the leg be extended slightly, and it is according to this same narrative on an elevation; and the physician places the palm of his right hand on the dislocated joint of the patient, and then grasps it with the other hand, and with that grasp places that [leg] extended to the lower parts at the part of the knee.
And if the dislocation is in the back parts, then it is not necessary that the patient be extended to the lower parts, and it is elevated above the earth; rather it is necessary that he be placed on a hard surface, just as it is necessary for the elevation if his hip is dislocated to the outside parts, according to what we have said of his lying on two hips; and it is on his face and the ligaments are extended, as we have said, there is hardly any [harm].
And it is necessary that compression be administered with a cloth on the place where the joint is.
Therefore, when the reduction is completed as it should be — and the sign of his reduction is not hidden from you, and it is that you extend the patient's two legs, and when you see those equal and the patient flexes his leg and extends it without difficulty, then know that the limb is returned as it should be — and then you adjust the two thighs and apply a plaster and bind it tightly so that the hip is not moved in any of the parts; and let the patient rest for three or four days.
And then untie the bandage and the plaster, and compare one leg with the other.
Then, if you see them equal in appearance, then know that the dislocation is now healed, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any cause for softening (or loosening), then you return and plaster that, and bind it according to your first binding, and leave it for three days.
And then untie it, and have him delay ambulation (walking) for some days, until it is strengthened.
Chapter 32.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways, for it is dislocated to the outside parts, and to the inside parts, and to the lower parts, more to the back parts; and to the front parts it is not dislocated.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you command the patient to apply his leg to his thigh; then you will know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all its types is that you make the patient lie down straight, so that he extends his leg, if he can do so; adhere your two hands to his face, and place his foot between your feet; and then make the patient's knee adhere to your palms, and hold those between your fingers over his knee with strength, and another attendant extend his foot until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its reduction is that his leg adheres to the thigh with light, painless ease.
And then plaster that and make the leg adhere to the thigh, and then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days, and then unbind them.
And do not allow the patient to walk for some days, until he is healed and strengthened.
But if the reduction is difficult for you, then administer strong extension with bandages, the use of which in the treatment of the hip was described previously.
Chapter 33.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the talus. (Talus refers to the ankle bone)
The talus is sometimes dislocated by a slight movement, and sometimes completely dislocated; and its dislocation is either inwards or outwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that sometimes the talus swells, extending towards the side to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment of its slight displacement is gentle reduction, and that is to extend it easily with the hands and to manipulate it until it returns to its place.
But in the treatment of it when it is completely dislocated, it is necessary that you make the patient lie straight, and that a strong and capable assistant hold him from behind, at the level of his back.
And then you, with your right hand, grasp the foot from the upper part, and with your left hand, from the lower part of the foot at the ankle.
And then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and then with the left hand, twice, and then extend with the left; and push the bones of the foot with the right towards the leg without effort.
And do this twice, as we have described.
And then push the bones of the foot to the leg a third time, and you are at the ankle.
Then it returns in one or two attempts according to this method, and if you see the foot set correctly, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation on it, for it will return.
But if the reduction by the method we have described is forbidden to you, then make the patient lie on his back on the ground, and tie a strong rope firmly to the ground, so that it lies between his thighs, and you have already wrapped a cloth around him so that it does not hurt the patient.
And then let his attendant hold his thighs, and then another attendant extend the foot with his hands or with a bandage that he ties around the ankle; and then let each attendant extend against the extension of his opposite number, and the rope should be between the patient's thighs, holding it, so that his body is not pulled down from his extension.
And then the doctor sets the dislocation with his hands, and another attendant holds the healthy thigh downwards, because the dislocation returns easily.
And so when the dislocation is reduced and the success of the reduction is clear to you, then apply a plaster and bind it tightly with bandages, and bind the foot to the leg with bandages.
And it is necessary that you fear or guard against the nerves which are behind the heel, so that the bandage on it is not tight and does not strongly injure them.
And then leave it for two or three days; and if the bandage is loosened, tighten it.
And then unbind it on the third or fourth day, and forbid the patient from walking for forty days. But if he is forced to walk before that time, there is no certainty that the dislocation will not be loosened and worsened, and then it will not receive a cure.
And if a hot abscess happens to him, then it is necessary that you administer to its suppuration that which precedes our narration, in another place of treatment and application, until it is healed.
Chapter 34.
On the treatment of dislocations of the toes.
It is necessary that the one who is dislocated be bandaged with slight extension without effort; and this is not difficult, rather it is easy.
But if the dislocation is in some of the bones of the instep, then it is necessary that you make the patient place his foot on the ground or on a table, and remain like that as if he were walking.
And then you stand, and place your foot on what protrudes from the joints, and then press that with your foot with force until it returns, and you see that it is already set and no prominence appears in the place.
And then place under the sole of his foot a board that covers the whole foot, to which are attached two ends, and then bind it with a decent and firm bandage for three days; and then untie it and make him rest from walking for many days, until it is strengthened and its restoration is assured.
Chapter 35.
Of the types of dislocation that are made with a wound or with a fracture or with both together.
When any of these causes occurs, and you engage in its cure and restoration, death often ensues.
And therefore it is not fitting that one should presume to treat it, except one who is wise in the art, of long experience, merciful and compassionate, slow, not rash or presumptuous; and who uses at the beginning only such medicines as reduce hot abscesses, and leaves the patient to divine judgment, except for him in whom you see hope of recovery, with alleviation of the disease, and in whom some hope appears. And then strive immediately to reduce it at the beginning of the cause, before a hot abscess develops.
And then if the limb returns as we wish, then administer a regime which reduces hot abscesses and treat the wound with what is suitable, of drying ointments.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and the jagged ends of the bone are separated, then you must bind them firmly together, and do in this as we have said in simple diseases, which has been preceded in those places.
And guard and avoid, according to your ability, that entry into paths of fear as my preaching has preceded for you; for that is better for you before your Creator, and more permanent for your glory, and more wholesome for your true body in your time. Thus end the words of Albucasis perfectly, for which may God be blessed. Amen.
